Three Evidences for an Independent Kalām Logic
Lists three pieces of evidence proving mutakallimun used a distinct logical method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three pieces of evidence that the mutakallimun used a distinct logical methodology and explain how each supports the claim.

Core Idea
The mutakallimun's method was distinct from Aristotelian logic, as shown by their critics and supporters.

This passage presents three key pieces of evidence demonstrating that early Muslim theologians (mutakallimun) developed their own approach to argumentation, separate from Aristotelian logic. By examining these points, you will see how the Islamic logical tradition was independent and often clashed with Hellenistic thought. Each evidence reveals a different facet of the theological method.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overarching claim
The passage aims to prove that mutakallimun used a methodology for research other than Aristotelian logic. Recognize this as the central thesis.
2
Extract the first evidence: al-Ghazali's critique
Al-Ghazali attacks the mutakallimun for relying on 'famous premises' (muqaddimat mashhura) that they conceded from opponents only due to fame, not rational law. He also mentions the 'theory of the intermediary' challenging non-contradiction.
The first evidence is a primary source showing internal criticism from a leading scholar.
3
Extract the second evidence: Imam al-Haramayn's statement
Imam al-Haramayn (Juwayni) says that the imams arranged intellectual proofs in a certain order, which he would then show to be corrupt. This implies a distinct, shared method among theologians.
This evidence highlights that theologians had their own arrangement of proofs.
4
Extract the third evidence: Hellenistic contempt
Representatives of Hellenistic thought, like Yahya ibn 'Adi, looked down upon the mutakallimun for violating Aristotelian logic, suggesting the theologians' approach was indeed different.
External criticism from adherents of Aristotelian logic confirms the distinctness.
5
Synthesize how the three evidences support independence
Together, the critiques from within (al-Ghazali), a methodological statement from a leading theologian (Juwayni), and disdain from outside Hellenistic thinkers build a strong case that mutakallimun had their own logical method.
The synthesis shows that the evidence is cumulative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويثبت أن المتكلمين استخدموا منهاجاً للبحث غير المنطق الأرسططاليسي ما يأتي : أ - أن الغزالي (٥٠٥ هـ - ١١١١ م) يهاجم هذا المنهاج الذي يخرج عن منطق أرسطو في أخذ المتكلمين بمقدمات مشهورة تسلموها من خصومهم واضطرهم إلى تسليمها لا القانون العقلي . . . ولعل الغزالي يشير إلى تلك النظرية التي كانت تتردد في أوساط المسلمين « نظرية الواسطة » والتي تنقد مبدأ عدم ارتفاع النقيضين أي مبدأ الثالث المرفوع . وفي فقرة أخرى إن « أكثر أقيسة الجدليين من المتكلمين والفقهاء في مجادلاتهم وتصانيفهم مؤلفة من مقدمات مشهورة فيما بينهم سلموها لمجرد الشهرة وذهلوا عن سببها . ولذا نرى أقيستهم تنتج نتائج متناقضة فيتحيرون فيها وتتخبط عقولهم في تنقيحها » . ب ـ ما يقوله إمام الحرمين « رتب أئمتنا أدلة العقول ترتيباً ننقله ثم نبين فساده » . جـ ـ احتقار ممثلي الفكر الهليني في العالم الإسلامي ـ شراحاً كانوا أو مترجمين ـ للمتكلمين لمخالفة هؤلاء المنطق الأرسططاليسي . يذكر القفطي (٦٤٦) أن يحيى بن عدي (٣٦٥ هـ - ٩٧٥ م) حضر مجلس بعض الوزراء
And it is proven that the mutakallimun used a methodology for research other than Aristotelian logic by the following: (a) al-Ghazali (505 AH - 1111 CE) attacks this methodology which departs from Aristotle's logic in that the mutakallimun accept famous premises (muqaddimat mashhura) that they conceded from their opponents and compelled them to accept, not the rational law... Perhaps al-Ghazali refers to that theory which was circulating among Muslims, 'the theory of intermediary' (nazariyyat al-wasita), which criticizes the principle of non-contradiction, i.e., the principle of the excluded middle. In another paragraph: 'Most of the syllogisms of the dialecticians (jadaliyyin) among the mutakallimun and fuqaha in their debates and writings are composed of premises famous among them, which they accepted merely due to fame and forgot their cause. Hence we see their syllogisms produce contradictory results, and they become confused and their minds stumble in refining them.' (b) What Imam al-Haramayn says: 'Our imams arranged the proofs of the intellects in an arrangement that we quote and then show its invalidity.' (c) The contempt of representatives of Hellenistic thought in the Islamic world—whether commentators or translators—for the mutakallimun due to the latter's opposition to Aristotelian logic. Al-Qifti (646 AH) mentions that Yahya ibn 'Adi (365 AH - 975 CE) attended the council of some vizier...
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage presents three pieces of evidence that the mutakallimun had a distinct research methodology separate from Aristotelian logic. First, al-Ghazali criticizes their method of relying on 'famous premises' (mashhurat) rather than on self-evident axioms. He also hints at the 'theory of the intermediary' which challenged the principle of non-contradiction (the excluded middle). Al-Ghazali further notes that their syllogisms often yield contradictory results due to reliance on unexamined premises. Second, Imam al-Haramayn (Juwayni) states that the imams (of kalām) arranged intellectual proofs in a particular order, but that he would then show their corruption, implying a shared methodology. Third, Hellenistic thinkers like Yahya ibn 'Adi looked down upon the mutakallimun for violating Aristotelian logic, suggesting the theologians' approach was indeed different. These evidences together support the claim of an independent Islamic logical tradition.

Key Terms
مقدمات مشهورة
نظرية الواسطة
الجدليين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did al-Ghazali criticize about the syllogisms of the mutakallimun?
A) They used too many mathematical examples
B) They relied on famous premises that led to contradictions
C) Not quoting the Quran
D) Ignoring the principles of grammar

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun employed a distinct logical methodology, relying on famous premises and dialectical reasoning, as criticized by al-Ghazali and acknowledged by their opponents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find an example of a famous premise (muqaddima mashhura) used in a theological debate between two mutakallimun. Explain why it is considered famous rather than self-evident.
?
Compare the method of Imam al-Haramayn as described in the passage with the method of Aristotle. How does al-Haramayn's approach differ from Aristotelian demonstration?

The Conditional Refutation of Dawaran: Six Counterexamples
Opponents' conditional argument and six counterexamples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the opponents' modus tollens argument against dawaran and evaluate the strength of the six counterexamples.

Core Idea
Concomitance without causation is possible; six counterexamples demonstrate this.

In the debate over whether dawaran (concomitance) can prove causality (illah), the opponents begin with a powerful logical argument: if dawaran were sufficient, then every concomitant would be a cause, which is false. They support this with six concrete examples from theology, ontology, and logic.

Guided Path
1
Locate the conditional premise and falsification
Find the sentence starting with 'لو كان الدوران دليل العلة' and identify the antecedent (dawaran is proof) and consequent (everything concomitant is cause).
2
Recognize the modus tollens form
The opponents argue: If P then Q. Not Q. Therefore not P. They disprove Q by counterexamples.
3
Examine the six counterexamples
List each of the six examples: cause-effect, substance-accident, divine essence-attributes, correlatives, six directions, definiens-definiendum. Explain why they are concomitant but not causal.
4
Assess the force of the objection
Why do these examples challenge dawaran? How must the proponent respond? This sets up further debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٤ ـ القسم الرابع : وهم الذين يرون أن الدوران لا يدل على العلية واستندوا في هذا إلى ما يأتي : أولاً ـ « أن الدوران لو كان دليل العلة ، لكان كل مدار علة للدائر معه ولكن التالي باطل فالمقدم مثله » وإثبات بطلان الدوران أن هناك حالات يتحقق فيها الدوران ولم تتحقق فيها العلية وأوضحها هي . ١ ـ أن العلة نفسها تدور مع المعلول نفسه وجوداً وعدماً ، مع أن المعلول ليس بعلة لعلة قطعاً . ٢ ـ الجوهر والعرض متلازمان نفياً وإثباتاً ، مع أن أحدهما ليس علة في وجود الآخر . ٣ ـ ذات الله وصفاته متلازمان . وكل صفة من صفاته تلازم سائر الصفات ولكن لا علية بينهما . ٤ ـ المتضايفان كالأبوة والنبوة متلازمان وجوداً وعدماً ، مع أن أحدهما ليس علة في وجود الآخر . ٥ - الجهات الست لا تنفك واحدة منهما عن الأخرى بينما لا يتحقق بينهما علية . ٦ - المحد والمحدود(١) . ثانياً - الدوران يتكون من أمرين . الإطراد والإنعكاس - والإطراد ليس دليلاً على علية الوصف . والإنعكاس غير مأخوذ به في العلل الشرعية . وإذا كان كل واحد منهما ليس دليلاً على العلية ، فمجموعهما ليس كذلك(٢) . ويجيب أصحاب الدوران بأنه ليس من اللازم أن يكون كل واحد منهما ليس بدليل علية العلية ، وأن يكون مجموعهما كذلك . فانه يثبت للمجموع ما لا يثبت للأفراد . ثالثاً - أن الوصف المدار يجوز أن يكون وصفاً ملازماً للعلة ، وليس هو العلة ، وذلك كالرائحة المخصوصة الملازمة للاسكار ، ولا سبيل إلى ذلك إلا بالتعرض لانتقاء وصف غيره بواسطة السبر . ويعني هذا الانتقال من طريقة الدوران إلى طريقة السير(٣) . وهذا المسلك هو قانون التلازم في الوقوع وفي التخلف عند مل Joint method of Agreement and Difference وهو يستند إلى أن العلة إذا حضرت ، حضر معلولها ، وإذا غابت ، غاب . فاذا ما بحثنا حالتين تظهر في كل منهما ظاهرة خاصة ، فوجدنا أنهما تختلفان في كل شيء عدا أمراً واحداً فقط وحالتين أخريتين لا تظهر فيهما الظاهرة فوجدنا أنهما لا تتفقان في شيء عدا تغيب ذلك الأمر - فاننا نستنتج أن ذلك الأمر الموجود في المثالين الأوليين ، المتغيب في المثالين الآخرين هو علة الظاهرة(٤) .
4 - The fourth section: They are those who hold that concomitance does not indicate causality. They rely on the following: First: 'If concomitance were proof of the cause, then everything that accompanies something would be the cause of that which accompanies it, but the consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise.' The proof that concomitance is false is that there are cases where concomitance occurs without causality. The clearest are: 1) The cause itself accompanies its effect in existence and non-existence, while the effect is definitely not the cause of the cause. 2) Substance and accident are mutually concomitant in negation and affirmation, while neither is the cause of the other's existence. 3) God's essence and attributes are mutually concomitant. Each attribute of His is concomitant with the other attributes, but there is no causality between them. 4) Correlatives like fatherhood and prophethood are mutually concomitant in existence and non-existence, while neither is the cause of the other's existence. 5) The six directions are inseparable from one another, while causality does not obtain between them. 6) The definiens and the definiendum. Second: Concomitance consists of two things: co-presence and co-absence. Co-presence is not proof of the causality of the description, and co-absence is not considered in religious causes. If each of them is not a proof of causality, then their combination is likewise not. The proponents of concomitance respond: It is not necessary that if each individually is not a proof of causality, then their combination is likewise not. For the combination has a property that individuals do not have. Third: The concomitant description might be a description that is invariably associated with the cause, without being the cause itself, like the specific aroma that accompanies intoxication. There is no way to determine this except by testing for the absence of another description via investigation (sabr). This means moving from the method of concomitance to the method of sifting. This path is the law of concomitant variation in occurrence and in absence, according to Mill's Joint method of Agreement and Difference. It is based on: when the cause is present, its effect is present; when it is absent, it is absent. If we examine two cases in each of which a particular phenomenon appears, and we find that they differ in everything except one single matter, and two other cases in which the phenomenon does not appear, and we find that they agree in nothing except the absence of that matter – then we conclude that that matter which is present in the first two examples and absent in the other two is the cause of the phenomenon.(4)
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth position in the debate on dawaran (concomitance) as evidence for causality (illah). The opponents of dawaran as proof give three main arguments. The first argument is logical: if dawaran were evidence, then any concomitant would be a cause, but that leads to absurdity. They list six examples where concomitance is present but causality is not: 1) a cause and its effect are concomitant but the effect is not the cause of the cause; 2) substance and accident are concomitant but one is not the cause of the other; 3) God's essence and attributes are concomitant but attributes are not causes of each other; 4) correlatives like fatherhood and prophethood are concomitant but not causes of each other; 5) the six directions are inseparable but not causally related; 6) the definiens and definiendum are reciprocal but not causes. The second argument is that dawaran comprises two components: tard (co-presence) and 'aks (co-absence). The opponents claim that tard is not a proof of causality, and 'aks is not even accepted in religious causes (shar'i 'illal). Therefore, the combination also cannot be a proof. The third argument is that a concomitant description might be merely a constant companion (lazim) of the true cause, not the cause itself, as the specific smell accompanies intoxication but is not the cause of intoxication. To distinguish this, one must use sabr (investigating other descriptions) – moving from dawaran to a more refined method. The author then explains this refined method as the 'law of concomitant variation' (qanun al-talazum), essentially Mill's Joint Method of Agreement and Difference. It requires comparing two cases where the phenomenon occurs and one case where it does not, isolating the one factor that is present in the positives and absent in the negatives, thereby identifying the cause. This passage is a significant engagement with both Islamic usul methodology and Western logic (through Mill).

Key Terms
الدوران
الإطراد
الإنعكاس
السبر
قانون التلازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the six counterexamples given by the opponents of dawaran?
A) Cause and effect
B) Fire and heat
C) God's essence and attributes
D) The definiens and the definiendum

Key Takeaway
The opponents refute dawaran by a modus tollens: if dawaran were proof, then all accompaniments would be causes; but the six counterexamples show this is false, so dawaran cannot be a proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a similar modus tollens argument against using a specific indicator in usul al-fiqh (e.g., 'if istishab were proof of continued ruling, then every state would persist indefinitely, which is false because evidence can change'). Then list two counterexamples of things that are concomitant but not causally related from Islamic theology (e.g., the attributes of Allah).

The Argument from Components: Tard and 'Aks
Second argument: dawaran comprises tard and 'aks; each insufficient, so the whole is insufficient.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the second objection that since tard and 'aks individually are not proofs, their combination cannot be, and the proponents' response about emergent properties.

Core Idea
The whole can have a property that its parts lack; the combination of co-presence and co-absence may constitute a valid proof.

The opponents refine their critique: dawaran is composed of tard (co-presence) and 'aks (co-absence). They argue that tard alone is not a proof of causality, and 'aks is not even accepted in religious causes. Therefore, the combination cannot be a proof either. The proponents of dawaran counter with a principle of composition: the combination may have a property the parts do not.

Guided Path
1
Break down dawaran into its components
Identify tard (ittirād) as co-presence and 'aks (in'ikās) as co-absence. Note their definitions.
2
Understand the opponents' composition argument
They claim: if tard is not a proof and 'aks is not a proof, then dawaran (the sum) is also not a proof. This is a fallacy of composition.
3
Recognize the proponents' emergent property response
The proponents argue that the combination may have probative value that neither part has individually.
4
Evaluate the dialectic
Which side appears stronger? Why might the opponents still be unsatisfied? (Leads to the third argument.)

Source Text

٤ ـ القسم الرابع : وهم الذين يرون أن الدوران لا يدل على العلية واستندوا في هذا إلى ما يأتي : أولاً ـ « أن الدوران لو كان دليل العلة ، لكان كل مدار علة للدائر معه ولكن التالي باطل فالمقدم مثله » وإثبات بطلان الدوران أن هناك حالات يتحقق فيها الدوران ولم تتحقق فيها العلية وأوضحها هي . ١ ـ أن العلة نفسها تدور مع المعلول نفسه وجوداً وعدماً ، مع أن المعلول ليس بعلة لعلة قطعاً . ٢ ـ الجوهر والعرض متلازمان نفياً وإثباتاً ، مع أن أحدهما ليس علة في وجود الآخر . ٣ ـ ذات الله وصفاته متلازمان . وكل صفة من صفاته تلازم سائر الصفات ولكن لا علية بينهما . ٤ ـ المتضايفان كالأبوة والنبوة متلازمان وجوداً وعدماً ، مع أن أحدهما ليس علة في وجود الآخر . ٥ - الجهات الست لا تنفك واحدة منهما عن الأخرى بينما لا يتحقق بينهما علية . ٦ - المحد والمحدود(١) . ثانياً - الدوران يتكون من أمرين . الإطراد والإنعكاس - والإطراد ليس دليلاً على علية الوصف . والإنعكاس غير مأخوذ به في العلل الشرعية . وإذا كان كل واحد منهما ليس دليلاً على العلية ، فمجموعهما ليس كذلك(٢) . ويجيب أصحاب الدوران بأنه ليس من اللازم أن يكون كل واحد منهما ليس بدليل علية العلية ، وأن يكون مجموعهما كذلك . فانه يثبت للمجموع ما لا يثبت للأفراد . ثالثاً - أن الوصف المدار يجوز أن يكون وصفاً ملازماً للعلة ، وليس هو العلة ، وذلك كالرائحة المخصوصة الملازمة للاسكار ، ولا سبيل إلى ذلك إلا بالتعرض لانتقاء وصف غيره بواسطة السبر . ويعني هذا الانتقال من طريقة الدوران إلى طريقة السير(٣) . وهذا المسلك هو قانون التلازم في الوقوع وفي التخلف عند مل Joint method of Agreement and Difference وهو يستند إلى أن العلة إذا حضرت ، حضر معلولها ، وإذا غابت ، غاب . فاذا ما بحثنا حالتين تظهر في كل منهما ظاهرة خاصة ، فوجدنا أنهما تختلفان في كل شيء عدا أمراً واحداً فقط وحالتين أخريتين لا تظهر فيهما الظاهرة فوجدنا أنهما لا تتفقان في شيء عدا تغيب ذلك الأمر - فاننا نستنتج أن ذلك الأمر الموجود في المثالين الأوليين ، المتغيب في المثالين الآخرين هو علة الظاهرة(٤) .
4 - The fourth section: They are those who hold that concomitance does not indicate causality. They rely on the following: First: 'If concomitance were proof of the cause, then everything that accompanies something would be the cause of that which accompanies it, but the consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise.' The proof that concomitance is false is that there are cases where concomitance occurs without causality. The clearest are: 1) The cause itself accompanies its effect in existence and non-existence, while the effect is definitely not the cause of the cause. 2) Substance and accident are mutually concomitant in negation and affirmation, while neither is the cause of the other's existence. 3) God's essence and attributes are mutually concomitant. Each attribute of His is concomitant with the other attributes, but there is no causality between them. 4) Correlatives like fatherhood and prophethood are mutually concomitant in existence and non-existence, while neither is the cause of the other's existence. 5) The six directions are inseparable from one another, while causality does not obtain between them. 6) The definiens and the definiendum. Second: Concomitance consists of two things: co-presence and co-absence. Co-presence is not proof of the causality of the description, and co-absence is not considered in religious causes. If each of them is not a proof of causality, then their combination is likewise not. The proponents of concomitance respond: It is not necessary that if each individually is not a proof of causality, then their combination is likewise not. For the combination has a property that individuals do not have. This path is the law of concomitant variation in occurrence and in absence, according to Mill's Joint method of Agreement and Difference. It is based on: when the cause is present, its effect is present; when it is absent, it is absent. Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth position in the debate on dawaran (concomitance) as evidence for causality (illah). The opponents of dawaran as proof give three main arguments. The first argument is logical: if dawaran were evidence, then any concomitant would be a cause, but that leads to absurdity. They list six examples where concomitance is present but causality is not: 1) a cause and its effect are concomitant but the effect is not the cause of the cause; 2) substance and accident are concomitant but one is not the cause of the other; 3) God's essence and attributes are concomitant but attributes are not causes of each other; 4) correlatives like fatherhood and prophethood are concomitant but not causes of each other; 5) the six directions are inseparable but not causally related; 6) the definiens and definiendum are reciprocal but not causes. The second argument is that dawaran comprises two components: tard (co-presence) and 'aks (co-absence). The opponents claim that tard is not a proof of causality, and 'aks is not even accepted in religious causes (shar'i 'illal). Therefore, the combination also cannot be a proof. The third argument is that a concomitant description might be merely a constant companion (lazim) of the true cause, not the cause itself, as the specific smell accompanies intoxication but is not the cause of intoxication. To distinguish this, one must use sabr (investigating other descriptions) – moving from dawaran to a more refined method. The author then explains this refined method as the 'law of concomitant variation' (qanun al-talazum), essentially Mill's Joint Method of Agreement and Difference. It requires comparing two cases where the phenomenon occurs and one case where it does not, isolating the one factor that is present in the positives and absent in the negatives, thereby identifying the cause. This passage is a significant engagement with both Islamic usul methodology and Western logic (through Mill).

Checkpoint
What is the opponents' claim about the components of dawaran?
A) Tard is sufficient but 'aks is not
B) Both tard and 'aks are sufficient individually
C) Neither tard nor 'aks is a proof, so the combination cannot be a proof
D) The combination is a proof because the parts are proofs

Key Takeaway
The opponents commit a fallacy of composition by arguing that since the parts of dawaran are insufficient, the whole must be insufficient; the proponents counter with the principle of emergent properties.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find an example in usul al-fiqh where a combination of two indicators (e.g., a hadith and a consensus) yields a ruling that neither could produce alone. Write a brief argument showing how the opponents' composition fallacy would apply.

The Third Argument and the Refined Method: Sabr and Joint Variation
Third objection: lazim vs. cause; solution through sabr and the joint method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the third objection that a concomitant may be a mere accompaniment (lazim), and describe how the joint method of agreement and difference (law of concomitant variation) addresses this.

Core Idea
The joint method of concomitant variation, combining co-presence and co-absence, provides a more rigorous way to isolate the true cause.

The opponents' third argument is that the concomitant description might be a necessary accompaniment (lazim) of the true cause, not the cause itself. They give the example of a specific aroma accompanying intoxication without being the cause. To overcome this, one must use sabr (investigation) and the law of concomitant variation, which systematically compares cases of presence and absence to isolate the cause.

Guided Path
1
Understand the lazim objection
The opponent says: what if the description that is concomitant with the effect is merely a constant companion of the true cause? Example: the smell of wine is concomitant with intoxication but does not cause it.
2
See the need for sabr (investigation)
To distinguish the true cause from a mere accompaniment, one must test other descriptions (al-sabr) to see which ones are truly causal. This involves eliminating alternatives.
3
Introduce the law of concomitant variation
The passage describes: compare two cases where the phenomenon appears (they differ except one common factor) and two cases where it does not appear (they agree only in absence of that factor). The factor present in positives and absent in negatives is the cause.
4
Recognize this as Mill's Joint Method
This method is logically superior to simple dawaran because it uses both positive and negative instances and controls for alternatives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٤ ـ القسم الرابع : وهم الذين يرون أن الدوران لا يدل على العلية واستندوا في هذا إلى ما يأتي : أولاً ـ « أن الدوران لو كان دليل العلة ، لكان كل مدار علة للدائر معه ولكن التالي باطل فالمقدم مثله » وإثبات بطلان الدوران أن هناك حالات يتحقق فيها الدوران ولم تتحقق فيها العلية وأوضحها هي . ١ ـ أن العلة نفسها تدور مع المعلول نفسه وجوداً وعدماً ، مع أن المعلول ليس بعلة لعلة قطعاً . ٢ ـ الجوهر والعرض متلازمان نفياً وإثباتاً ، مع أن أحدهما ليس علة في وجود الآخر . ٣ ـ ذات الله وصفاته متلازمان . وكل صفة من صفاته تلازم سائر الصفات ولكن لا علية بينهما . ٤ ـ المتضايفان كالأبوة والنبوة متلازمان وجوداً وعدماً ، مع أن أحدهما ليس علة في وجود الآخر . ٥ - الجهات الست لا تنفك واحدة منهما عن الأخرى بينما لا يتحقق بينهما علية . ٦ - المحد والمحدود(١) . ثانياً - الدوران يتكون من أمرين . الإطراد والإنعكاس - والإطراد ليس دليلاً على علية الوصف . والإنعكاس غير مأخوذ به في العلل الشرعية . وإذا كان كل واحد منهما ليس دليلاً على العلية ، فمجموعهما ليس كذلك(٢) . ويجيب أصحاب الدوران بأنه ليس من اللازم أن يكون كل واحد منهما ليس بدليل علية العلية ، وأن يكون مجموعهما كذلك . فانه يثبت للمجموع ما لا يثبت للأفراد . ثالثاً - أن الوصف المدار يجوز أن يكون وصفاً ملازماً للعلة ، وليس هو العلة ، وذلك كالرائحة المخصوصة الملازمة للاسكار ، ولا سبيل إلى ذلك إلا بالتعرض لانتقاء وصف غيره بواسطة السبر . ويعني هذا الانتقال من طريقة الدوران إلى طريقة السير(٣) . وهذا المسلك هو قانون التلازم في الوقوع وفي التخلف عند مل Joint method of Agreement and Difference وهو يستند إلى أن العلة إذا حضرت ، حضر معلولها ، وإذا غابت ، غاب . فاذا ما بحثنا حالتين تظهر في كل منهما ظاهرة خاصة ، فوجدنا أنهما تختلفان في كل شيء عدا أمراً واحداً فقط وحالتين أخريتين لا تظهر فيهما الظاهرة فوجدنا أنهما لا تتفقان في شيء عدا تغيب ذلك الأمر - فاننا نستنتج أن ذلك الأمر الموجود في المثالين الأوليين ، المتغيب في المثالين الآخرين هو علة الظاهرة(٤) .
4 - The fourth section: They are those who hold that concomitance does not indicate causality. They rely on the following: First: 'If concomitance were proof of the cause, then everything that accompanies something would be the cause of that which accompanies it, but the consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise.' The proof that concomitance is false is that there are cases where concomitance occurs without causality. The clearest are: 1) The cause itself accompanies its effect in existence and non-existence, while the effect is definitely not the cause of the cause. 2) Substance and accident are mutually concomitant in negation and affirmation, while neither is the cause of the other's existence. 3) God's essence and attributes are mutually concomitant. Each attribute of His is concomitant with the other attributes, but there is no causality between them. 4) Correlatives like fatherhood and prophethood are mutually concomitant in existence and non-existence, while neither is the cause of the other's existence. 5) The six directions are inseparable from one another, while causality does not obtain between them. 6) The definiens and the definiendum. Second: Concomitance consists of two things: co-presence and co-absence. Co-presence is not proof of the causality of the description, and co-absence is not considered in religious causes. If each of them is not a proof of causality, then their combination is likewise not. The proponents of concomitance respond: It is not necessary that if each individually is not a proof of causality, then their combination is likewise not. For the combination has a property that individuals do not have. Third: The concomitant description might be a description that is invariably associated with the cause, without being the cause itself, like the specific aroma that accompanies intoxication. There is no way to determine this except by testing for the absence of another description via investigation (sabr). This means moving from the method of concomitance to the method of sifting. This path is the law of concomitant variation in occurrence and in absence, according to Mill's Joint method of Agreement and Difference. It is based on: when the cause is present, its effect is present; when it is absent, it is absent. If we examine two cases in each of which a particular phenomenon appears, and we find that they differ in everything except one single matter, and two other cases in which the phenomenon does not appear, and we find that they agree in nothing except the absence of that matter – then we conclude that that matter which is present in the first two examples and absent in the other two is the cause of the phenomenon.(4)
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth position in the debate on dawaran (concomitance) as evidence for causality (illah). The opponents of dawaran as proof give three main arguments. The first argument is logical: if dawaran were evidence, then any concomitant would be a cause, but that leads to absurdity. They list six examples where concomitance is present but causality is not: 1) a cause and its effect are concomitant but the effect is not the cause of the cause; 2) substance and accident are concomitant but one is not the cause of the other; 3) God's essence and attributes are concomitant but attributes are not causes of each other; 4) correlatives like fatherhood and prophethood are concomitant but not causes of each other; 5) the six directions are inseparable but not causally related; 6) the definiens and definiendum are reciprocal but not causes. The second argument is that dawaran comprises two components: tard (co-presence) and 'aks (co-absence). The opponents claim that tard is not a proof of causality, and 'aks is not even accepted in religious causes (shar'i 'illal). Therefore, the combination also cannot be a proof. The third argument is that a concomitant description might be merely a constant companion (lazim) of the true cause, not the cause itself, as the specific smell accompanies intoxication but is not the cause of intoxication. To distinguish this, one must use sabr (investigating other descriptions) – moving from dawaran to a more refined method. The author then explains this refined method as the 'law of concomitant variation' (qanun al-talazum), essentially Mill's Joint Method of Agreement and Difference. It requires comparing two cases where the phenomenon occurs and one case where it does not, isolating the one factor that is present in the positives and absent in the negatives, thereby identifying the cause. This passage is a significant engagement with both Islamic usul methodology and Western logic (through Mill).

Key Terms
الدوران
السبر
قانون التلازم
مل

B) Use sabr and the law of concomitant variation
C) Rely on revelation alone
D) Accept that causation cannot be known

Key Takeaway
The third objection exposes the weakness of dawaran: a concomitant may be a mere accompaniment. The solution is to adopt the joint method of concomitant variation, which systematically compares positive and negative cases to isolate the true cause.

Using the joint method described, design a mini-analysis to identify the 'illah for the prohibition of gambling (maysir). List two cases where gambling occurs (e.g., dice games, horse racing) and two where it does not (e.g., a friendly card game without stakes), and determine the common factor that is present in the former and absent in the latter.

The Invalidity of Evidence Principle and Its Critique
Author explains the principle 'batalan al-dalil' and its critique by later scholars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical principle of 'batalan al-dalil' and evaluate the validity of its two premises (minor and major) as criticized by later scholars.

Core Idea
The absence of evidence is not evidence of absence — a critical caution for argument assessment.

This lesson introduces the sixth way in manahij al-bahth, a method for refuting claims by invalidating all their evidence. The principle states that if the evidence for a claim is false, then the claim itself is false. However, later scholars identified a serious flaw: the method depends on two weak premises. Understanding this critique sharpens your ability to distinguish valid refutations from invalid ones.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle and its formulations
Locate the core statement: 'The invalidity of the evidence indicates the invalidity of the claim.' Note the two versions: one as a general principle, and one attributed to al-Baqillani. Recognise that this is a method of refutation.
The principle is also expressed as 'What has no evidence must be negated.'
2
Analyse the two stages of the method
The first stage: gather opponent's evidences and prove them false. The second stage: enumerate all possible evidences (hasr) and refute them through induction. Recognise that the second stage reduces to the first without the enumeration step.
Some theologians saw enumeration as an unnecessary addition.
3
Reconstruct the implied syllogism
The method relies on two premises: (Minor) All possible evidences for the claim have been enumerated and invalidated. (Major) If there is no valid evidence for a claim, the claim is false. The conclusion is that the claim is false.
The author states later scholars criticise both premises as weak.
4
Examine the critique of the premises
The minor premise requires exhaustive enumeration, which is often impossible because evidence may be unknown. The major premise is invalid because absence of evidence does not necessarily mean the claim is false—the claim might be true but have no evidence discovered yet.
The critique also points out absurd consequences: it would negate necessary truths and prove the infinite.
5
Evaluate the method's validity in Islamic debates
Consider a real debate: does the fact that a mutakallim cannot find evidence for a theological position mean the position is false? The critique shows this method cannot prove with certainty; it must be supplemented with other refutation methods.
This principle is often confused with 'absence of evidence is evidence of absence', which is a fallacy.

Source Text

الطريق السادس :
بطلان الدليل يؤذن ببطلان المدلول :

الباقلاني « بطلان الدليل يؤذن ببطلان المدلول »(٣) .

والكبرى ضعيفتان(٤) .
The sixth way: The invalidity of the evidence indicates the invalidity of the claim. 'What has no evidence must be negated' (2) or we place it in the form that al-Baqillani places it: 'The invalidity of the evidence indicates the invalidity of the claim' (3). This way consists of two stages: The first stage is that we seek the evidences of those who affirm a thing, and we prove their falsehood and weakness such that we do not find any other evidence for the existence of the thing besides them. Or we perform an enumeration of the aspects of evidence. Then we perform a thorough induction over them, which ends in negating all these aspects such that we do not find other aspects besides them. Hence, it becomes clear to us that this second form is essentially the first, as it ends in the non-existence of any evidence other than the negated ones, except that it is distinguished by the idea of enumeration. Some theologians saw in this idea an unjustified addition, and therefore they turned away from it to the first form. So it is the negation of what has no evidence because the negation of what has no evidence results in the elimination of necessary and theoretical matters on one hand and the proof of the infinite on the other—both of which are impossible. Later scholars criticized this way from the perspective that both the minor and major premises are weak (4).
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth way, which is the principle that the invalidity of the evidence indicates the invalidity of the claim (batalan al-dalil yu'dhinu bi-batalan al-madlul). The author states two formulations: one from unknown source ('what has no evidence must be negated') and one from al-Baqillani. The method has two stages: either gather the opponent's evidences and prove them false, or enumerate all possible evidences and refute them through induction. The author argues that the second form reduces to the first, except with an added idea of enumeration (hasr). Some theologians found this addition unjustified. Then the author notes that this method leads to absurd consequences: it would require negating necessary truths and theoretical propositions, and also prove the infinite, both impossible. Finally, later scholars criticized this way because its minor and major premises are weak. The minor premise is that all evidences have been enumerated and refuted, which is difficult to establish. The major premise is that if there is no evidence, the claim is false, which is not always valid because a claim might have unknown evidence. So the method is not sound.

Key Terms
بطلان الدليل
المدلول
الحصر
المقدمتين الصغرى والكبرى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did later scholars criticize the sixth way?
A) Because it requires exhaustive enumeration which is often impossible
B) Because it proves the existence of infinite regress
C) Because it eliminates necessary truths
D) Because both the minor and major premises are weak

Key Takeaway
The principle 'batalan al-dalil yu'dhinu bi-batalan al-madlul' is methodologically flawed because it relies on the impossible task of exhaustive enumeration and the invalid inference from absence of evidence to falsehood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Three Aristotelian Laws of Thought
Author defines the three Aristotelian laws of thought.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and state the law of identity, law of non-contradiction, and law of excluded middle, and give their symbolic forms.

Core Idea
The three laws are the self-evident foundation of Aristotelian logic, but Muslim theologians challenged their universality.

In this passage, the author introduces the three Aristotelian laws of thought that form the bedrock of classical logic. These laws—identity, non-contradiction, and excluded middle—were considered self-evident by Aristotle. However, Muslim legal theorists (usuliyyun) and theologians built a new logic opposing them. Understanding these laws is crucial before we examine the Islamic critique.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three laws from the passage
Read the Arabic/text and locate where the author names the three laws: قانون الذاتية, قانون عدم الجمع بين النقيضين, and قانون عدم ارتفاع النقيضين. Note their English equivalents: law of identity, law of non-contradiction, law of excluded middle.
2
State each law in plain language
For each law, write a simple sentence that captures its meaning: (1) Everything is itself; (2) Nothing can both be and not be at the same time; (3) Everything either is or is not—no middle.
Use your own words, not the technical phrasing.
3
Write the symbolic form as given
The passage gives symbolic forms: A is A (identity); A is not both A and not-A (non-contradiction); A is either A or not-A (excluded middle). Memorise these symbols.
4
Note the relationship between non-contradiction and identity
The author mentions that some logicians think the second law merely repeats the first. Reflect on why someone might say that: if everything is itself, then it cannot be its opposite.
This is a point of debate among logicians.

الفَصْلُ السَّادِسُ
نقد المتكلمين لقانوني الفكر الأرسططاليسي
أ - قانون عدم الجمع بين النقيضين .
ب - قانون عدم ارتفاع النقيضين .
تبين لنا أن الأصوليين لم يقبلوا المنطق الأرسططاليسي ، وأنهم أقاموا منطقاً جديداً يعارضه . وقد دعاهم هذا إلى مهاجمة بعض مفكري الإسلام لهذا المنطق في أسسه - وهذا الأساس هو البديهيات التي يقوم عليها وهي القوانين الثلاثة المشهورة قانون الذاتية . وقد عرفه الإسلاميون باسم قانون الهوية أو قانون لهو هو وهي قانون « عدم الجمع بين النقيضين » - أي قانون عدم التناقض وصورته الشرطية مبدأ « قانون ارتفاع النقيضين » أي قانون الوسط الممتنع . ونقد الأصوليين لهذين المبدأين يشبه إلى حد اتجاه المناطقة المعاصرين في القرن العشرين لنقد هذا القانون أو هذين القانونين ، فانه كان من أهم المسائل التي درست في فلسفة العلوم - في هذا القرن - نقد بديهيات العقل . كما أن هذه المسألة بالذات أثيرت في المدرسة الرياضية المعاصرة ، فلم يقبل المعاصرون قانون الثالث المرفوع - أي قانون عدم ارتفاع النقيضين . ونقد قانون عدم الجمع بين النقيضين قانون عدم ارتفاع النقيضين عند المسلمين هو ما سنحاول بحثه في هذا الفصل . ثم نبحث في الفصل القادم نقد المتكلمين لمبدأ آخر بديهي هو قانون العلية .
قلنا إن هذه القوانين هي أساس المنطق الأرسططاليسي بحيث يعرف هذا المنطق وهذا ما يشعر بخطر هذه القوانين أو هذه القواعد في إقامة البناء الأرسططاليسي وقد رأى أرسطو بها مبادىء عامة بديهية تجعل من المنطق علماً حقيقياً أو علم العلوم وتسمح له بوضع مناهج علمية . ويطلق على هذه القوانين تعبيرات شتى ـ فتارة تسمى « بقوانين الفكر » (١) وطوراً ببديهيات البرهان الأساسية ، وحصرها أرسطو في ثلاثة : قانون الذاتية وقانون عدم الجمع بين النقيضين وقانون عدم ارتفاع النقيضين . وهذه القوانين تعود إلى طبيعة العقل فهي بينة في ذاتها . ولم يكن أرسططاليس أول من اكتشفها بل وجدت قبله ، إلا أنه أول من جردها قوانين سابقة على كل تفكير .
أما صورة المبدأ الأول : فهي أن كل شيء هو نفسه ، وصورة المبدأ الثاني : أن الشيء ونقيضه لا يجتمعان في شيء واحد في آن واحد . ويعبر عنه أرسطو بأنه « لا يمكن أن يوجد الشيء ولا يوجد في واحد بعينه » أما المدرسيون فيقولون، التناقض هو أن تثبت أو تنفي لشيء أو عن شيء شيئاً معيناً في آن واحد » وهذا القانون متصل بالقانون الأول ـ فهو يكمله ، لأنه لولا ما يمدنا به لأصبح مبدأ الذاتية غير كامل من الناحية المنطقية أولاً ، وغير مفهوماً فهماً تاماً ثانياً . وذهب بعض المناطقة إلى أن هذا القانون لا يسير بنا خطوة أوسع من قانون الذاتية ـ بل إنه يردد مضمون قانون الذاتية ـ وهو أن الحقيقة واحدة ـ بعبارة مخالفة في الصورة لا في جوهر المعنى . أما صورة القانون الثالث . الشيء إما أن يكون لا ـ وإما لا يكون . أو كما يقول أرسطو « لا وسط بين النقيضين » وإذا كان يعبر عن القانون الأول في صورته الرمزية بأن ا هي ا والقانون الثاني بأن ا ليست ا ولا لا ا ، فان هذا القانون الثالث يعبرون عنه بأن ا إما أن تكون لا ا أو لا لا ا أي أن الحكم ونقيضه لا يمكن أن يكونا باطلين ، بل لا بد لأحدهما من أن يكون صحيحاً والآخر غير صحيح (٢) .
موقف المتكلمين من هذه القوانين . انتقلت هذه القوانين مع ما نقل من
Chapter Six: Critique of the Aristotelian Laws of Thought by the Theologians. A - Law of Non-Contradiction. B - Law of Excluded Middle. It became clear to us that the usuliyyun (legal theorists) did not accept Aristotelian logic, and that they established a new logic opposing it. This led some Muslim thinkers to attack this logic in its foundations - and this foundation is the self-evident principles upon which it is based, namely the three famous laws: the law of identity. The Muslims have known it as the law of identity or the law 'it is it', and the law of non-contradiction - i.e., the law of non-contradiction, and its conditional form is the principle of 'law of excluded middle', i.e., the law of the excluded middle. The critique of these two principles by the usuliyyun resembles to some extent the direction of contemporary logicians in the twentieth century in criticizing this law or these two laws, for it was among the most important issues studied in the philosophy of science - in this century - the critique of the self-evident principles of the mind. Likewise, this very issue was raised in the contemporary mathematical school, so the contemporaries did not accept the law of the excluded middle - i.e., the law of exclusion of the two opposites. The critique of the law of non-contradiction and the law of excluded middle among Muslims is what we will attempt to examine in this chapter. Then we will examine in the next chapter the theologians' critique of another self-evident principle, the law of causality. We said that these laws are the foundation of Aristotelian logic such that this logic is defined by them, and this indicates the danger of these laws or these rules in establishing the Aristotelian edifice. Aristotle considered them general self-evident principles that make logic a true science or the science of sciences and allow it to set scientific methods. These laws are given various expressions - sometimes called 'laws of thought' (1) and sometimes 'basic self-evident principles of demonstration', and Aristotle restricted them to three: the law of identity, the law of non-contradiction, and the law of excluded middle. These laws revert to the nature of the mind, so they are self-evident in themselves. Aristotle was not the first to discover them; they existed before him, but he was the first to abstract them as laws prior to all thought. As for the form of the first principle: it is that everything is itself. The form of the second principle: that a thing and its opposite do not coexist in one thing at one time. Aristotle expresses it as 'it is not possible that a thing exists and does not exist in one and the same'. As for the Scholastics, they say: 'contradiction is to affirm or deny a certain thing of something at one and the same time'. This law is connected to the first law - it completes it, because without what it provides, the principle of identity would be incomplete logically first, and not fully understood second. Some logicians held that this law does not take us a step wider than the law of identity - rather it repeats the content of the law of identity - which is that truth is one - in a different form, not in the essence of meaning. As for the form of the third law: a thing either is or is not. Or as Aristotle says: 'no middle between two opposites'. If the first law is expressed symbolically as A is A, and the second as A is not both A and not-A, then this third law is expressed as A is either A or not-A, i.e., a judgment and its opposite cannot both be false, but one must be true and the other false (2). The position of the theologians regarding these laws. These laws were transmitted along with what was transmitted
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage introduces the sixth chapter of the work, which focuses on the critique by Muslim theologians (mutakallimun) of the Aristotelian laws of thought. The author begins by stating that the legal theorists (usuliyyun) rejected Aristotelian logic and built a new logic in opposition. This led to an attack on the foundations of Aristotelian logic, specifically the three famous self-evident principles: the law of identity, the law of non-contradiction, and the law of excluded middle. The author notes that this critique resembles the contemporary 20th-century criticism of these laws in the philosophy of science and mathematics, where the law of excluded middle was rejected. The author then explains that these three laws are the foundation of Aristotelian logic, making it a true science. Aristotle is credited with abstracting them as prior to all thought, though they existed before. The forms of the three laws are then detailed: the law of identity (everything is itself), the law of non-contradiction (a thing and its opposite cannot coexist at the same time), and the law of excluded middle (a thing either is or is not, with no middle). The author discusses the relationship between the first two laws, noting that some logicians see the second as merely restating the first. Symbolic representations are given: A is A for identity; A is not both A and not-A for non-contradiction; A is either A or not-A for excluded middle. The passage ends with the beginning of a section on the position of the theologians, which is likely continued in subsequent pages. The commentary should note that this is an exposition of the Aristotelian framework and the upcoming critique by Muslim scholars.

The Islamic Critique of the Laws of Thought
Author introduces the Islamic critique of Aristotelian laws and compares it to modern critiques.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the Islamic critique of the Aristotelian laws as presented in the passage and connect it to modern criticisms.

Core Idea
Muslim theologians rejected the absolute self-evidence of the laws, anticipating 20th-century critiques in philosophy of science.

The passage indicates that Muslim legal theorists (usuliyyun) did not accept Aristotelian logic and built an alternative. Their attack targeted the foundations—the three laws. The author draws a direct parallel to the 20th-century rejection of the law of excluded middle in mathematical and scientific philosophy. This lesson unpacks that critique and its significance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three laws from the passage
Re-read the Arabic to locate the three laws: law of identity, non-contradiction, and excluded middle. These are the targets of the Islamic critique.
2
Locate the author's claim about the Islamic rejection
Find the sentence: 'تبين لنا أن الأصوليين لم يقبلوا المنطق الأرسططاليسي، وأنهم أقاموا منطقاً جديداً يعارضه'. This directly states that the usuliyyun rejected Aristotelian logic and established a new one.
Note that the rejection includes the foundational laws, not just details.
3
Note the parallel with 20th-century critiques
The passage says: 'نقد الأصوليين لهذين المبدأين يشبه إلى حد اتجاه المناطقة المعاصرين في القرن العشرين' – the critiques resemble contemporary criticisms. Also, 'فلم يقبل المعاصرون قانون الثالث المرفوع' – contemporaries rejected the law of excluded middle.
This shows the Islamic critique was ahead of its time.
4
Explain the significance of the critique in one sentence
Summarize why this matters: the Islamic critique challenged the universality of self-evident principles, opening the door to alternative logical systems that could accommodate theological doctrines (e.g., the possibility of a 'middle' in legal rulings).
This step requires synthesizing the passage with prior knowledge.

تبين لنا أن الأصوليين لم يقبلوا المنطق الأرسططاليسي ، وأنهم أقاموا منطقاً جديداً يعارضه . وقد دعاهم هذا إلى مهاجمة بعض مفكري الإسلام لهذا المنطق في أسسه - وهذا الأساس هو البديهيات التي يقوم عليها وهي القوانين الثلاثة المشهورة قانون الذاتية . وقد عرفه الإسلاميون باسم قانون الهوية أو قانون لهو هو وهي قانون « عدم الجمع بين النقيضين » - أي قانون عدم التناقض وصورته الشرطية مبدأ « قانون ارتفاع النقيضين » أي قانون الوسط الممتنع . ونقد الأصوليين لهذين المبدأين يشبه إلى حد اتجاه المناطقة المعاصرين في القرن العشرين لنقد هذا القانون أو هذين القانونين ، فانه كان من أهم المسائل التي درست في فلسفة العلوم - في هذا القرن - نقد بديهيات العقل . كما أن هذه المسألة بالذات أثيرت في المدرسة الرياضية المعاصرة ، فلم يقبل المعاصرون قانون الثالث المرفوع - أي قانون عدم ارتفاع النقيضين . ونقد قانون عدم الجمع بين النقيضين قانون عدم ارتفاع النقيضين عند المسلمين هو ما سنحاول بحثه في هذا الفصل . ثم نبحث في الفصل القادم نقد المتكلمين لمبدأ آخر بديهي هو قانون العلية .
قلنا إن هذه القوانين هي أساس المنطق الأرسططاليسي بحيث يعرف هذا المنطق وهذا ما يشعر بخطر هذه القوانين أو هذه القواعد في إقامة البناء الأرسططاليسي وقد رأى أرسطو بها مبادىء عامة بديهية تجعل من المنطق علماً حقيقياً أو علم العلوم وتسمح له بوضع مناهج علمية . ويطلق على هذه القوانين تعبيرات شتى ـ فتارة تسمى « بقوانين الفكر » (١) وطوراً ببديهيات البرهان الأساسية ، وحصرها أرسطو في ثلاثة : قانون الذاتية وقانون عدم الجمع بين النقيضين وقانون عدم ارتفاع النقيضين . وهذه القوانين تعود إلى طبيعة العقل فهي بينة في ذاتها . ولم يكن أرسططاليس أول من اكتشفها بل وجدت قبله ، إلا أنه أول من جردها قوانين سابقة على كل تفكير .
أما صورة المبدأ الأول : فهي أن كل شيء هو نفسه ، وصورة المبدأ الثاني : أن الشيء ونقيضه لا يجتمعان في شيء واحد في آن واحد . ويعبر عنه أرسطو بأنه « لا يمكن أن يوجد الشيء ولا يوجد في واحد بعينه » أما المدرسيون فيقولون، التناقض هو أن تثبت أو تنفي لشيء أو عن شيء شيئاً معيناً في آن واحد » وهذا القانون متصل بالقانون الأول ـ فهو يكمله ، لأنه لولا ما يمدنا به لأصبح مبدأ الذاتية غير كامل من الناحية المنطقية أولاً ، وغير مفهوماً فهماً تاماً ثانياً . وذهب بعض المناطقة إلى أن هذا القانون لا يسير بنا خطوة أوسع من قانون الذاتية ـ بل إنه يردد مضمون قانون الذاتية ـ وهو أن الحقيقة واحدة ـ بعبارة مخالفة في الصورة لا في جوهر المعنى . أما صورة القانون الثالث . الشيء إما أن يكون لا ـ وإما لا يكون . أو كما يقول أرسطو « لا وسط بين النقيضين » وإذا كان يعبر عن القانون الأول في صورته الرمزية بأن ا هي ا والقانون الثاني بأن ا ليست ا ولا لا ا ، فان هذا القانون الثالث يعبرون عنه بأن ا إما أن تكون لا ا أو لا لا ا أي أن الحكم ونقيضه لا يمكن أن يكونا باطلين ، بل لا بد لأحدهما من أن يكون صحيحاً والآخر غير صحيح (٢) .

Chapter Six: Critique of the Aristotelian Laws of Thought by the Theologians. A - Law of Non-Contradiction. B - Law of Excluded Middle. It became clear to us that the usuliyyun (legal theorists) did not accept Aristotelian logic, and that they established a new logic opposing it. This led some Muslim thinkers to attack this logic in its foundations - and this foundation is the self-evident principles upon which it is based, namely the three famous laws: the law of identity. The Muslims have known it as the law of identity or the law 'it is it', and the law of non-contradiction - i.e., the law of non-contradiction, and its conditional form is the principle of 'law of excluded middle', i.e., the law of the excluded middle. The critique of these two principles by the usuliyyun resembles to some extent the direction of contemporary logicians in the twentieth century in criticizing this law or these two laws, for it was among the most important issues studied in the philosophy of science - in this century - the critique of the self-evident principles of the mind. Likewise, this very issue was raised in the contemporary mathematical school, so the contemporaries did not accept the law of the excluded middle - i.e., the law of exclusion of the two opposites. The critique of the law of non-contradiction and the law of excluded middle among Muslims is what we will attempt to examine in this chapter. Then we will examine in the next chapter the theologians' critique of another self-evident principle, the law of causality. We said that these laws are the foundation of Aristotelian logic such that this logic is defined by them, and this indicates the danger of these laws or these rules in establishing the Aristotelian edifice. Aristotle considered them general self-evident principles that make logic a true science or the science of sciences and allow it to set scientific methods. These laws are given various expressions - sometimes called 'laws of thought' (1) and sometimes 'basic self-evident principles of demonstration', and Aristotle restricted them to three: the law of identity, the law of non-contradiction, and the law of excluded middle. These laws revert to the nature of the mind, so they are self-evident in themselves. Aristotle was not the first to discover them; they existed before him, but he was the first to abstract them as laws prior to all thought. As for the form of the first principle: it is that everything is itself. The form of the second principle: that a thing and its opposite do not coexist in one thing at one time. Aristotle expresses it as 'it is not possible that a thing exists and does not exist in one and the same'. As for the Scholastics, they say: 'contradiction is to affirm or deny a certain thing of something at one and the same time'. This law is connected to the first law - it completes it, because without what it provides, the principle of identity would be incomplete logically first, and not fully understood second. Some logicians held that this law does not take us a step wider than the law of identity - rather it repeats the content of the law of identity - which is that truth is one - in a different form, not in the essence of meaning. As for the form of the third law: a thing either is or is not. Or as Aristotle says: 'no middle between two opposites'. If the first law is expressed symbolically as A is A, and the second as A is not both A and not-A, then this third law is expressed as A is either A or not-A, i.e., a judgment and its opposite cannot both be false, but one must be true and the other false (2). The Three Laws of Thought: An Overview
Author defines the three fundamental laws of thought and their symbolic formulations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three Aristotelian laws of thought and state their symbolic formulations.

Core Idea
The three laws of thought are self-evident axioms that underpin all rational discourse.

This lesson introduces the three fundamental laws of thought as defined by Aristotle: identity, non-contradiction, and excluded middle. These laws are considered basic axioms of demonstration, and they are innate to the intellect. You will learn their verbal formulations and symbolic expressions.

Guided Path
1
Locate the three laws in the text
Find where the author lists the three laws: law of identity (قانون الذاتية), law of non-contradiction (قانون عدم الجمع بين النقيضين), and law of excluded middle (قانون عدم ارتفاع النقيضين).
2
Identify the verbal formulation of each law
For identity: 'everything is itself'. For non-contradiction: 'a thing and its contradictory cannot coexist'. For excluded middle: 'a thing either is or is not'.
Note that the author provides these formulations in Arabic.
3
Match each law to its symbolic expression
The symbols use the variable 'ا' (A). Identity: A is A. Non-contradiction: A is not both A and not-A. Excluded middle: A is either A or not-A.
These symbolic forms are given towards the end of the passage.
4
Understand that these are axioms of demonstration
The passage calls them 'badihiyyat al-burhan' (basic axioms of demonstration), meaning they are self-evident and prior to all reasoning.

ويطلق على هذه القوانين تعبيرات شتى ـ فتارة تسمى « بقوانين الفكر » وطوراً ببديهيات البرهان الأساسية ، وحصرها أرسطو في ثلاثة : قانون الذاتية وقانون عدم الجمع بين النقيضين وقانون عدم ارتفاع النقيضين . وهذه القوانين تعود إلى طبيعة العقل فهي بينة في ذاتها . ولم يكن أرسططاليس أول من اكتشفها بل وجدت قبله ، إلا أنه أول من جردها قوانين سابقة على كل تفكير . أما صورة المبدأ الأول : فهي أن كل شيء هو نفسه ، وصورة المبدأ الثاني : أن الشيء ونقيضه لا يجتمعان في شيء واحد في آن واحد . ويعبر عنه أرسطو بأنه « لا يمكن أن يوجد الشيء ولا يوجد في واحد بعينه » أما المدرسيون فيقولون، التناقض هو أن تثبت أو تنفي لشيء أو عن شيء شيئاً معيناً في آن واحد » وهذا القانون متصل بالقانون الأول ـ فهو يكمله ، لأنه لولا ما يمدنا به لأصبح مبدأ الذاتية غير كامل من الناحية المنطقية أولاً ، وغير مفهوماً فهماً تاماً ثانياً . وذهب بعض المناطقة إلى أن هذا القانون لا يسير بنا خطوة أوسع من قانون الذاتية ـ بل إنه يردد مضمون قانون الذاتية ـ وهو أن الحقيقة واحدة ـ بعبارة مخالفة في الصورة لا في جوهر المعنى . أما صورة القانون الثالث . الشيء إما أن يكون لا ـ وإما لا يكون . أو كما يقول أرسطو « لا وسط بين النقيضين » وإذا كان يعبر عن القانون الأول في صورته الرمزية بأن ا هي ا والقانون الثاني بأن ا ليست ا ولا لا ا ، فان هذا القانون الثالث يعبرون عنه بأن ا إما أن تكون لا ا أو لا لا ا أي أن الحكم ونقيضه لا يمكن أن يكونا باطلين ، بل لا بد لأحدهما من أن يكون صحيحاً والآخر غير صحيح
Various expressions are used for these laws — sometimes they are called 'laws of thought' and at other times 'basic axioms of demonstration'. Aristotle confined them to three: the law of identity, the law of non-contradiction, and the law of excluded middle. These laws pertain to the nature of the intellect and are self-evident. Aristotle was not the first to discover them; they existed before him, but he was the first to abstract them as laws prior to all thinking. As for the form of the first principle: it is that everything is itself. The form of the second principle: a thing and its contradictory cannot coexist in one thing at one time. Aristotle expresses it as 'It is impossible for a thing to exist and not exist in the same one'. The Scholastics say: 'Contradiction is to affirm or deny a certain thing of or for a thing at the same time.' This law is connected to the first law — it completes it, because without what it provides, the principle of identity would be logically incomplete first, and not fully understood second. Some logicians held that this law does not take us a step beyond the law of identity — rather it repeats the content of the law of identity — which is that truth is one — in a different form not in the essence of the meaning. As for the form of the third law: a thing either is or is not. Or as Aristotle says: 'There is no middle between contradictories.' If the first law is expressed symbolically as 'A is A', and the second as 'A is not both A and not-A', then this third law is expressed as 'A is either A or not-A', i.e., a judgment and its contradictory cannot both be false; rather one must be true and the other false.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage defines the three fundamental laws of thought as systematized by Aristotle: the law of identity, the law of non-contradiction, and the law of excluded middle. It begins by stating that these laws are known by various names and are self-evident to the intellect. Aristotle is credited with abstracting them as prior to all reasoning. The passage then explains each law: the first law states that everything is itself; the second forbids the coexistence of a thing and its opposite; the third asserts that no middle exists between contradictories. It notes the interconnection between the first two laws and mentions a debate among logicians about whether the second law adds anything beyond the first. Symbolic formulations are given using the variable 'ا' (A). The passage is definitional and expository, setting the stage for the subsequent critique by Muslim theologians.

The Law of Identity: A is A
Author defines the law of identity and its symbolic form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the law of identity, explain its symbolic form, and identify violations in Islamic theological statements.

Core Idea
The law of identity is the bedrock of rational thought: everything is itself.

The first and most basic law of thought is the law of identity: everything is itself. It asserts that each thing has a fixed nature. In symbolic logic, it is expressed as 'A is A'. This lesson focuses on understanding this law and its role as a foundational axiom.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition of the law of identity
In the passage, the law is stated as 'كل شيء هو نفسه' (everything is itself). Write this down.
2
Identify its symbolic expression
The passage gives the symbolic form: 'ا هي ا' (A is A).
This is the simplest logical statement.
3
Understand its self-evident nature
The passage says these laws are 'بينة في ذاتها' (self-evident). The law of identity is immediately known to the intellect.
4
Apply the law to an Islamic theological example
In Kalam, the law of identity implies that the Creator is exactly the Creator and not the creation. If one confuses the attributes, they violate identity.
For instance, 'The Creator is the creation' would violate identity.

ويطلق على هذه القوانين تعبيرات شتى ـ فتارة تسمى « بقوانين الفكر » وطوراً ببديهيات البرهان الأساسية ، وحصرها أرسطو في ثلاثة : قانون الذاتية وقانون عدم الجمع بين النقيضين وقانون عدم ارتفاع النقيضين . وهذه القوانين تعود إلى طبيعة العقل فهي بينة في ذاتها . ولم يكن أرسططاليس أول من اكتشفها بل وجدت قبله ، إلا أنه أول من جردها قوانين سابقة على كل تفكير . أما صورة المبدأ الأول : فهي أن كل شيء هو نفسه ، وصورة المبدأ الثاني : أن الشيء ونقيضه لا يجتمعان في شيء واحد في آن واحد . ويعبر عنه أرسطو بأنه « لا يمكن أن يوجد الشيء ولا يوجد في واحد بعينه » أما المدرسيون فيقولون، التناقض هو أن تثبت أو تنفي لشيء أو عن شيء شيئاً معيناً في آن واحد » وهذا القانون متصل بالقانون الأول ـ فهو يكمله ، لأنه لولا ما يمدنا به لأصبح مبدأ الذاتية غير كامل من الناحية المنطقية أولاً ، وغير مفهوماً فهماً تاماً ثانياً . وذهب بعض المناطقة إلى أن هذا القانون لا يسير بنا خطوة أوسع من قانون الذاتية ـ بل إنه يردد مضمون قانون الذاتية ـ وهو أن الحقيقة واحدة ـ بعبارة مخالفة في الصورة لا في جوهر المعنى . أما صورة القانون الثالث . الشيء إما أن يكون لا ـ وإما لا يكون . أو كما يقول أرسطو « لا وسط بين النقيضين » وإذا كان يعبر عن القانون الأول في صورته الرمزية بأن ا هي ا والقانون الثاني بأن ا ليست ا ولا لا ا ، فان هذا القانون الثالث يعبرون عنه بأن ا إما أن تكون لا ا أو لا لا ا أي أن الحكم ونقيضه لا يمكن أن يكونا باطلين ، بل لا بد لأحدهما من أن يكون صحيحاً والآخر غير صحيح
Various expressions are used for these laws — sometimes they are called 'laws of thought' and at other times 'basic axioms of demonstration'. Aristotle confined them to three: the law of identity, the law of non-contradiction, and the law of excluded middle. These laws pertain to the nature of the intellect and are self-evident. Aristotle was not the first to discover them; they existed before him, but he was the first to abstract them as laws prior to all thinking. As for the form of the first principle: it is that everything is itself. The form of the second principle: a thing and its contradictory cannot coexist in one thing at one time. Aristotle expresses it as 'It is impossible for a thing to exist and not exist in the same one'. The Scholastics say: 'Contradiction is to affirm or deny a certain thing of or for a thing at the same time.' This law is connected to the first law — it completes it, because without what it provides, the principle of identity would be logically incomplete first, and not fully understood second. Some logicians held that this law does not take us a step beyond the law of identity — rather it repeats the content of the law of identity — which is that truth is one — in a different form not in the essence of the meaning. As for the form of the third law: a thing either is or is not. Or as Aristotle says: 'There is no middle between contradictories.' If the first law is expressed symbolically as 'A is A', and the second as 'A is not both A and not-A', then this third law is expressed as 'A is either A or not-A', i.e., a judgment and its contradictory cannot both be false; rather one must be true and the other false.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage defines the three fundamental laws of thought as systematized by Aristotle: the law of identity, the law of non-contradiction, and the law of excluded middle. It begins by stating that these laws are known by various names and are self-evident to the intellect. Aristotle is credited with abstracting them as prior to all reasoning. The passage then explains each law: the first law states that everything is itself; the second forbids the coexistence of a thing and its opposite; the third asserts that no middle exists between contradictories. It notes the interconnection between the first two laws and mentions a debate among logicians about whether the second law adds anything beyond the first. Symbolic formulations are given using the variable 'ا' (A). The passage is definitional and expository, setting the stage for the subsequent critique by Muslim theologians.

Key Terms
قانون الذاتية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the symbolic form of the law of identity as given in the passage?
A) A is not A
B) A is A
C) A is either A or not-A
D) A is both A and not-A

Key Takeaway
The law of identity states that everything is itself, symbolically A is A, and it is the foundational axiom of all reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a theological statement that violates the law of identity. For example, 'The Qur'an is not the Qur'an.' Explain why it violates identity.
?
In Fiqh, the legal ruling 'wajib' (obligatory) must be identical to itself. Give an example of a logical error that would occur if a jurist treated 'wajib' as not 'wajib'.

The Law of Non-Contradiction: A is not both A and not-A
Author defines the law of non-contradiction and its symbolic form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the law of non-contradiction, explain its symbolic form, and apply it to Islamic theological and legal arguments.

Core Idea
Contradictions cannot coexist; the law of non-contradiction is the guardian of rational consistency.

The second law of thought is the law of non-contradiction: a thing and its contradictory cannot coexist in one thing at one time. This law ensures logical consistency. In symbolic form, it is expressed as 'A is not both A and not-A'. This lesson explores its definition and its connection to the law of identity.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition of the law of non-contradiction
The passage states: 'الشيء ونقيضه لا يجتمعان في شيء واحد في آن واحد' (a thing and its contradictory cannot coexist in one thing at one time).
2
Identify its symbolic expression
The passage gives: 'ا ليست ا ولا لا ا' (A is not both A and not-A).
This symbolically captures the prohibition of contradiction.
3
Understand its relationship to the law of identity
The passage notes that this law completes the law of identity. Some logicians argue it merely repeats identity in different words.
The author suggests it is complementary.
4
Apply the law to a Fiqh example
In Fiqh, a ruling cannot be both wajib (obligatory) and haram (forbidden) for the same person at the same time. This would violate non-contradiction.
For instance, claiming an action is both wajib and haram is a contradiction.

The Law of Excluded Middle: A is either A or not-A
Author defines the law of excluded middle and its symbolic form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the law of excluded middle, explain its symbolic form, and use it to evaluate propositions in Islamic jurisprudence.

Core Idea
For any contradictory pair, one must be true; there is no middle ground.

The third law of thought is the law of excluded middle: a thing either is or is not. There is no middle between contradictories. In symbolic form, it is 'A is either A or not-A'. This law ensures that every proposition has a truth value. This lesson examines its definition and application.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition of the law of excluded middle
The passage states: 'الشيء إما أن يكون لا وإما لا يكون' (a thing either is or is not). Also 'لا وسط بين النقيضين' (no middle between contradictories).
2
Identify its symbolic expression
The passage gives: 'ا إما أن تكون لا ا أو لا لا ا' (A is either A or not-A).
This expresses that either A is true or not-A is true.
3
Understand that it rules out a third option
The passage says 'لا وسط بين النقيضين', meaning there is no third truth value.
4
Apply to a Fiqh ruling
A legal action is either valid or invalid; there is no third alternative. For instance, a contract is either valid or invalid.
This law forces a binary judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Mutakallimun's Critique of Non-Contradiction via Divine Power
Author presents the mutakallimun's critique of the law of non-contradiction by limiting its scope through divine power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the theological basis on which some mutakallimun challenged the law of non-contradiction and evaluate whether the challenge is logically sound.

Core Idea
Some theologians argued that God's power includes opposites, thereby negating the law of non-contradiction.

In this lesson, we examine a historical challenge to one of the fundamental laws of thought—the law of non-contradiction. Some Muslim theologians, when considering divine omnipotence, argued that God's power extends beyond the logically possible to encompass contradictions. This critique, while not a direct rebuttal, sought to restrict the law's application in theological contexts. By understanding this argument, you will see how theological commitments can lead to logical deviations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the law being challenged
What is the law of non-contradiction? It states that something cannot both exist and not exist in the same respect. The author says some mutakallimun limited its application.
The law is a fundamental axiom in Aristotelian logic.
2
Extract the theologians' claim
Find the passage where it says 'God's power encompasses both the possible and the impossible.' What exactly do they claim divine power can do?
They say it can combine existence and non-existence, etc.
3
Understand the reasoning
If God can combine opposites, then the law of non-contradiction would be false in cases involving divine power. The theologians use the concept of qudra (power) to challenge the law.
This is a theological exception, not a logical refutation.
4
Evaluate the critique
Does the argument actually refute the logical law? Or does it merely assert a theological exception that may be inconsistent with the law? Consider whether divine power is relevant to logical consistency.
Later Ash'ari theology limited qudra to the possible, reasserting the law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
موقف المتكلمين من هذه القوانين . انتقلت هذه القوانين مع ما نقل من التراث الفلسفي الأرسططاليسي إلى العربية وعرفها المسلمون . نقل إليهم هذا المنطق ـ على أنه صورة اليقين المطلق ، فلما وضعوا منطقهم الخاص ، وساروا في أبحاثهم بدون تقيد بهذا المنطق ، خرج البعض منهم على هذه القوانين . فلم يقبلوا القانونين الثانيين بالذات وسأعرض لخروجهم عليها ثم أحاول أن أبين هل كانت هناك مصادر أجنبية استمد المتكلمون المسلمون منها هذا الخروج وسأتناول في إيجاز بعض المشاكل الكلامية أو الفلسفية التي أقصدها لذاتها ، لكن لكي أستخرج المنهج الذي سار عليه أصحاب تلك المشاكل في بحثهم لها . ۱ ـ موقف المتكلمين من قانون عدم الجمع بين النقيضين : أما قانون عدم الجمع بين النقيضين : فلا نستطيع أن نقول إنه نقد نقداً مباشراً ولكن حاول بعض المتكلمين أن يحددوا استخدامه فخرجوا في أبحاثهم عليه . أما أول صورة في الخروج على هذا القانون ، فنستخلصها استخلاصاً عاماً من كلام بعض المتكلمين في باب القدرة . هل القدرة تتعلق بالممكن والمستحيل أم بالممكن فقط ؟ يجيب هؤلاء المتكلمون بأن قدرة الله تشمل الاثنين الممكن والمستحيل . فللقدرة الإلهية أن تجمع بين الوجود والعدم وتجمع بين القدرة والعجز وتجمع بين العلم والجهل . وبهذا قضوا على قانون عدم الجمع بين النقيضين .
The position of the theologians regarding these laws. These laws were transmitted along with what was transmitted of the Aristotelian philosophical heritage into Arabic, and Muslims came to know them. This logic was transmitted to them as the form of absolute certainty. When they developed their own logic and proceeded in their investigations without adhering to this logic, some of them deviated from these laws. They did not accept the second and third laws in particular. I will present their deviation from them, then I will try to show whether there were foreign sources from which the Muslim theologians derived this deviation. I will briefly discuss some theological or philosophical problems, not for their own sake, but to extract the method followed by the proponents of those problems in their investigation. 1. The position of the theologians on the law of non-contradiction: As for the law of non-contradiction, we cannot say that it was criticized directly, but some theologians attempted to restrict its application, thus deviating from it in their investigations. The first form of deviation from this law is extracted generally from the statements of some theologians in the chapter on power (qudra). Does power relate to the possible and the impossible, or only to the possible? These theologians answer that God's power encompasses both the possible and the impossible. For divine power can combine existence and non-existence, combine power and incapacity, and combine knowledge and ignorance. With this, they annulled the law of non-contradiction.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage introduces the Muslim theologians' (mutakallimun) stance towards the Aristotelian laws of thought, specifically the second law (non-contradiction) and the third law (excluded middle). The author explains that these laws were transmitted to Muslims as part of the Aristotelian heritage and were considered the form of absolute certainty. However, when Muslims developed their own logic, some deviated from these laws. The focus here is on the law of non-contradiction. The author states that some theologians did not directly criticize it but instead limited its application, effectively deviating from it. The first example of this deviation is from the chapter on divine power (qudra). Theologians asked: Does divine power extend to both the possible and the impossible, or only to the possible? They answered that God's power encompasses both, allowing the combination of existence and non-existence, power and incapacity, knowledge and ignorance. This, the author claims, negates the law of non-contradiction. The commentary should explain that this is a theological rather than logical argument, and the law is being challenged on the basis of divine omnipotence.
