Dissecting the Deniers' Argument Against States
Author presents the argument of the deniers of states that forces a contradiction to deny intermediate attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the deniers' argument against intermediate attributes and identify its logical flaw.

Core Idea
To deny states, the deniers force a false dichotomy that itself violates the law of non-contradiction.

In this passage, the author presents a second 'deviation' from the law of non-contradiction, this time by the deniers of states (nafat al-ahwal). Their argument attempts to eliminate the middle ground of 'states' (ahwal) — attributes that are neither existent nor non-existent. By analyzing their reasoning, we see how even opponents of a position can inadvertently violate logical principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the debate
The deniers of states reject any attribute (sifa) that is distinct from the essence but neither existent nor non-existent. The affirmers (muthbita) accept such states. Determine what each side claims about divine attributes.
2
Extract the deniers' premise
Their argument is: 'Existence is either existent (its existence identical to itself) or non-existent.' This implies there is no third category. Write this as a logical disjunction: either existent or non-existent.
The deniers assume this dichotomy covers all possibilities.
3
Apply the premise to attributes
An attribute, if it exists, must fall into one of the two categories. If it is identical to the essence, it is just the essence (existent). If it is not, it is non-existent (nothing). Thus any claim of an intermediate state is impossible.
This directly denies the affirmers' position.
4
Spot the contradiction
The author notes that the deniers 'combined the contradictories.' By asserting the dichotomy while also addressing the affirmers' view, they implicitly recognize the possibility of a middle ground, creating a contradiction.
The deniers were aware of this, but the passage is cut off — their explanation is not given.
5
Evaluate logical force
Assess whether the deniers' argument successfully refutes states. The flaw is that the premise itself is not proven; it assumes what it tries to prove (begging the question against states). The contradiction they fall into shows the limitation of a strict dichotomy.
Later Ash'ari and Maturidi schools affirmed states, indicating the argument was not decisive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage presents a second example of deviation from the law of non-contradiction by the mutakallimun. The author describes an argument made by those who deny the existence of 'states' (ahwal) — attributes that are neither existent nor non-existent — against those who affirm them. The argument aims to deny any attribute distinct from the essence. The argument states that existence is either existent (identical to the essence) or non-existent (nothing). This forces a contradiction, as the deniers are claiming that the attribute must be either existent or non-existent, yet they deny the middle ground of 'state'. The author notes that the deniers were aware of this contradiction but provided a justification. The passage is incomplete, but it illustrates another way theologians challenged the law of non-contradiction by seemingly violating it in their own arguments.

Key Takeaway
The deniers of states attempted to reject intermediate attributes by enforcing a strict dichotomy, but in doing so they fell into self-contradiction, illustrating the complexity of applying the law of non-contradiction to theological questions.

?

Al-Ghazali's Critique of Necessary Causality
Author explains al-Ghazali's denial of necessary causal connection between observed events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain al-Ghazali's argument against necessary causal connection and identify its theological implications.

Core Idea
For al-Ghazali, causality is habit, not necessity—God is the only true cause.

In this passage, the author outlines al-Ghazali's groundbreaking critique of Aristotelian causality. Ghazali argues that what we call 'cause' and 'effect' are merely events that habitually occur together, with no necessary logical or physical bond. This critique has profound implications for theology and natural science, as it affirms God's continuous creative power while explaining empirical regularity as divinely ordained custom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target doctrine
Read the passage to pinpoint exactly what al-Ghazali is critiquing. Note the phrase 'that this observed conjunction... is a conjunction of necessary correlation.'
2
Understand the core argument: observation vs. necessity
Ghazali claims we only observe temporal succession, not causal production. Find where he says observation indicates 'occurrence alongside it, not occurrence by it.'
3
Analyze Ghazali's examples
List several examples he gives (fire and burning, drinking and quenching, etc.). How does each illustrate the lack of necessary connection?
4
Recognize the theological implications
Ghazali concludes that the true agent is God, who creates effects according to His habit. How does this preserve divine power and the possibility of miracles?
5
Assess the nature of the critique
Determine whether the critique is empirical, metaphysical, or theological. Consider its basis in observation and its appeal to God's power.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أسبابها ، فهي وجود حادثتين فقط ، الواحدة بجانب الأخرى ، في مستقر العادة » ولا يمكن أبداً « إيجاب العلة للحكم » ولا تولد شيء عن سبب يوجبه أما إنهم يقولون . إنهم يعلمون حساً واضطراراً أن الإحراق والأسكار الحادثين واقعان عن حرارة النار وشدة الشراب ، فإنه جهل عظيم . وذلك أن الذي نشاهده ونحسه إنما هو تغير حال الجسم عند تناول الشراب ومجاورة النار وكونه سكران ومحترقاً ومتغيراً عما كان عليه فقط » وما يعلم بالضرورة والحس والمشاهدة لا يجوز جحده . كل شيء إذن يحدث في مستقر العادة ، بالضرورة والمشاهدة . وهنا يفتح الباقلاني الباب أمام القدرة الإلهية لكي تتدخل باستمرار وأن تخرق « العادات » والطبيعة بلا شك عند الباقلاني وعند الغزالي كما سنرى بعده مطردة ، وكذلك قوانينها ، ولكن هذه القوانين ليست مطلقة في ذاتها . هنا كان الباقلاني والغزالي بل والأشاعرة عامة ، رواداً ممتازين للعلم الحديث الذي يثبت نفس الشيء . وما لبث نقد العلية الأرسططاليسية أن أخذ صورته الكاملة على يد أبي حامد الغزالي . بل لقد وسم التهافت بنقد العلية وبحثها الممتاز عند الفيلسوف الأشعري الكبير . ولقد نقل الغزالي موضوع نقد العلية إلى مكانها المنطقي ، وهو العلم الطبيعي فيرى أن من أهم المسائل التي يخالفهم فيها في هذا العلم مذهبهم « بأن هذا الأقتران المشاهد في الوجود بين الأسباب والمسببات اقتران تلازم بالضرورة فليس في المقدور ولا في الإمكان إيجاد السبب دون المسبب ، ولا وجود المسبب دون السبب ، ويرى الغزالي خطورة هذه الفكرة الطبيعية للعلية أو للسببية ، إذا طبقت في نطاق ديني هام . فيتجه نحو تراث الأشاعرة ، ليستمد منه مادة ، يصوغها كما قلت من قبل صياغة كاملة ، في إنكار العلاقة الضرورية اللازمة بين العلة والمعلول . إن إنكار هذه العلاقة يستند عند الغزالي إلى أننا نشاهد تعاقب حادثتين - الواحدة بعد الأخرى ، فاصطلحنا على تسمية إحداهما علة والأخرى معلولاً بدون وجود أية رابطة عقلية لازمة بين الحادثتين « الاقتران بين ما يعتقد في العادة سبباً ، وما يعتقد مسبباً ، ليس ضرورياً عندنا ، بل كل شيئين ليس هذا ذاك ولا ذاك هذا ، إن إثبات أحدهما لا يتضمن على الإطلاق إثبات الآخر ولا نفي أحدهما يتضمن على الإطلاق نفي الآخر ، وليس من ضرورة وجود أحدهما وجود الآخر ، ولا من ضرورة عدم أحدهما عدم الآخر ـ ويعطي الغزالي أمثلة متعددة ـ الري والشرب ، والشبع والأكل ، والإحتراق ولقاء النار ، والنور وطلوع الشمس ، والموت وقطع الرقبة ، والشفاء وشرب الدواء ، وإسهال البطن واستعمال المسهل وغير هذا في كل المشاهدات المتقارنة في الطب والنجوم والصناعات والحرف . أما هذا الاقتران فهو يعود إلى ما سبق من تقدير الله لخلقها على التساوق لا لكونه ضرورياً في نفسه . بل إن في مقدور الله أن يخلق الشبع بدون الأكل وأن يخلق الموت دون قطع الرقبة وأن يديم الحياة مع قطع الرقبة . . . وهلم جرا كما يقول الغزالي ـ إلى جميع المقترنات . ويعين الغزالي مثالاً يثبت فكرته وهو الإحتراق في القطن عند ملاقاة النار فإنه يجوز أن يتلاقى القطن والنار دون أن يحدث الإحتراق . ويجوز حدوث انقلاب القطن رماداً محترقاً بدون أن يتلاقى مع النار فالنار إذن ليست فاعلة الإحتراق على الإطلاق . إنها لا تفعل شيئاً « إن فاعل الاحتراق يخلق السواد في القطن والتفرق في أجزائه وجعله حراقاً أو رماداً هو الله تعالى فأما النار فهي جماد لا فعل لها » إذن فما دليل الفلاسفة على أنها الفاعل ، وما الذي دعاهم إلى القول بهذه العلاقة التلازمية بينها وبين الاحتراق . الواقع أنه لا دليل ولا برهان على أن إحدى الحادثتين علة للأخرى والأخرى معلول لها سوى مجرد المشاهدة . والمشاهدة تدل على الحصول عنده ولا تدل على الحصول به ، وأنه لا علة سواه .
Its causes: it is only the existence of two events, one alongside the other, in the seat of habit. And it is never possible to affirm the cause for the judgment, nor does anything arise from a cause that necessitates it. But if they say that they know by sense and necessity that burning and intoxication occurring come from the heat of fire and the strength of drink, that is great ignorance. That is because what we witness and sense is only the change of the body's state upon consuming drink and proximity to fire, and its being intoxicated and burnt and changed from what it was, nothing more. What is known by necessity, sense, and observation cannot be denied. Everything then occurs in the seat of habit, by necessity and observation. Here al-Baqillani opens the door for divine power to intervene constantly and break 'habits.' Nature is undoubtedly, according to al-Baqillani and al-Ghazali as we will see after him, regular, and likewise its laws, but these laws are not absolute in themselves. Here al-Baqillani and al-Ghazali, indeed the Ash'aris in general, were excellent pioneers of modern science which affirms the same thing. The critique of Aristotelian causality soon took its complete form at the hands of Abu Hamid al-Ghazali. Indeed, al-Tahafut was branded by the critique of causality and its excellent investigation by the great Ash'ari philosopher. Al-Ghazali transferred the topic of the critique of causality to its logical place, which is natural science. He holds that among the most important issues in which he disagrees with them in this science is their doctrine 'that this observed conjunction in existence between causes and effects is a conjunction of necessary correlation, so it is not within power or possibility to bring about the cause without the effect, nor the existence of the effect without the cause.' Al-Ghazali sees the danger of this natural idea of causality or causation when applied in an important religious domain. So he turns toward the Ash'ari heritage to draw material from it, which he formulates, as I said before, in a complete formulation, in denying the necessary necessary relationship between cause and effect. This denial of the relationship is based, according to al-Ghazali, on our observation of the succession of two events, one after the other; we then agreed to call one cause and the other effect, without the existence of any necessary mental connection between the two events. 'The conjunction between what is believed habitually to be a cause and what is believed to be an effect is not necessary for us. Rather, any two things, not this being that nor that being this, affirming one does not at all entail affirming the other, nor does negating one at all entail negating the other. Nor is it necessary that the existence of one entails the existence of the other, nor that the non-existence of one entails the non-existence of the other.' And al-Ghazali gives multiple examples: quenching and drinking, satiety and eating, burning and contact with fire, light and sunrise, death and cutting of the neck, recovery and taking medicine, diarrhea and use of a laxative, and so on in all observed conjunctions in medicine, astronomy, crafts, and trades. As for this conjunction, it goes back to God's prior decree to create them in succession, not because of necessity in itself. Rather, it is within God's power to create satiety without eating, to create death without cutting the neck, and to sustain life with the cutting of the neck... and so on, as al-Ghazali says, to all conjunctions. Al-Ghazali gives an example that proves his idea: burning of cotton upon contact with fire. For it is possible for the cotton and fire to meet without burning occurring. And it is possible for the cotton to turn into burnt ashes without meeting fire. Thus fire is not the agent of burning at all. It does nothing. 'The agent of burning creates blackness in the cotton, disintegration of its parts, and turning it into charcoal or ashes is God Almighty. As for fire, it is inanimate, it has no action.' So what is the philosophers' proof that it is the agent? What made them assert this correlative relationship between it and burning? The reality is that there is no proof or demonstration that one of the two events is a cause of the other and the other is its effect, except mere observation. And observation indicates occurrence alongside it, not occurrence by it, and that there is no cause besides Him.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage critically examines the Aristotelian concept of necessary causality, presenting the Ash'ari position as articulated by al-Baqillani and especially al-Ghazali. The author begins by stating that in the Ash'ari view, causation is merely the conjunction of two events in the 'seat of habit' (mustaqarr al-'ada). There is no necessary generation of one from the other. The author then refutes the empiricist claim that we directly perceive fire causing burning or drink causing intoxication, arguing that we only observe the change in the body upon contact, not the causal nexus itself. Al-Baqillani is credited with opening the door for continuous divine intervention and the breaking of habits, while affirming that natural laws are regular but not absolute. The author then notes that al-Ghazali fully developed this critique in his Tahafut al-Falasifa, moving the discussion to the realm of natural science. Al-Ghazali's central argument is that the observed conjunction between causes and effects is not a necessary correlation; it is physically and logically possible to have one without the other. He provides numerous examples from daily life (drinking and quenching, eating and satiety, fire and burning, etc.) to illustrate that the connection is based on habitual observation, not logical necessity. The true agent is God, who creates the effect at the moment of the cause's presence according to His custom. The passage concludes by emphasizing that observation only shows temporal succession, not causal production, and that there is no proof for a necessary tie beyond habit.

Key Terms
علة
معلول
اقتران
عادة

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali's critique shifts causality from metaphysical necessity to divine habit, preserving God's absolute power while accounting for empirical regularity.

Al-Ghazali's Occasionalism: Existence with vs. Existence by
Al-Ghazali argues against necessary causation, using the soul example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Ghazali's distinction between existence with a thing and existence by it, and his denial of necessary causation.

Core Idea
Al-Ghazali severs the link between events, attributing all to God's direct action.

In this passage, al-Ghazali challenges the Aristotelian notion of necessary causality. Using the example of the soul and its faculties, he argues that things may exist concurrently without one causing the other. This ontological move has profound implications for logic and science.

Guided Path
1
Identify the distinction
Find where al-Ghazali distinguishes between 'existence with a thing' and 'existence by it'. What example does he use?
The soul and its powers are not generated by the father or nature.
2
Extract the argument
What conclusion does al-Ghazali draw from this distinction? Why is there no necessary causal connection?
He says: 'There is no causal relationship whatsoever between contingent things.'
3
Note the alternative
What does al-Ghazali propose instead of necessary causation? How does he explain regular occurrences?
He introduces the concept of 'habit' (ʿāda) as the explanation.

الروح والقوى المدركة والمحركة في النطفة ليس متوالداً من فعل الطبائع : الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبة واليبوسة . ولا من فعل الأب . ولا من فعل الإنسان نفسه ، فليس الإنسان فاعلاً لحياته وحواسه وسائر المعاني التي هي فيه « ومعلوم أنها موجودة عنده ، ولم يقل أحد أنها موجودة به » وتسيطر على الغزالي فكرة « أن الوجود عند الشيء لا يدل على أنه موجود به » . ثم من أين يأمن الإنسان أن يكون في مبادىء الوجود علل وأسباب تفيض منها هذه الحوادث عند التلاقي بينها « إلا أنها ثابتة ليست تنعدم ، ولا هي أجسام متحركة فتغيب ، ولو انعدمت أو غابت لأدركنا التفرقة ، وفهمنا أن ثم سبباً وراء ما شاهدناه » . لا علاقة علية إذن بوجه من الوجوه بين الممكنات ولكن تفسر حدوث حادثة بعد أخرى باطراد العادة ليس إلا . فإذا كان الإحراق يحدث عقيب مماسة النار ، والري بعد شرب الماء ، فليس للمماسة ولا للشرب دخل في وجود الاحتراق والري . بل الكل حدث طبقاً لقانون جازم مرغم له أن يوجد الإحتراق بدون المماسة أو الري بدون الشرب . ولكن النتائج التي تنتج عن إنكار العلية كافية لهدم حقائق العلوم كلها ، فإن العلوم جميعها تستند إلى هذا القانون . تنبه ابن رشد إلى هذا ، فاعتبر هذا النقد قولاً خطابياً أو جدلياً تارة وقولاً سوفسطائياً طوراً وكان يحس بنتائجه الخطيرة في نطاق العلوم كلها . « إن من رفع الأسباب فقد رفع العقل وصناعة المنطق تضع وضعاً أن ها هنا أسباباً ومسببات وأن المعرفة بتلك المسببات لا تكون على التمام إلا بمعرفة أسبابها ، فرفع هذه الأشياء هو مبطل للعلم ورفع له » فتتداعى أبحاث المنطق كلها بتداعي هذا القانون . فإنه دخل في نظرية البرهان كما دخل في نظرية الحد وأدى بطلانه في تلك النظرية الأخيرة إلى مخالفة الأصوليين للأرسططاليسيين في أغلب مباحث الحد . بل نرى إمام الحرمين يقول « إن الجمع بالعلة - في قياس الغائب على الشاهد لا أصل له إذ لا علة ولا معلول عندنا » . تنبه الغزالي إلى أنه سيعترض عليه بأن إنكار العلية يؤدي إلى مجالات شنيعة فإن من أنكر لزوم المعلولات عن عللها . وأضافها إلى إرادة مخترعها ، ولم يكن لهذه الإرادة أيضاً منهج مخصوص متعين ، بل يمكن تعينه وتنوعه فجاز لكل واحد منا أن يكون بين يديه سباع ضارية ونيران مشتعلة وجبال راسية وأعداء مستعدة بالأسلحة لقتله وهو لا يراها . . . ومن وضع كتاباً في بيته فيجوز أن يكون قد انقلب حيواناً ، أو لو ترك غلاماً في بيته ، فيجوز انقلابه كلباً ، أو ترك التراب ، فيجوز انقلابه مسكاً . ولكن يجيب الغزالي : بأن هذه الأشياء التي نتجت عن القضاء على قانون العلية لم تحدث وليست هي واجبة . ولكن لا شك أنها ممكنة ، يجوز أن تقع وألا تقع « واستمرار العادة بها مرة بعد أخرى ترسخ في أذهاننا جريانها على وفق العادة الماضية ، ترسخاً لا تنفك عنه » . إن من المسلم به أن النار خلقت ، بحيث إذا تلاقت مع قطعتين متماثلتين أحرقتهما ، ولم تفرق بينهما طالما كانتا متماثلتين من جميع الوجوه ولكن مع هذا يجوز أن يلقى شخص في النار فلا يحترق ، فقد تتغير صفة النار أو تتغير صفة الشخص .
The soul and the perceiving and moving powers in the sperm are not generated from the action of the natures: heat, cold, moisture, and dryness. Nor from the action of the father. Nor from the action of man himself. So man is not the doer of his life, his senses, and the other meanings that are in him. 'It is known that they exist with him, and no one said that they exist by him.' Al-Ghazali is dominated by the idea that 'existence with a thing does not indicate that it exists by it.' Then how can man be certain that in the principles of existence there are causes and reasons from which these events flow when they meet, 'except that they are fixed, not non-existent, nor are they moving bodies that disappear, and if they ceased to exist or disappeared we would perceive the difference and understand that there is a reason beyond what we witnessed.' There is no causal relationship whatsoever between contingent things, but the occurrence of one event after another is explained only by the continuity of habit. So if burning occurs after contact with fire, and quenching after drinking water, then contact and drinking have no role in the existence of burning and quenching. Rather, everything occurs according to a decisive, compelled law that can produce burning without contact or quenching without drinking. But the results that arise from denying causality are sufficient to destroy the truths of all sciences, for all sciences are based on this law. Ibn Rushd noticed this, so he considered this critique a rhetorical or dialectical statement at times and a sophistical statement at other times, and he felt its dangerous consequences in the realm of all sciences. 'Whoever denies causes has denied reason, and the art of logic establishes that there are causes and effects, and that knowledge of those effects is not complete except by knowledge of their causes. So denying these things is an invalidation of knowledge and a removal of it.' So all investigations of logic collapse with the collapse of this law. For it entered the theory of demonstration as it entered the theory of definition, and its invalidity in the latter led to the disagreement of the Uṣūliyyūn with the Aristotelians in most topics of definition. Rather, we see Imām al-Ḥaramayn saying, 'Combining by causality – in analogy from the absent to the present – has no basis, for there is no cause and no effect according to us.' Al-Ghazali realized that he would be objected to that denying causality leads to dreadful consequences: for whoever denies the necessity of effects from their causes and ascribes them to the will of their Creator, and this will also has no specific determined method; rather it can be determined and varied, so it would be possible for each one of us to have in front of him predatory beasts, blazing fires, solid mountains, and enemies armed with weapons to kill him, and he does not see them... And whoever places a book in his house, it is possible that it has turned into an animal, or if he left a boy in his house, it is possible he turned into a dog, or left dust, it is possible it turned into musk. But al-Ghazali answers: that these things that resulted from the elimination of the law of causality have not occurred and are not necessary. But there is no doubt they are possible; they may occur or not occur. 'The continuity of habit with them time after time becomes firmly rooted in our minds of their occurrence according to past habit, a rooting that is not dissociated from it.' It is taken for granted that fire was created such that when it meets two identical pieces it burns them, and it does not differentiate between them as long as they are identical in all respects. But despite this, it is possible that a person is thrown into fire and does not burn, for the nature of fire may change or the nature of the person may change.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage critically examines the implications of denying causality (al-ʿilliyya) for logic and science, focusing on al-Ghazali's occasionalist position. The text begins by establishing al-Ghazali's view that existence with a thing does not entail existence by it, using the example of the soul and faculties not being generated by natural forces. It then argues that there is no necessary causal connection between events; rather, constant conjunction is due to habit (ʿāda). The author highlights Ibn Rushd's (Averroes) reaction that this denial undermines reason and the entire art of logic, which is built upon cause-effect relationships. The passage continues by showing how the denial of causality leads to absurd possibilities in the objection: without necessary causation, anything could happen unpredictably. Finally, al-Ghazali's response is presented: such possibilities are not actualized because of the continuous habit (custom) that God has established, which creates firm expectations in our minds. The text uses examples from natural phenomena (fire burning, water quenching) and everyday scenarios (book turning into animal) to illustrate the dialectical exchange. The commentary should note that this discussion is crucial for logic because both demonstration (burhān) and definition (ḥadd) rely on causal knowledge.

Key Terms
علية
عادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Ghazali's argument in this passage, what is the real explanation for the regular occurrence of one event after another?
A) Necessary causal relationships between the events.
B) The custom (ʿāda) of God who directly creates each event.
C) The inherent natures of things interacting.
D) Random chance with no pattern.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali denies necessary causation, replacing it with divine habit, which poses a fundamental challenge to logic and science.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a case in fiqh where a ruling is extended by causation (taʿlīl). How would al-Ghazali's denial of causality challenge that extension?
?
Explain how the concept of divine custom (ʿāda) might still allow for regularities that form the basis of scientific knowledge, according to al-Ghazali.

Ibn Rushd's Defense of Causality: Logic Collapses Without It
Ibn Rushd responds to al-Ghazali's denial of causality, linking it to the destruction of logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Rushd's argument that denying causality invalidates all sciences and the art of logic.

Core Idea
Without causes, there can be no certain knowledge and no demonstration.

Ibn Rushd (Averroes) was acutely aware that al-Ghazali's occasionalism strikes at the heart of Aristotelian logic. In this passage, he argues that if there are no causes, then there can be no knowledge of effects, and logic itself—which presupposes causal relations—is rendered useless. He accuses al-Ghazali of sophistry.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ibn Rushd's claim
What does Ibn Rushd say about the relationship between causality and the art of logic?
He says: 'Whoever denies causes has denied reason and the art of logic...'
2
Understand the reasoning
Why does he think causation is necessary for logic? Which parts of logic depend on it?
He specifically mentions demonstration (burhān) and definition (ḥadd).
3
Connect to the broader sciences
How does the denial affect all sciences according to the author?
The text says: 'the results that arise from denying causality are sufficient to destroy the truths of all sciences'.

The soul and the perceiving and moving powers in the sperm are not generated from the action of the natures: heat, cold, moisture, and dryness. Nor from the action of the father. Al-Ghazali's Response to the 'Absurd Consequences' Objection
Al-Ghazali addresses the objection that his view leads to unpredictability and absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze al-Ghazali's response to the objection that his denial of causality leads to absurd scenarios, using the concept of divine custom (ʿāda).

Core Idea
Possible absurdities are prevented by God's custom, which grounds our expectations.

Opponents of occasionalism argue that if there is no necessary causation, then anything could happen—a book could turn into an animal, or a person thrown into fire might not burn. Al-Ghazali responds that such possibilities, while conceivable, are not actualized because of God's continuous habit (ʿāda). This response maintains the possibility of miracles while preserving regularity for science.

Guided Path
1
State the objection
What are the absurd consequences that the objector raises? List at least two.
Examples: a person might be surrounded by dangers he doesn't see; a boy could turn into a dog.
2
Identify al-Ghazali's response
How does al-Ghazali answer these objections? What concept does he use?
He uses the idea of 'habit' (ʿāda) and says that such events are possible but not actual.
3
Evaluate the argument
Does al-Ghazali's response successfully defend occasionalism? What is the role of 'firm rooting' in the mind?
He says that continuous habit creates a firm expectation, but the possibility remains.

الروح والقوى المدركة والمحركة في النطفة ليس متوالداً من فعل الطبائع : الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبة واليبوسة . ولا من فعل الأب . ولا من فعل الإنسان نفسه ، فليس الإنسان فاعلاً لحياته وحواسه وسائر المعاني التي هي فيه « ومعلوم أنها موجودة عنده ، ولم يقل أحد أنها موجودة به » وتسيطر على الغزالي فكرة « أن الوجود عند الشيء لا يدل على أنه موجود به » . ثم من أين يأمن الإنسان أن يكون في مبادىء الوجود علل وأسباب تفيض منها هذه الحوادث عند التلاقي بينها « إلا أنها ثابتة ليست تنعدم ، ولا هي أجسام متحركة فتغيب ، ولو انعدمت أو غابت لأدركنا التفرقة ، وفهمنا أن ثم سبباً وراء ما شاهدناه » . لا علاقة علية إذن بوجه من الوجوه بين الممكنات ولكن تفسر حدوث حادثة بعد أخرى باطراد العادة ليس إلا . فإذا كان الإحراق يحدث عقيب مماسة النار ، والري بعد شرب الماء ، فليس للمماسة ولا للشرب دخل في وجود الاحتراق والري . بل الكل حدث طبقاً لقانون جازم مرغم له أن يوجد الإحتراق بدون المماسة أو الري بدون الشرب . ولكن النتائج التي تنتج عن إنكار العلية كافية لهدم حقائق العلوم كلها ، فإن العلوم جميعها تستند إلى هذا القانون . تنبه ابن رشد إلى هذا ، فاعتبر هذا النقد قولاً خطابياً أو جدلياً تارة وقولاً سوفسطائياً طوراً وكان يحس بنتائجه الخطيرة في نطاق العلوم كلها . « إن من رفع الأسباب فقد رفع العقل وصناعة المنطق تضع وضعاً أن ها هنا أسباباً ومسببات وأن المعرفة بتلك المسببات لا تكون على التمام إلا بمعرفة أسبابها ، فرفع هذه الأشياء هو مبطل للعلم ورفع له » فتتداعى أبحاث المنطق كلها بتداعي هذا القانون . فإنه دخل في نظرية البرهان كما دخل في نظرية الحد وأدى بطلانه في تلك النظرية الأخيرة إلى مخالفة الأصوليين للأرسططاليسيين في أغلب مباحث الحد . بل نرى إمام الحرمين يقول « إن الجمع بالعلة - في قياس الغائب على الشاهد لا أصل له إذ لا علة ولا معلول عندنا » . تنبه الغزالي إلى أنه سيعترض عليه بأن إنكار العلية يؤدي إلى مجالات شنيعة فإن من أنكر لزوم المعلولات عن عللها . وأضافها إلى إرادة مخترعها ، ولم يكن لهذه الإرادة أيضاً منهج مخصوص متعين ، بل يمكن تعينه وتنوعه فجاز لكل واحد منا أن يكون بين يديه سباع ضارية ونيران مشتعلة وجبال راسية وأعداء مستعدة بالأسلحة لقتله وهو لا يراها . . . ومن وضع كتاباً في بيته فيجوز أن يكون قد انقلب حيواناً ، أو لو ترك غلاماً في بيته ، فيجوز انقلابه كلباً ، أو ترك التراب ، فيجوز انقلابه مسكاً . ولكن يجيب الغزالي : بأن هذه الأشياء التي نتجت عن القضاء على قانون العلية لم تحدث وليست هي واجبة . ولكن لا شك أنها ممكنة ، يجوز أن تقع وألا تقع « واستمرار العادة بها مرة بعد أخرى ترسخ في أذهاننا جريانها على وفق العادة الماضية ، ترسخاً لا تنفك عنه » . إن من المسلم به أن النار خلقت ، بحيث إذا تلاقت مع قطعتين متماثلتين أحرقتهما ، ولم تفرق بينهما طالما كانتا متماثلتين من جميع الوجوه ولكن مع هذا يجوز أن يلقى شخص في النار فلا يحترق ، فقد تتغير صفة النار أو تتغير صفة الشخص .
The soul and the perceiving and moving powers in the sperm are not generated from the action of the natures: heat, cold, moisture, and dryness. Nor from the action of the father. Nor from the action of man himself. So man is not the doer of his life, his senses, and the other meanings that are in him. 'It is known that they exist with him, and no one said that they exist by him.' Al-Ghazali is dominated by the idea that 'existence with a thing does not indicate that it exists by it.' Then how can man be certain that in the principles of existence there are causes and reasons from which these events flow when they meet, 'except that they are fixed, not non-existent, nor are they moving bodies that disappear, and if they ceased to exist or disappeared we would perceive the difference and understand that there is a reason beyond what we witnessed.' There is no causal relationship whatsoever between contingent things, but the occurrence of one event after another is explained only by the continuity of habit. So if burning occurs after contact with fire, and quenching after drinking water, then contact and drinking have no role in the existence of burning and quenching. Rather, everything occurs according to a decisive, compelled law that can produce burning without contact or quenching without drinking. But the results that arise from denying causality are sufficient to destroy the truths of all sciences, for all sciences are based on this law. Ibn Rushd noticed this, so he considered this critique a rhetorical or dialectical statement at times and a sophistical statement at other times, and he felt its dangerous consequences in the realm of all sciences. 'Whoever denies causes has denied reason, and the art of logic establishes that there are causes and effects, and that knowledge of those effects is not complete except by knowledge of their causes. So denying these things is an invalidation of knowledge and a removal of it.' So all investigations of logic collapse with the collapse of this law. For it entered the theory of demonstration as it entered the theory of definition, and its invalidity in the latter led to the disagreement of the Uṣūliyyūn with the Aristotelians in most topics of definition. Rather, we see Imām al-Ḥaramayn saying, 'Combining by causality – in analogy from the absent to the present – has no basis, for there is no cause and no effect according to us.' Al-Ghazali realized that he would be objected to that denying causality leads to dreadful consequences: for whoever denies the necessity of effects from their causes and ascribes them to the will of their Creator, and this will also has no specific determined method; rather it can be determined and varied, so it would be possible for each one of us to have in front of him predatory beasts, blazing fires, solid mountains, and enemies armed with weapons to kill him, and he does not see them... And whoever places a book in his house, it is possible that it has turned into an animal, or if he left a boy in his house, it is possible he turned into a dog, or left dust, it is possible it turned into musk. But al-Ghazali answers: that these things that resulted from the elimination of the law of causality have not occurred and are not necessary. But there is no doubt they are possible; they may occur or not occur. 'The continuity of habit with them time after time becomes firmly rooted in our minds of their occurrence according to past habit, a rooting that is not dissociated from it.' It is taken for granted that fire was created such that when it meets two identical pieces it burns them, and it does not differentiate between them as long as they are identical in all respects. But despite this, it is possible that a person is thrown into fire and does not burn, for the nature of fire may change or the nature of the person may change.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage critically examines the implications of denying causality (al-ʿilliyya) for logic and science, focusing on al-Ghazali's occasionalist position. The text begins by establishing al-Ghazali's view that existence with a thing does not entail existence by it, using the example of the soul and faculties not being generated by natural forces. It then argues that there is no necessary causal connection between events; rather, constant conjunction is due to habit (ʿāda). The author highlights Ibn Rushd's (Averroes) reaction that this denial undermines reason and the entire art of logic, which is built upon cause-effect relationships. The passage continues by showing how the denial of causality leads to absurd possibilities in the objection: without necessary causation, anything could happen unpredictably. Finally, al-Ghazali's response is presented: such possibilities are not actualized because of the continuous habit (custom) that God has established, which creates firm expectations in our minds. The text uses examples from natural phenomena (fire burning, water quenching) and everyday scenarios (book turning into animal) to illustrate the dialectical exchange. The commentary should note that this discussion is crucial for logic because both demonstration (burhān) and definition (ḥadd) rely on causal knowledge.

عادة
علية
خارق العادة

C) Because the objects themselves have fixed natures preventing change.
D) Because human will prevents them.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali defends occasionalism by admitting the logical possibility of absurdities but grounding actual regularity in God's continuous custom.

Al-Ghazali's Initial Neutrality Toward Logic
Al-Ghazali's initial neutrality and later critique of logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify al-Ghazali's initial position that logic is religiously neutral and explain why he later retracted it.

Core Idea
Logic is neutral; its misuse in theology is the problem.

Al-Ghazali initially defended logic as a neutral tool, akin to the methods of Muslim theologians. However, he later identified fatal flaws in its application to metaphysical questions, leading him to reject it as a path to certainty in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify al-Ghazali's claim about logic's neutrality
Find the passage where he says 'As for logical matters, none of them relate to religion at all negatively.' This is his initial stance.
2
Extract his justification
Al-Ghazali argues that logic deals with proofs, conditions of demonstration, and definitions, and is similar to what theologians do, except in terminology and thoroughness.
3
Note the qualification
He says denying logic only reflects poorly on the denier's intellect and religion; thus logic per se is not objectionable.
4
Recognize the shift
The author then introduces that al-Ghazali found two causes that 'destroy Aristotelian logic from its foundation.' This indicates his neutrality was temporary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والباطنية والفلاسفة والصوفية(١) . وهو حين يرفض طرق الفرق الثلاث الأخرى من البحث ، يدلي براي جديد يهدم به كل أفكاره السابقة فيه . يقول « أما المنطقيات فلا يتعلق شيء منها بالدين نفياً باتاً . بل هو النظر في طرق الأدلة والمقاييس وشروط مقدمات البرهان وكيفية تركيبها وشروط الحد الصحيح وكيفية تركيبه وأن العلم إما تصور وسبيل معرفته الحد وإما تصديق وسبيل معرفته البرهان . وليس في هذا ما ينبغي أن ينكر . بل هو من جنس ما ذكره المتكلمون وأهل النظر من الأدلة وإنما يفارقونهم بالعبارات والاصطلاحات وبزيادة الاستقصاء في التعريفات والتشعيبات »(٢) وليس للدين تعلق بهذا عند الغزالي « وأي تعلق لهذا بمهمات الدين حتى يجحد وينكر . فاذا أنكر ، لم يحصل من إنكاره عند أهل المنطق إلا سوء الاعتقاد في عقل المنكر بل في دينه الذي يزعم أنه موقوف على مثل هذا الإنكار »(۳) ولكن هناك علتين تهدمان المنطق الارسطاطليسي من أساسه . أما أولاهما ـ فهي قصور البرهان الفلسفي عن أن يصل بالإنسان حين يطبقه في الإلهيات إلى درجة اليقين . يقول الغزالي « لهم نوع من الظلم في هذا العلم ، وهو أنهم يجمعون للبرهان شروطاً يعلم أنها تورث اليقين لا محالة ، ولكنهم عند الانتهاء إلى المقاصد الدينية ما أمكنهم الوفاء بتلك الشروط ، بل تساهلوا غاية التساهل »(٤) ولذلك كان من أهم الأسباب التي دعت المسلمين إلى نقد المنطق الارسططاليسي اتصاله بإلهيات أرسطو ، وكانت هذه الإلهيات مخالفة لعقائد المسلمين ، واعتبرت العلة في هذه المخالفة إخراجها على قواعد ناقصة هي قواعد المنطق الارسططاليسي . أما ثانيتهما فيفسرها الغزالي بقوله « إذ ربما ينظر في المنطق من يستحسنه ويراه واضحاً فيظن أن ما ينقل عنهم من الكفريات مؤيدة بمثل تلك البراهين ، فاستعجل بالكفر قبل الانتهاء إلى العلوم الإلهية »(۱) والغريب أن ابن رشد حين يحاول الدفاع عن إلهيات أرسطو ضد الغزالي يحاول إلزام الغزالي صحة هذه الإلهيات لإخراجها على القانون المنطقي ، الذي اعتبره الغزالي قانون العقل الذي لا يرد(٢) . ولهاتين العلتين هدم الغزالي فكرته الأولى عن المنطق ، باعتباره منهجاً من مناهج البحث الموصلة إلى اليقين في جميع فروع المعرفة الإنسانية . وعاد يتلمس طريق المعرفة في الكشف الصوفي أو بمعنى أدق في التجربة الباطنية . فالغزالي إذن تبين له آخر الأمر ما ينتجه تطبيق منطق أرسطو على المسائل الإسلامية وخاصة الإلهية من تناقض .

وما نستطيع إذن استنتاجه من بحثنا في الفصول السابقة أن نظار المسلمين لم يقبلو المنطق الارسططاليسي . ويفسر ابن تيمية هذا تفسيراً دقيقاً « وما زال نظار المسلمين يعيبون طريقة أهل المنطق ويثبتون ما فيها من العي واللكنة وقصور العقل وعجز النطق ويثبتون أنها إلى فساد المنطق العقلي واللساني أقرب منها إلى تقويم ذلك . ولا يرضون أن يسلكوها في نظرهم ومناظراتهم لا مع من يوالونه ولا مع من يعادونه » ووضعوا بجانب هذا منطقاً مخالفاً للمنطق الارسططاليسي . ثم أتى أبو حامد الغزالي فدعا إلى مزج المنطق بعلوم المسلمين « وإنما كثر استعمالها في زمن أبي حامد فانه أدخل مقدمة في المنطق اليوناني في أول كتابه - المستصفى - وزعم أنه لا يثق بعلم من لا يعرف المنطق وصنف فيه معيار العلم ومحك النظر - وصنف كتاباً سماه - القسطاس المستقيم - ذكر فيه خمسة موازين - الثلاث الحمليات والشرطي المتصل والشرطي المنفصل وغير عباراتها إلى أمثلة أخذها من كلام
And the Batinites, the philosophers, and the Sufis. And when he rejects the methods of the three other groups of inquiry, he puts forward a new opinion that demolishes all his previous ideas in it. He says: 'As for logical matters, none of them relate to religion at all negatively. Rather, it is the examination of the methods of proofs and syllogisms, the conditions of the premises of demonstration, how they are composed, the conditions of a correct definition, how it is composed, and that knowledge is either conception (tasawwur) and its way is definition, or assent (tasdiq) and its way is demonstration. There is nothing in this that should be denied. Rather, it is of the same kind as what the theologians and people of theoretical inquiry mentioned concerning proofs, except that they differ from them in expressions and terminologies and in the addition of thoroughness in definitions and subdivisions.' And according to al-Ghazali, religion has no connection with this: 'What connection does this have with the important matters of religion that it should be denied and rejected? If it is denied, then the denier earns nothing from the people of logic except a bad opinion of his intellect, nay, of his religion, which he claims depends on such denial.' But there are two causes that destroy Aristotelian logic from its foundation. The first is the insufficiency of philosophical demonstration to bring a person, when he applies it in metaphysics, to the degree of certainty. Al-Ghazali says: 'They have a kind of injustice in this science, in that they gather conditions for demonstration that they know inevitably produce certainty, but when they come to religious objectives, they are unable to fulfill those conditions, nay, they are extremely lax.' Therefore, one of the most important reasons that called Muslims to criticize Aristotelian logic was its connection with Aristotle's metaphysics, and these metaphysics were contrary to the beliefs of Muslims, and the cause of this contradiction was considered to be its being derived from deficient rules, namely the rules of Aristotelian logic. As for the second cause, al-Ghazali explains it by saying: 'Perhaps someone who studies logic, finding it appealing and clear, may think that what is transmitted from them of disbelief is supported by such demonstrations, and so he hastens to disbelief before reaching the divine sciences.' And strangely, when Ibn Rushd tries to defend Aristotle's metaphysics against al-Ghazali, he tries to force al-Ghazali to accept the soundness of these metaphysics because they are derived from logical law, which al-Ghazali considered the law of reason that cannot be rejected. Because of these two causes, al-Ghazali demolished his earlier idea of logic as a method of inquiry leading to certainty in all branches of human knowledge. And he returned to seeking the path of knowledge in Sufi unveiling, or more precisely, in inner experience. So in the end, al-Ghazali realized what contradiction is produced by applying Aristotle's logic to Islamic issues, especially theological ones.

What we can therefore conclude from our discussion in previous chapters is that Muslim theorists did not accept Aristotelian logic. Ibn Taymiyya explains this precisely: 'Muslim theorists have always criticized the method of the logicians and confirmed what is in it of defect, stammering, deficiency of intellect, and incapacity of speech, and they confirmed that it is closer to corrupting rational and linguistic logic than to rectifying it. And they are not satisfied to adopt it in their reasoning and debates, neither with those they befriend nor with those they oppose.' And alongside this, they established a logic contrary to Aristotelian logic. Then came Abu Hamid al-Ghazali and called for mixing logic with Islamic sciences: 'Indeed, its use increased in the time of Abu Hamid, for he introduced a preface on Greek logic in the beginning of his book al-Mustasfa, and claimed that he does not trust the knowledge of one who does not know logic, and he composed on it Mi'yar al-Ilm and Mihakk al-Nazar, and he composed a book called al-Qistas al-Mustaqim, in which he mentioned five balances: the three categoricals, the conditional conjunctive, and the conditional disjunctive, and he transformed their expressions into examples taken from speech.'
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is a historical analysis of the Islamic critique of Aristotelian logic, focusing on al-Ghazali and Ibn Taymiyya. The author begins by noting that al-Ghazali, after reviewing the methods of the Batinites, philosophers, and Sufis, presents a new opinion that overturns his previous views. Al-Ghazali initially argues that logic per se is neutral: it is merely the study of proofs, syllogisms, conditions of demonstration, and definitions, and it is not contrary to religion; indeed, it is similar to the methods of Muslim theologians except for terminology and thoroughness. He says that denying logic only reflects badly on the denier's intellect and religion. However, al-Ghazali then identifies two fundamental flaws in Aristotelian logic. First, the philosophical demonstration (burhan) fails to achieve certainty when applied to metaphysical (ilāhiyyāt) matters. The logicians set strict conditions for certainty but fail to meet them in theology, being extremely lax. This led Muslims to criticize Aristotle's logic because it was linked to his metaphysics, which contradict Islamic beliefs; the faulty rules of logic were blamed for this contradiction. Second, al-Ghazali warns that someone impressed by logic may accept the heretical doctrines (kufriyyāt) transmitted from the philosophers, thinking they are supported by demonstrations, and thus hasten to unbelief. Al-Ghazali's critique prompts Ibn Rushd to defend Aristotle by insisting that the metaphysical conclusions are derived from logical law, which al-Ghazali himself acknowledged as irrefutable. The author then concludes that al-Ghazali abandoned his earlier view of logic as a universal method for certainty and turned to Sufi unveiling (kashf). The passage then shifts to Ibn Taymiyya, who confirms that Muslim scholars always criticized Aristotelian logic for its defects and established an alternative logic. Ibn Taymiyya notes that al-Ghazali later popularized mixing Greek logic with Islamic sciences by introducing it in his legal theory work al-Mustasfa and writing other works on logic.

Key Terms
منطق
برهان
يقين
كشف صوفي

C) He considered it superior to Islamic methods.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali's critique did not reject logic per se but its pretension to yield certainty in metaphysical questions.

The Two Causes of Rejection
Al-Ghazali's two main reasons for rejecting Aristotelian logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain al-Ghazali's two specific objections to Aristotelian logic and assess their validity.

Core Idea
Two flaws: logical insufficiency and practical danger.

Al-Ghazali identifies two root causes that undermine Aristotelian logic. First, the demonstration (burhan) fails to achieve its own standards of certainty when applied to theology. Second, the persuasive appearance of logic may lead unsuspecting students into accepting heretical beliefs. Understanding these critiques is crucial for evaluating the Islamic response to Greek philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Locate the first cause
Find the passage where al-Ghazali says 'the insufficiency of philosophical demonstration to bring a person... to the degree of certainty.'
2
Analyze al-Ghazali's reasoning for the first cause
Logicians impose strict conditions for certainty but are lax in fulfilling them for theological matters; thus, demonstration fails in metaphysics.
3
Find the second cause
Al-Ghazali warns that someone impressed by logic may think heretical doctrines are supported by demonstrations and thus hasten to disbelief.
4
Consider the paradox
Al-Ghazali's own earlier embrace of logic made him realize its dangers; Ibn Rushd later tried to use logic to defend metaphysics against al-Ghazali.

والباطنية والفلاسفة والصوفية(١) . وهو حين يرفض طرق الفرق الثلاث الأخرى من البحث ، يدلي براي جديد يهدم به كل أفكاره السابقة فيه . يقول « أما المنطقيات فلا يتعلق شيء منها بالدين نفياً باتاً . بل هو النظر في طرق الأدلة والمقاييس وشروط مقدمات البرهان وكيفية تركيبها وشروط الحد الصحيح وكيفية تركيبه وأن العلم إما تصور وسبيل معرفته الحد وإما تصديق وسبيل معرفته البرهان . وليس في هذا ما ينبغي أن ينكر . بل هو من جنس ما ذكره المتكلمون وأهل النظر من الأدلة وإنما يفارقونهم بالعبارات والاصطلاحات وبزيادة الاستقصاء في التعريفات والتشعيبات »(٢) وليس للدين تعلق بهذا عند الغزالي « وأي تعلق لهذا بمهمات الدين حتى يجحد وينكر . فاذا أنكر ، لم يحصل من إنكاره عند أهل المنطق إلا سوء الاعتقاد في عقل المنكر بل في دينه الذي يزعم أنه موقوف على مثل هذا الإنكار »(۳) ولكن هناك علتين تهدمان المنطق الارسطاطليسي من أساسه . أما أولاهما ـ فهي قصور البرهان الفلسفي عن أن يصل بالإنسان حين يطبقه في الإلهيات إلى درجة اليقين . يقول الغزالي « لهم نوع من الظلم في هذا العلم ، وهو أنهم يجمعون للبرهان شروطاً يعلم أنها تورث اليقين لا محالة ، ولكنهم عند الانتهاء إلى المقاصد الدينية ما أمكنهم الوفاء بتلك الشروط ، بل تساهلوا غاية التساهل »(٤) ولذلك كان من أهم الأسباب التي دعت المسلمين إلى نقد المنطق الارسططاليسي اتصاله بإلهيات أرسطو ، وكانت هذه الإلهيات مخالفة لعقائد المسلمين ، واعتبرت العلة في هذه المخالفة إخراجها على قواعد ناقصة هي قواعد المنطق الارسططاليسي . أما ثانيتهما فيفسرها الغزالي بقوله « إذ ربما ينظر في المنطق من يستحسنه ويراه واضحاً فيظن أن ما ينقل عنهم من الكفريات مؤيدة بمثل تلك البراهين ، فاستعجل بالكفر قبل الانتهاء إلى العلوم الإلهية »(۱) والغريب أن ابن رشد حين يحاول الدفاع عن إلهيات أرسطو ضد الغزالي يحاول إلزام الغزالي صحة هذه الإلهيات لإخراجها على القانون المنطقي ، الذي اعتبره الغزالي قانون العقل الذي لا يرد(٢) . ولهاتين العلتين هدم الغزالي فكرته الأولى عن المنطق ، باعتباره منهجاً من مناهج البحث الموصلة إلى اليقين في جميع فروع المعرفة الإنسانية . وعاد يتلمس طريق المعرفة في الكشف الصوفي أو بمعنى أدق في التجربة الباطنية . فالغزالي إذن تبين له آخر الأمر ما ينتجه تطبيق منطق أرسطو على المسائل الإسلامية وخاصة الإلهية من تناقض .

وما نستطيع إذن استنتاجه من بحثنا في الفصول السابقة أن نظار المسلمين لم يقبلو المنطق الارسططاليسي . ويفسر ابن تيمية هذا تفسيراً دقيقاً « وما زال نظار المسلمين يعيبون طريقة أهل المنطق ويثبتون ما فيها من العي واللكنة وقصور العقل وعجز النطق ويثبتون أنها إلى فساد المنطق العقلي واللساني أقرب منها إلى تقويم ذلك . ولا يرضون أن يسلكوها في نظرهم ومناظراتهم لا مع من يوالونه ولا مع من يعادونه » ووضعوا بجانب هذا منطقاً مخالفاً للمنطق الارسططاليسي . ثم أتى أبو حامد الغزالي فدعا إلى مزج المنطق بعلوم المسلمين « وإنما كثر استعمالها في زمن أبي حامد فانه أدخل مقدمة في المنطق اليوناني في أول كتابه - المستصفى - وزعم أنه لا يثق بعلم من لا يعرف المنطق وصنف فيه معيار العلم ومحك النظر - وصنف كتاباً سماه - القسطاس المستقيم - ذكر فيه خمسة موازين - الثلاث الحمليات والشرطي المتصل والشرطي المنفصل وغير عباراتها إلى أمثلة أخذها من كلام
And the Batinites, the philosophers, and the Sufis. And when he rejects the methods of the three other groups of inquiry, he puts forward a new opinion that demolishes all his previous ideas in it. He says: 'As for logical matters, none of them relate to religion at all negatively. Rather, it is the examination of the methods of proofs and syllogisms, the conditions of the premises of demonstration, how they are composed, the conditions of a correct definition, how it is composed, and that knowledge is either conception (tasawwur) and its way is definition, or assent (tasdiq) and its way is demonstration. There is nothing in this that should be denied. Rather, it is of the same kind as what the theologians and people of theoretical inquiry mentioned concerning proofs, except that they differ from them in expressions and terminologies and in the addition of thoroughness in definitions and subdivisions.' And according to al-Ghazali, religion has no connection with this: 'What connection does this have with the important matters of religion that it should be denied and rejected? If it is denied, then the denier earns nothing from the people of logic except a bad opinion of his intellect, nay, of his religion, which he claims depends on such denial.' But there are two causes that destroy Aristotelian logic from its foundation. The first is the insufficiency of philosophical demonstration to bring a person, when he applies it in metaphysics, to the degree of certainty. Al-Ghazali says: 'They have a kind of injustice in this science, in that they gather conditions for demonstration that they know inevitably produce certainty, but when they come to religious objectives, they are unable to fulfill those conditions, nay, they are extremely lax.' Therefore, one of the most important reasons that called Muslims to criticize Aristotelian logic was its connection with Aristotle's metaphysics, and these metaphysics were contrary to the beliefs of Muslims, and the cause of this contradiction was considered to be its being derived from deficient rules, namely the rules of Aristotelian logic. As for the second cause, al-Ghazali explains it by saying: 'Perhaps someone who studies logic, finding it appealing and clear, may think that what is transmitted from them of disbelief is supported by such demonstrations, and so he hastens to disbelief before reaching the divine sciences.' And strangely, when Ibn Rushd tries to defend Aristotle's metaphysics against al-Ghazali, he tries to force al-Ghazali to accept the soundness of these metaphysics because they are derived from logical law, which al-Ghazali considered the law of reason that cannot be rejected. Because of these two causes, al-Ghazali demolished his earlier idea of logic as a method of inquiry leading to certainty in all branches of human knowledge. And he returned to seeking the path of knowledge in Sufi unveiling, or more precisely, in inner experience. So in the end, al-Ghazali realized what contradiction is produced by applying Aristotle's logic to Islamic issues, especially theological ones.

What we can therefore conclude from our discussion in previous chapters is that Muslim theorists did not accept Aristotelian logic. Ibn Taymiyya explains this precisely: 'Muslim theorists have always criticized the method of the logicians and confirmed what is in it of defect, stammering, deficiency of intellect, and incapacity of speech, and they confirmed that it is closer to corrupting rational and linguistic logic than to rectifying it. And they are not satisfied to adopt it in their reasoning and debates, neither with those they befriend nor with those they oppose.' And alongside this, they established a logic contrary to Aristotelian logic. Then came Abu Hamid al-Ghazali and called for mixing logic with Islamic sciences: 'Indeed, its use increased in the time of Abu Hamid, for he introduced a preface on Greek logic in the beginning of his book al-Mustasfa, and claimed that he does not trust the knowledge of one who does not know logic, and he composed on it Mi'yar al-Ilm and Mihakk al-Nazar, and he composed a book called al-Qistas al-Mustaqim, in which he mentioned five balances: the three categoricals, the conditional conjunctive, and the conditional disjunctive, and he transformed their expressions into examples taken from speech.'
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is a historical analysis of the Islamic critique of Aristotelian logic, focusing on al-Ghazali and Ibn Taymiyya. The author begins by noting that al-Ghazali, after reviewing the methods of the Batinites, philosophers, and Sufis, presents a new opinion that overturns his previous views. Al-Ghazali initially argues that logic per se is neutral: it is merely the study of proofs, syllogisms, conditions of demonstration, and definitions, and it is not contrary to religion; indeed, it is similar to the methods of Muslim theologians except for terminology and thoroughness. He says that denying logic only reflects badly on the denier's intellect and religion. However, al-Ghazali then identifies two fundamental flaws in Aristotelian logic. First, the philosophical demonstration (burhan) fails to achieve certainty when applied to metaphysical (ilāhiyyāt) matters. The logicians set strict conditions for certainty but fail to meet them in theology, being extremely lax. This led Muslims to criticize Aristotle's logic because it was linked to his metaphysics, which contradict Islamic beliefs; the faulty rules of logic were blamed for this contradiction. Second, al-Ghazali warns that someone impressed by logic may accept the heretical doctrines (kufriyyāt) transmitted from the philosophers, thinking they are supported by demonstrations, and thus hasten to unbelief. Al-Ghazali's critique prompts Ibn Rushd to defend Aristotle by insisting that the metaphysical conclusions are derived from logical law, which al-Ghazali himself acknowledged as irrefutable. The author then concludes that al-Ghazali abandoned his earlier view of logic as a universal method for certainty and turned to Sufi unveiling (kashf). The passage then shifts to Ibn Taymiyya, who confirms that Muslim scholars always criticized Aristotelian logic for its defects and established an alternative logic. Ibn Taymiyya notes that al-Ghazali later popularized mixing Greek logic with Islamic sciences by introducing it in his legal theory work al-Mustasfa and writing other works on logic.

Key Terms
منطق
برهان
يقين
كشف صوفي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Ghazali, why does demonstration fail in theology?
A) The logicians do not fulfill the conditions they set for certainty.
B) Theology is inherently uncertain.
C) Muslim theologians are not intelligent enough.
D) Logic is forbidden in Islam.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali's twofold critique shows that logic's failure is both technical (lack of rigor) and practical (leading astray).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the first cause, provide an example from Islamic theology where a philosophical demonstration might be considered lax (e.g., the proof of the existence of God from motion).
?
For the second cause, explain how a student might be misled by the apparent rigor of a syllogism that actually contains a heretical conclusion.

Ibn Taymiyya's Support for the Rejection
Ibn Taymiyya's support for the rejection and critique of Ghazali's mixing of logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to articulate Ibn Taymiyya's position on Aristotelian logic and its relationship to al-Ghazali's critique.

Core Idea
Muslim scholars rejected Greek logic and built their own.

Ibn Taymiyya supports the overall rejection of Aristotelian logic by Muslim theorists, emphasizing its deficiencies and the establishment of an alternative logic. He also notes that al-Ghazali, despite his critique, actually promoted the mixing of logic with Islamic sciences, which Ibn Taymiyya sees as problematic.

Guided Path
1
Find Ibn Taymiyya's statement
Locate the passage: 'Muslim theorists have always criticized the method of the logicians...'
2
Note his characterization of logic
He says it contains defect, stammering, deficiency of intellect, and is closer to corrupting logic than rectifying it.
3
Observe the connection to al-Ghazali
Ibn Taymiyya mentions that al-Ghazali introduced Greek logic into Islamic sciences, thus popularizing it despite his own critique.

النظر في طرق الأدلة والمقاييس وشروط مقدمات البرهان وكيفية تركيبها وشروط الحد الصحيح وكيفية تركيبه وأن العلم إما تصور وسبيل معرفته الحد وإما تصديق وسبيل معرفته البرهان . وليس في هذا ما ينبغي أن ينكر . Demonstration -- Burhan
intermediate

The Condition of Nafy al-Muzahim in Causal Inference
Explains the logical condition of excluding competitors in establishing a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why nafy al-muzahim is necessary for causal inference and distinguish between probable and certain causal claims in Islamic jurisprudence.

Core Idea
Without excluding competitors, no cause can be uniquely attributed.

In this passage, we learn a crucial logical condition for causal reasoning in usul al-fiqh. The author explains that if an effect is associated with multiple possible causes, we cannot attribute it to any one without eliminating the others. This condition, known as nafy al-muzahim, underpins the validity of methods like correlation and suitability. Let's explore how this works step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem of competing causes
If an effect appears with two things, neither can be claimed as the sole cause without further evidence. Why is that? Without exclusion, attribution is arbitrary.
This is the core logical problem: multiple candidates for causality.
2
Identify the methods jurists use
Jurists use dawaran (correlation) and munasaba (suitability), but these only work if competitors are excluded. They require nafy al-muzahim to be valid.
3
Learn how sabr wa taqsim helps
Sabr wa taqsim (trial and division) is the method of dividing and eliminating alternatives until only one remains. It is the practical tool for achieving nafy al-muzahim.
This method is fundamental in usul al-fiqh.
4
Distinguish between probable and certain causality
The certainty of nafy al-muzahim determines the certainty of the causal belief. If the negation is probable, causality is probable; if certain, causality is certain.
The passage explicitly states this epistemological gradation.

Source Text

ان ينفي المزاحم « وإلا فمتى حصل الأثر مقروناً بأمرين ، لم تكن إضافته إلى أحدهما دون الآخر بأولى من العكس ، ومن إضافته إلى كليهما ، وما يحتج به الفقهاء في إثبات كون الوصف علة للحكم من دوران ومناسبة ، وغير ذلك إنما يفيد المقصود مع نفي المزاحم وذلك يعلم بالسبر والتقسيم » فان كان نفي المزاحم ظنياً ، كان اعتقاد العلية ظنياً ، وإن كان قطعياً كان الاعتقاد قطعياً ، إذ كان قاطعاً فان الحكم لا بد من علة ، وقاطعاً بأنه لا يصح للعلة إلا الوصف الفلاني » ويرى ابن تيمية : أنه يدخل في هذا الباب القضايا العادية ـ أي التي تجري على مجرد العادة من قضايا الطب وغيرها من قضايا العلوم التجريبية .
That the competing factor be negated (nafy al-muzahim). Otherwise, if the effect is associated with two things, its attribution to one of them rather than the other is not more probable than the opposite, nor than its attribution to both. And what the jurists use to prove that a description is the cause (illa) of a ruling—such as correlation (dawaran), suitability (munasaba), and other methods—only serves the purpose when the competing factor is negated, and that is known through trial and division (sabr wa taqsim). So if the negation of the competing factor is probable, then the belief in causality is probable; and if it is certain, then the belief is certain, since it is certain that a ruling must have a cause, and certain that no description other than that specific one can be the cause. Ibn Taymiyya holds that this category includes regular empirical propositions—i.e., those that occur merely by habit, such as the propositions of medicine and other experimental sciences.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage explains the logical condition for establishing a causal relationship between a description (wasf) and a ruling (hukm) in Islamic jurisprudence, known as 'nafy al-muzahim' (negation of the competing factor). The author begins by stating that if an effect is associated with two possible causes, attribution to one is not more valid than to the other or to both. Therefore, to prove that a specific description is the cause, one must eliminate all other possibilities. The jurists use methods like dawaran (correlation), munasaba (suitability), and sabr wa taqsim (trial and division) to do this, but these only work if the competing factors are excluded. The passage then distinguishes between probable and certain negation: if the negation is probable, the causal belief is probable; if certain, the belief is certain. Ibn Taymiyya extends this method to cover regular empirical propositions from medicine and other experimental sciences, which are based on habit. This shows the connection between logical causal inference and empirical science in Ibn Taymiyya's thought.

Key Terms
نفي المزاحم
السبر والتقسيم
الدوران
المناسبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The negation of the competing factor must be certain.
B) The cause must be observed repeatedly.
C) The effect must be produced experimentally.
D) The jurists must agree on the cause.

Key Takeaway
Nafy al-muzahim is the condition that transforms probabilistic causal inference into certain knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Take two possible causes for a ruling given in a fiqh text (e.g., prohibition of wine: intoxicating quality vs. redness). Explain how you would apply sabr wa taqsim to determine which one is the actual illa.

Ibn Taymiyya's Extension to Empirical Sciences
Extends the condition to empirical sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the condition of nafy al-muzahim to empirical claims and evaluate the certainty of scientific knowledge according to Ibn Taymiyya.

Core Idea
Empirical science requires excluding competitors just like jurisprudence.

Ibn Taymiyya makes a profound contribution by extending the logical condition of nafy al-muzahim from jurisprudence to empirical sciences like medicine. He argues that regular empirical propositions are analogous to juristic causal claims—they are based on habit and require the exclusion of competing factors. This bridges the gap between logical inference and empirical observation.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ibn Taymiyya's claim
He says that the condition of nafy al-muzahim applies to regular empirical propositions (al-qadaya al-'adiyya).
These are propositions based on habit, not necessary connections.
2
Understand 'regular empirical propositions'
These are propositions from medicine and other experimental sciences that are based on mere habit, e.g., 'this herb cures fever' based on repeated observation.
Ibn Taymiyya uses 'habit' in a Humean sense before Hume.
3
Analyze the analogy with juristic reasoning
Just as a jurist must exclude competing causes (muzahimat) to establish a legal cause, a scientist must exclude alternative explanations to establish an empirical cause.
4
Consider the epistemological implication
If nafy al-muzahim is only probable, empirical knowledge is probable; if it becomes certain (through exhaustive elimination), empirical knowledge could be certain. But habitual propositions are typically probable.
This shows that Ibn Taymiyya was aware of the probabilistic nature of empirical science.

Critique of Universal Propositions in Demonstration
Author critiques the requirement of universal propositions in demonstration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's conditional argument against the necessity of universal premises in demonstration and evaluate its validity.

Core Idea
Universal premises in demonstration lead to circularity or regress — they are epistemically useless.

In this passage, the author targets a cornerstone of Aristotelian logic: the claim that every demonstration (burhan) must contain a universal premise. He argues that this requirement is either redundant or leads to a logical impasse. By breaking down his conditional reasoning, we will see why the universal premise fails to provide the certainty it promises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
The author says: 'every demonstration requires a universal premise.' This is the claim under critique. Find it in the Arabic: 'ولا بد في البرهان من قضية كلية'.
2
Distinguish the two epistemic statuses
The author says the universal premise must be known to be universal. That knowledge is either intuitive (badihi) or theoretical (nazari). Locate these terms in the passage.
3
Trace the consequences of each status
If intuitive, then knowledge of particulars is more obvious, making demonstration unnecessary. If theoretical, it needs proof, leading to circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul). Both are invalid. Identify the Arabic phrases for these consequences.
This is the core of the critique: both options reveal the universal premise as useless or self-defeating.
4
Evaluate the author's conclusion
The author concludes that the universal premise has no benefit (لا فائدة). Assess whether this follows logically. Consider a possible counterargument from a defender of Aristotle. Does the critique assume something about the relationship between universal and particular knowledge?
This step requires critical thinking about the argument's validity and underlying assumptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث من النقد . نقد القضية الكلية . العلوم اليقينية النظرية لا يحصل عليها إلا بالبرهان ولا بد في البرهان من قضية كلية . القضية الكلية هي عنصر البرهان الجوهري ، فلا يمكن الإنتاج في أي قياس عن جزئيتين . ولكن ليست لهذه القضية الكلية ثمة فائدة . لأنه إذا كان لا بد في كل برهان من قضية كلية » ، فيجب أن نعلم كونها كلية . فإذا كان العلم بهذه القضية بديهياً ، كان العلم ببديهية أفرادها بطريق الأولى . أما إذا كان نظرياً احتاج إلى علم بديهي وهذا يؤدي إلى الدور المنطقي أو التسلسل وكلاهما باطل . ويحدث هذا في جميع القضايا الكلية البديهية التي يجعلها المناطقة مبادىء البرهان ويسمونها « الواجب قبولها »(۱) . وكل برهان تكون فيه هذه القضايا مقدمة كبرى ، يمكن العلم بالنتيجة بدون توسط تلك القضية وما من قضية من هذه القضايا الكلية التي تجعل مقدمة في البرهان ، إلا والعلم بالنتيجة ممكن بدون توسط ذلك البرهان ـ بل هو الواقع كثيراً » فالإنسان يعلم
The third aspect of the critique: Critique of the universal proposition. Theoretical certain sciences are only obtained by demonstration, and demonstration necessarily requires a universal proposition. The universal proposition is the essential element of demonstration, so it is impossible to produce a conclusion in any syllogism from two particulars. But this universal proposition has no benefit. Because if every demonstration requires a universal proposition, then we must know it to be universal. If the knowledge of this proposition is intuitive, then knowledge of the intuitiveness of its individuals is a fortiori. But if it is theoretical, it needs intuitive knowledge, and this leads to logical circularity or infinite regress, both of which are invalid. And this occurs in all the universal intuitive propositions that the logicians make as principles of demonstration and call them 'the necessary-to-accept.' And every demonstration that has these propositions as a major premise, it is possible to know the conclusion without the mediation of that proposition. And there is no such universal proposition that is made a premise in a demonstration except that knowledge of the conclusion is possible without the mediation of that demonstration—indeed that is often the actual case. For a person knows
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage presents the third critique of Aristotelian logic: the critique of the universal proposition (al-qadiyya al-kulliyya) in demonstration. The author states the standard Aristotelian position: certain theoretical sciences are only attained through demonstration (burhan), and demonstration necessarily requires a universal premise. This universal premise is the essential component, as no syllogism can yield a conclusion from two particular premises. However, the author argues that this universal premise is ultimately useless. The reasoning is conditional: if every demonstration requires a universal premise, then we must know that premise is universal. That knowledge is either intuitive (badihi) or theoretical (nazari). If it is intuitive, then the knowledge of its particulars is even more obvious (by first priority), making the demonstration unnecessary. If it is theoretical, it itself requires proof, leading to either circularity (al-dawr al-mantiqi) or infinite regress (al-tasalsul), both of which are invalid. The author extends this to the so-called 'necessary-to-accept' (al-wajib qabuluha) premises that logicians treat as starting points of demonstration. Furthermore, even in demonstrations that use such universal propositions as major premises, the conclusion can be known without the mediation of that proposition—indeed, this is often the case. Thus, the universal premise adds no epistemic benefit. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the argument is clear: the requirement of universal propositions in demonstration is problematic and fails to provide the certainty claimed.

Key Terms
قضية كلية
برهان
دور منطقي
تسلسل

Key Takeaway
The requirement of universal premises in demonstration is epistemically problematic: it either makes demonstration redundant or leads to circularity/regress, undermining the claim of demonstrative certainty.
