Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Demonstration: Particularity of Axioms
Ibn Taymiyya's critique of demonstration and its foundations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that the basic axioms of demonstration are empirically derived from particular judgments and evaluate its implications for metaphysical knowledge.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya argued that demonstration's axioms are not rational but empirical—particular before universal.

The passage explores Ibn Taymiyya's radical critique that the fundamental axioms of Aristotelian logic are not self-evident postulates but empirical generalizations. He challenges the entire edifice of demonstrative knowledge by asserting that universal propositions are merely enumerations of particulars, making demonstration circular. This critique, which the author traces back to Sextus Empiricus and later to John Stuart Mill, is uniquely tied to Ibn Taymiyya's theological concern: that demonstration cannot yield knowledge of the Creator. We will unpack his key arguments and assess their validity.

Guided Path
1
State the logicians' claim being attacked
Identify the Aristotelian claim that the basic axioms of demonstration (e.g., law of non-contradiction) are self-evident rational truths known prior to any experience, and that demonstration provides certain, new knowledge from these axioms.
The passage contrasts this with Ibn Taymiyya's empirical approach.
2
Follow Ibn Taymiyya's deconstruction
Notice how Ibn Taymiyya uses examples of particular judgments known directly, such as 'this one is half of these two' or 'this object cannot be both existent and non-existent', to show that we do not need universal premises. He argues that these particular judgments are the basis from which we later generalize universal laws.
The passage states: 'فبديهيات البرهان الأساسية أساسها جزئي لا كلي' – the basic axioms have a particular, not universal, basis.
3
Identify his alternative
Ibn Taymiyya replaces the classical view with the claim that universal propositions are merely enumerations of particulars, and demonstration is circular – it only reveals what is already implicit in the premises. True knowledge must come from direct sensory experience or revelation, not from syllogistic reasoning.
He does not reject all reasoning but the claim that Aristotelian demonstration yields certain metaphysical truths.
4
Evaluate the critique's internal validity
Consider whether Ibn Taymiyya's own position is self-defeating. He uses universal claims (e.g., 'all axioms are empirical') to attack universals. Some argue this is a performative contradiction. Also, note that his critique leads to skepticism about all general knowledge, which may conflict with his own theological commitments.
The passage notes that Ibn Taymiyya adopted arguments of skeptics like Sextus Empiricus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن هذا الواحد نصف هذين الأثنين من غير استدلال على ذلك بالقضية الكلية ، وأن هذا العين لا يكون موجوداً معدوماً كما يعلم العين الآخر ، ولا يحتاج ذلك إلى أن يستدل عليه بأن كل شيء لا يكون موجوداً معدوماً كما يعلم العين الآخر . ولا يحتاج ذلك إلى أن يستدل عليه بأن كل شيء لا يكون موجوداً معدوماً معاً(١). وكذلك الأمر في حكم التضاد العام وحكم التناقض كلها أحكام جزئية . ثم قد ننتقل من هذه الأحكام الجزئية إلى الحكم العام . فبديهيات البرهان الأساسية أساسها جزئي لا كلي ، محسوسي تجريبي لا عقلي : وهذا نقد قاس لأسس المنطق الأرسططاليسي . وقد سبق ابن تيمية - المنطقي الانجليزي بوزانكيت وغيره من مناطقة - حين أعلنوا أن بديهيات البرهان الأساسية أي قوانين الفكر المشهورة ليست بديهيات وإنما هي مسلمات تستمد من الخبرة والتجربة فالإنسان إذن يتوصل إلى القضية الجزئية قبل التوصل إلى القضية الكلية . والقضية الكلية ليست إلا تعداداً لأفراد جزئية مندرجة تحتها . والبرهان لا يعطي شيئاً جديداً على الإطلاق ، بل فيه مصادرة على المطلوب . والغريب أن ابن تيمية لم يذكر هذا التعبير الأخير الذي عرفته مدرسة الشراح الإسلاميين تمام المعرفة . من أين استمد ابن تيمية هذا النقد الاسمي والذي يدرجه في قائمة الفلاسفة الإسميين ثم إننا نعلم أنه قد سبقه فيه سكستوس أمبريقوس - ووصل إلى مدرسة الشراح الإسلاميين - كما قلنا - واستعاده جون استوارت مل في العصور الحديثة ، ووجهه نحو القياس الأرسططاليسي أيضاً . ونحن لا نشك مطلقاً في أن ابن تيمية كما تأثر جون أستيوارت مل بسكستوس أمبريقوس والفكر الإنساني يحيا في حلقة متصلة . إنه يستعيد في كل آن مشاكل الفلسفة . أي يستعيدها في جوهرها . ولكنه يبعث فيها نوعاً من الجدة والدقة ويضفي عليها روحاً جديدة ، فكأنها تحيا في حاضر مستمر . وقد اصطنع ابن تيمية حجج الشكاك ، وخاصة هنا . كما اصطنعها من بعده مل - غير أنه ربطها بمذهبه العام ثم استخرج منها النتيجة التي سنراها فيما بعد . لم يكتف ابن تيمية بالقول بأن في كل برهان يشمل قضية كلية مصادرة على المطلوب بل حاول ما وسعه الجهد أن يحطم صرح العلم الأرسططاليسي اليوناني كله . فقال أن العلم الجزئي ليس علماً على الأطلاق وبأن العلم الحقيقي هو الكلي اليقيني الذي نصل إليه بالبرهان(۱) لأن النتائج المعلومة بالبرهان لا تكون إلا كلية . فحاول أن يطبق قواعد البرهان على الإلهيات ، فلم يصل إلى شيء على الإطلاق . لأن البرهان لا يفيد إلا العلم بالكليات ، والكليات لا تكون إلا في الأذهان لا في الأعيان ، وهي غير موجودة في الواقع ، فلن نصل إذن إلى شيء موجود . يقرر ابن تيمية أنه إذا كان البرهان لا يفيد إلا العلم بالكليات ، والكليات إنما تكون في الأذهان لا في الأعيان ، وليس في الخارج إلا وجود معين، لم يعلم بالبرهان شيء من المعينات فلا يعلم به موجود أصلاً . بل إنما يعلم به أمور مقدرة في الأذهان ، لا تتحقق في الخارج أصلاً . يقول ابن تيمية : وأي كمال للنفس في مجرد تصور هذه الأمور العامة الكلية إذا لم تتصور أعيان الموجودات المعينة الجزئية . وأي علم في هذا برب العالمين الذي لا تكمل النفوس إلا بمعرفته وعبادته محبة وذلاً(۲). إن مبحث الوجود ولواحقه ـ وهو مبحث العلم الأعلى ـ لا يمكن التوصل إليه بالكليات . ويتبين هذا في بحثنا أو في تطبيقنا للبرهان على تلك المباحث . أما الوجود فهو أمر ظاهر ، فلا حاجة إذن إلى محاولة حده أو البرهنة عليه . ويقرر ابن تيمية أن المناطقة أنفسهم يسلمون بهذا . فليس هو إذن غاية الإستدلال العقلي ولكن هي أقسامه ، ونفس أقسامه إلى واجب وممكن وجوهر
That this one is half of these two without inferring that from the universal proposition, and that this individual cannot be existent and non-existent just as the other individual is known, and that does not need to be inferred by saying that everything cannot be existent and non-existent just as the other individual is known. And that does not need to be inferred by saying that everything cannot be existent and non-existent together. Similarly, the case regarding the judgment of general opposition and the judgment of contradiction are all particular judgments. Then we may move from these particular judgments to the general judgment. So the basic axioms of demonstration have a particular basis, not a universal one; they are sensory and empirical, not rational: and this is a harsh critique of the foundations of Aristotelian logic. And Ibn Taymiyya preceded the English logician Bosanquet and other logicians when they declared that the basic axioms of demonstration, i.e., the famous laws of thought, are not axioms but rather postulates derived from experience and experiment. Thus man arrives at the particular proposition before arriving at the universal proposition. And the universal proposition is nothing but an enumeration of particular individuals subsumed under it. And demonstration gives absolutely nothing new, rather it contains a begging of the question. And it is strange that Ibn Taymiyya did not mention this last expression that the school of Islamic commentators knew fully. Where did Ibn Taymiyya derive this nominalist critique which he includes in the list of nominalist philosophers? Then we know that Sextus Empiricus preceded him in it - and it reached the school of Islamic commentators - as we said - and John Stuart Mill revived it in modern times, and directed it against Aristotelian syllogism as well. And we have absolutely no doubt that Ibn Taymiyya, just as John Stuart Mill was influenced by Sextus Empiricus, and human thought lives in a continuous circle. He revives anew the problems of philosophy. He revives them in their essence. But he breathes into them a kind of novelty and precision and gives them a new spirit, as if they live in a continuous present. And Ibn Taymiyya adopted the arguments of the skeptics, especially here. Just as Mill after him adopted them - except that he linked them to his general doctrine and then extracted from them the result that we will see later. Ibn Taymiyya was not content with saying that every demonstration that includes a universal proposition contains a begging of the question, but he tried as much as he could to destroy the entire edifice of Greek Aristotelian science. So he said that particular knowledge is not knowledge at all, and that true knowledge is the universal certain knowledge that we reach by demonstration, because the results known by demonstration are only universal. So he tried to apply the rules of demonstration to metaphysics, and he reached absolutely nothing. Because demonstration only yields knowledge of universals, and universals are only in the minds, not in external objects, and they do not exist in reality; so we will not reach anything existent. Ibn Taymiyya states that if demonstration only yields knowledge of universals, and universals are only in the minds, not in external objects, and there is nothing in the external world except specific existence, then nothing specific is known by demonstration, so no existent is known by it at all. Rather, only matters are known that are estimated in the mind, not realized externally at all. Ibn Taymiyya says: 'And what perfection is there for the soul in merely conceiving these general universal matters if it does not conceive the particular individual existents? And what knowledge is there in this of the Lord of the Worlds, whose knowledge and worship with love and humility perfect souls?' The topic of existence and its concomitants - which is the topic of the highest science - cannot be reached through universals. This is evident in our research or in our application of demonstration to those topics. As for existence, it is obvious; so there is no need to attempt to define it or demonstrate it. And Ibn Taymiyya states that the logicians themselves concede this. So it is not the goal of rational inference, but rather its divisions, and the division of it into necessary and possible and substance
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of Ibn Taymiyya's attack on Aristotelian logic, specifically on demonstration (burhan) and its foundations. The author begins by illustrating particular judgments that are known without universal premises, such as 'this one is half of these two' or that a thing cannot be both existent and non-existent. These are known directly, without needing to cite a universal law. From such particular judgments, we may derive general ones. Thus, the basic axioms of demonstration are particular, empirical, not rational. This is a harsh critique of Aristotelian logic. The author notes that Ibn Taymiyya anticipated later logicians like Bosanquet in declaring that the so-called 'axioms' of thought are not self-evident but empirical postulates. Consequently, the universal proposition is just an enumeration of particulars, and demonstration only gives what is already known, i.e., it is circular (musadara 'ala al-matlub). The author finds it odd that Ibn Taymiyya did not mention this circularity explicitly, though the Islamic commentators knew it well. The author then traces Ibn Taymiyya's skepticism to Sextus Empiricus, and notes that John Stuart Mill later revived the same critique. However, Ibn Taymiyya linked these skeptical arguments to his own theological doctrine. The critique intensifies: Ibn Taymiyya tried to destroy the entire Aristotelian science. He argued that true knowledge is universal and certain, but demonstration only yields universals. When applied to metaphysics (ilahiyyat), demonstration fails because universals exist only in the mind, not in external reality. Therefore, demonstration cannot know any particular existent, let alone the Creator. Ibn Taymiyya mocks the idea of perfecting the soul through bare universals if one does not know concrete particular beings, especially the Lord. Finally, the passage notes that existence itself is self-evident and needs no demonstration; logicians admit this. Thus, the goal of reasoning is not to prove existence but to divide it into necessary, possible, etc.

Key Terms
برهان
قضية كلية
قضية جزئية
بديهيات
مصادرة على المطلوب

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique reduces the axioms of demonstration to empirical generalizations from particulars, arguing that demonstration is therefore circular and cannot yield new or certain metaphysical knowledge, especially of God.

Demonstration -- Burhan
intermediate
10 min
Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Demonstration for Particulars
Ibn Taymiyya argues that demonstration (burhan) cannot know particular beings like the Necessary Being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's critique of demonstration (burhan) as a tool for knowing particular existents, and identify the epistemological grounds of his objection.

Core Idea
Demonstration yields only universal knowledge; particular entities like God require a different epistemology.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the classical claim that burhan (demonstrative syllogism) is the highest form of certain knowledge. He argues that since demonstration operates through universal premises, it cannot grasp unique, particular beings such as the Necessary Being (wajib al-wujud). This lesson unpacks his critique and its implications for Islamic theology and philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target doctrine
Locate the exact claim Ibn Taymiyya is attacking: that demonstration can prove the existence of particular beings, especially the Necessary Being, the ten intellects, spheres, and elements.
The target is the philosophers' confidence in burhan for all existents.
2
Extract the core objection
Ibn Taymiyya's key premise: demonstration proceeds from universal premises, but the Necessary Being is a unique particular (i.e., its conception prevents sharing). Therefore, demonstration cannot yield knowledge of it.
This is an epistemological objection: the scope of burhan is limited to universals.
3
Identify the grounds of the objection
Classify the objection: it is epistemological (limitation of burhan) and metaphysical (rejection of abstract essences as real). Ibn Taymiyya also argues that contingent beings change, so universal premises about them lack permanence.
He contrasts his view with the philosophers' reliance on essences (mahiyyat mujarrada) like 'man as such', which he deems mental constructs.
4
Evaluate the internal validity
Check if Ibn Taymiyya's own argument is logically sound. He assumes that burhan only gives universals, and the Necessary Being is not a universal. The conclusion follows if the premise holds.
This is a valid categorical syllogism in the first figure: All burhan yields universal knowledge; the Necessary Being is not universal; therefore, burhan cannot know the Necessary Being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعرض علة ومعلول وقديم وحادث أخص من مسمى الوجود « وليس مجرد انقسام الأمر العام إلى أقسام ، بدون أن نتوصل إلى معرفة الأقسام ، ما يقتضي علماً كلياً عظيماً أعلى من تصور الوجود » ويتبين هذا من البحث في القسمين الرئيسيين للوجود وهما واجب الوجود وممكن الوجود .
أما واجب الوجود : فهو أكمل الموجودات . ووجوده معين غير كلي لأن الكلي لا يمنع تصوره من وقوع الشركة فيه . وواجب الوجود يمنع تصوره من وقوع الشركة فيه . إذن فلن نصل بواسطة البرهان إلى معرفة واجب الوجود ولا العقول العشرة ولا الأفلاك ولا العناصر . فكلها ، جواهر معينة لا أمور كلية عامة ، أو بمعنى آخر يقرر ابن تيمية أنه لا يصل الفلاسفة إلى جملة الموجودات عندهم ، ولن يفيد البرهان إذن علماً حقيقياً .
أما الأشياء الممكنة الوجود فالبرهان القياسي لا يفيد أموراً كلية « واجبة البقاء فيها » لأن تلك الممكنات لا تستمر على حالة واحدة ، بل هي قابلة للتغير والاستحالة .
طبق ابن تيمية فكرة عدم إفادة البرهان العلم إلا بالكليات على ثلاثة مباحث في الإلهيات ، وقسميه - واجب الوجود وممكن الوجود : وفي المباحث الثلاثة لم ينتج البرهان شيئاً .
وإذا كان البرهان نفسه المستند إلى قضية كلية لن تؤدي إلى إثبات واجب الوجود وممكن الوجود ، وهما أعيان معينة موجودة في الواقع ، فإنه سيستند عليه من ناحية أخرى آراء اليونان الميتافيزيقية كالقول بوجودات مطلقة لا وجود لها ولا اثبات في الواقع . وأهم تلك الموجودات هي الماهيات المجردة من حيث هي هي . ولكن ابن تيمية يرى أن هذه الموجودات لا وجود لها في الحقيقة ، ليس في الخارج الإنسان من حيث هو ولا الوجود من حيث هو ولا السواد من حيث هو . إن ابن تيمية يشير في وضوح إلى نظرية المثل عند أفلاطون ، بل إنه يقرر أن القول بوجود تلك الماهيات يشبه القول بوجود تلك المثل الأفلاطونية أو الأعداد المجردة الفيثاغورية . وليس لهذه ولا لتلك وجود خارجي ولكن هي أمور تقديرية في الذهن . أليس القول ـ وهذا ما لم يصرح به ابن تيمية في هذا الموضع ـ بوجود تلك الماهيات القديمة أو تلك المثل هو القول بتعدد القدماء . إن ابن تيمية تسيطر عليه فكرته العامة عن استناد المنطق إلى إلهيات اليونان ، وعن هذه الفكرة يصدر هنا . ولم يلجأ إلى حجج أرسطو في نقد نظرية المثل .
And the presentation of cause and effect and the eternal and the originated is more specific than the name of existence. 'It is not merely the division of the general matter into parts, without our reaching knowledge of the parts, which necessitates a great universal knowledge higher than the conception of existence.' And this is clarified by research into the two principal divisions of existence, which are the Necessary Being and the contingent being. As for the Necessary Being: He is the most perfect of existents. His existence is particular, not universal, because the universal does not prevent its conception from sharing in it. And the Necessary Being prevents its conception from sharing in it. Therefore, we will not arrive through demonstration at knowledge of the Necessary Being, nor the ten intellects, nor the spheres, nor the elements. For all of them are particular substances, not universal general matters; or in other words, Ibn Taymiyyah affirms that the philosophers do not attain the totality of existents according to them, and demonstration will not yield true knowledge. As for contingent things, syllogistic demonstration does not yield universal matters 'whose permanence is necessary in them' because those contingents do not continue in one state, but are subject to change and transformation. Ibn Taymiyyah applied the idea that demonstration only yields knowledge of universals to three topics in metaphysics, and its two divisions - the Necessary Being and the contingent being: in the three topics, demonstration produced nothing. And if demonstration itself, based on a universal proposition, will not lead to the proof of the Necessary Being and the contingent being, while they are particular entities existing in reality, then on the other hand it will rely on the metaphysical opinions of the Greeks, such as the claim of absolute existences that have no existence or proof in reality. And the most important of those existences are the abstract essences considered as such. But Ibn Taymiyyah holds that these existences have no existence in reality; in the external world there is not 'man as such' nor 'existence as such' nor 'blackness as such'. Ibn Taymiyyah clearly refers to Plato's theory of Forms, and indeed he asserts that the claim of the existence of those essences resembles the claim of the existence of those Platonic Forms or the abstract Pythagorean numbers. Neither these nor those have external existence, but they are estimative matters in the mind. Is not the claim - and Ibn Taymiyyah did not state this explicitly in this place - of the existence of those eternal essences or those Forms the claim of the multiplicity of the eternal? Ibn Taymiyyah is dominated by his general idea about the dependence of logic on Greek theology, and from this idea he proceeds here. And he did not resort to Aristotle's arguments in criticizing the theory of Forms.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the demonstrative syllogism (burhan) as a tool for acquiring knowledge of particular existents. The author opens by stating that cause-effect and eternal-originated are more specific than the general notion of existence. The passage then explains that the necessary being (wajib al-wujud) is a particular entity, not a universal, because a universal concept can be shared by many individuals, whereas the necessary being is unique. Therefore, demonstration, which proceeds from universal premises, cannot yield knowledge of the necessary being, nor of the ten intellects, spheres, or elements, as these are all particular substances. Similarly, contingent beings are always changing, so universals derived from them cannot be permanent. Ibn Taymiyya applies this critique to three metaphysical inquiries, concluding that demonstration fails in all. He further argues that the philosophers, unable to prove particular beings through demonstration, instead posit abstract essences (mahiyyat mujarrada) that have no external reality—like 'man as such'—and compares these to Plato's Forms and Pythagorean numbers. He asserts that these are merely mental constructs. The passage ends with Ibn Taymiyya's implicit charge that believing in such eternal essences implies a belief in multiple eternal entities, a form of shirk, but notes that he does not rely on Aristotle's refutation of the Forms.

كلي
واجب الوجود
ماهيات مجردة

Demonstration -- Burhan
advanced

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Certain Materials of Demonstration
Ibn Taymiyya argues that logicians' certain premises are neither universal nor certain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the five certain materials and explain Ibn Taymiyya's specific objection to its universality and certainty.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya inverts the logicians' ranking: sensibles are most particular, not universal.

The logicians claimed that demonstration (burhān) rests on certain universal premises called 'mawād al-burhān'—sensibles, inner intuitions, experiments, tawātur reports, and intuitions. Ibn Taymiyya argues that each of these is particular, not universal, and thus cannot ground certain knowledge. In this lesson, you will trace his critique and see how he turns the logicians' own criteria against them.

Guided Path
1
List the five certain materials
From the Arabic text, identify each of the five categories that logicians consider certain and universal: al-ḥissiyyāt, al-wijdāniyyāt, al-mujarrabāt, al-mutawātirāt, al-ḥadsiyyāt.
These correspond to sensibles, inner intuitions, experimental premises, successively transmitted reports, and intuitive premises.
2
Examine Ibn Taymiyya's objection to sensibles
He argues that sense perception only gives particulars, not universals. People may agree on the genus (e.g., sweetness) but differ on the specific instance (e.g., this taste). Therefore, sensibles cannot serve as universal premises.
Ibn Taymiyya says: 'What these people smell, taste, and touch is not what those people smell, taste, and touch, even though the genus is one.'
3
Analyze his treatment of inner intuitions and experiments
Inner intuitions (wijdāniyyāt) like pain or pleasure are completely private—no two people share the exact same feeling. Experiments (mujarrabāt) are based on particular observations; they cannot be universal either.
Ibn Taymiyya notes that disagreement in inner intuitions is even greater than in sensibles.
4
Uncover the inversion
Logicians treat sensibles as universal but mutawātirāt as particular to those who know them. Ibn Taymiyya inverts this: sensibles are actually private, while mutawātirāt are transmitted to many, making them more shareable. So the logicians' hierarchy is reversed.
This is a key move: Ibn Taymiyya argues that the closer a material is to certainty, the more it relies on transmission, not sense.
5
Apply to philosophical sciences
Ibn Taymiyya traces mathematics to ḥadsiyyāt, and natural sciences and astronomy to mujarrabāt. Since these are not universal or certain, they cannot yield demonstrative certainty. He likens them to khabar wāhid (single-narrator reports) in fiqh.
This shows the practical implication of his critique for disciplines like physics and astronomy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلية كالإنسان الكلي المطلق موجودة في الخارج وجوداً مقارناً للأشخاص، وهذا خطأ لأن الإنسان الكلي المطلق لا وجود له إلا في الذهن ـ إن كان له ثمة وجود ـ نستطيع هنا أن نتبين المذهب الإسمي في أوجه. بعد هذا الإستطراد الطويل في مسائل ميتافيزيقية، يحاول ابن تيمية أن يدلل على أن ما يدعوه المناطقة بالمواد اليقينية ـ التي اعتبرت مقدمات كلية للبرهان ليست كلية ولا يقينية على الاطلاق. وأول تلك المواد الحسيات وهي ليست كلية أصلاً لأن الحس لا يدرك أمراً كلياً، والكليات إنما يدركها العقل وطريق إدراكها ـ كما سنرى بعد قليل ـ قياس التمثيل أو التجربة أو العادة أو الإستقراء الناقص أو هذه الثلاثة أو الأربعة مجتمعة. وفي إيجاز ليست الحسيات كلية، ولا يشارك الناس في إدراكهم لها أو يتشاركون في ادراك شيء معين من الحسيات. قد يحدث اتفاقهم في ادراك جنس الشيء، ولكن يختلفون في عين المدرك. فليس الذي « يشمه هؤلاء ويذوقونه ويلمسونه، هو الذي يشمه ويذوقه ويلمسه هؤلاء، مع أن جنس المشموم أو المذاق أو الملموس واحد. والمادة الثانية من المواد اليقينية: هي الوجدانيات الباطنية، وليس فيها مطلقاً كلي. بل إنها إدراك جزئي لا يتشارك فيه الناس. بل يكاد الاختلاف يكون فيها أشد من الاختلاف في إدراك الحسيات، لأن في إدراك الوجدانيات من الخصوص في المدرك والمدرك ما ليس في الأول. لأنه قد يتفق في الأول التشابه بين الناس في إدراك جنس بعض المحسوسات المنفصلة كالشمس وغيرها بينما لا يتفق الناس أبداً في إدراك آلامهم ولذاتهم. أما المادة الثالثة: فهي المجربات وتستند المجربات إلى التجربة والتجربة لا تقع إلا على شيء معين. أما المادة الرابعة: وهي المتواترات فإنها بتواتر عن مسموع أو عن مرئي وكلامهما معين. أما المادة الخامسة: فهي الحدسيات. والحدسيات لا يمكن اعتبارها كلية يقينية. ولكن على فرض هذا فهي تشبه المجربات. وهناك من المناطقة من يجمع تلك المواد الثلاثة الأخيرة تحت اسم التجريبيات. ويقول ابن تيمية إنه كما أن الناس يتفقون في إحساسهم لأجناس بعض المحسوسات مع اختلافهم في عينها، فإنهم أيضاً يتفقون في معرفة جنس المتواترات أو المجربات، مع اختلافهم في تصور عينها. فمع أنه قد يتواتر لدى قوم أو يجرب ما لم يتواتر عند غيرهم أو يجربوه. فإنهم قد يتفقون في الجنس، ولكن من الغريب عند ابن تيمية أن يقول المناطقة بأن المتواترات والمجربات والحدسيات « يختص بها من علمها » فلا يقيم الإنسان بها الحجة أو البرهان على غيره. فهي ليست حجة على غير أصحابها، بينما يعتبرون الحسيات قضايا كلية. والمسألة عكسية تماماً وقرب هذه المواد من اليقينيات لا يضارعه قرب الحسيات منها. إن المتواترات مثلاً تنقل بواسطة عدد كبير من الناس، وتصل إلى أسماع عدد كبير من الناس أيضاً. أما الحسيات فلا يشترك فيها إلا من أحسها. هل يشترك الناس في تذوقهم للأشياء أو لمسهم لها أو شمها: إن الحسيات في الواقع عند ابن تيمية هي التي يختص بها من علمها. ولا يستطيع انسان أن يقيم البرهان بها على غيره. ولو حاولنا أن نرجع علوم الفلسفة إلى شيء من هذه المواد، لتبين لنا أن العلوم الرياضية تعود إلى الحدسيات، والعلوم العقلية الطبيعية والعلوم الفلكية كعلم الهيئة إلى المجربات. وليس في هذه العلوم الأخيرة شيء يقيني البتة. ولكن تعود إلى تجربة أو تجارب شخصية لأفراد من البشر. ولا تتواتر تلك القضايا تواتراً يقينياً بل يمكن أن تنقل بواسطة ما يسمى في الفقه « خبر الواحد » وإذا ما ذكر أن جماعة لا فرداً واحداً قاموا بعمل تجربة من التجارب، ثم نقلت هذه التجربة من جيل إلى جيل. فغاية ما نستطيع أن نقول إنه تواتر من النوع المعروف « بالتواتر الخاص » ويرى ابن تيمية أنه مع كل هذا، ينكر
The universal, like the absolute universal human, exists externally with a concomitant existence to individuals, and this is wrong because the absolute universal human has no existence except in the mind—if it has any existence at all. Here we can discern the nominalist school in some aspects. After this long digression into metaphysical issues, Ibn Taymiyya attempts to demonstrate that what the logicians call the certain materials—which they considered universal premises for demonstration—are neither universal nor certain at all. The first of those materials is the sensibles, and they are not universal at all because sense does not perceive a universal thing; rather, universals are perceived only by the mind, and the way of perceiving them—as we will see shortly—is analogical reasoning (qiyās al-tamthīl), or experiment, or habit, or incomplete induction, or these three or four combined. In brief, the sensibles are not universal, and people do not share in perceiving them or share in perceiving any particular sensible thing. It may happen that they agree in perceiving the genus of a thing, but they differ in the specific object perceived. So what 'these people smell, taste, and touch is not what those people smell, taste, and touch, even though the genus of what is smelled, tasted, or touched is one.' The second material of the certain materials is the inner intuitions, and there is absolutely no universal in them. Rather, they are a particular perception that people do not share. In fact, the difference in them is almost more severe than the difference in perceiving the sensibles, because in perceiving intuitions there is a specificity in the perceiver and the perceived that is not in the former. For it may happen in the former that there is similarity between people in perceiving the genus of some separate sensibles like the sun and others, whereas people never agree in perceiving their pains and pleasures. As for the third material: it is the experimental premises, and they are based on experiment, and experiment does not occur except on a particular thing. As for the fourth material: it is the mutawātirāt, which are by successive transmission from something heard or seen, and their discourse is particular. As for the fifth material: it is the intuitive premises. The intuitive premises cannot be considered universal and certain. But assuming this, they resemble the experimental premises. And there are those among the logicians who combine these last three materials under the name 'experimentals'. Ibn Taymiyya says that just as people agree in their sensation of the genera of some sensibles while differing in their specific identity, so too they agree in knowing the genus of the mutawātirāt or experimental premises, while differing in their conception of the specific instance. For it may be that something is successively transmitted or experimented upon among one people that is not transmitted or experimented upon among others. They may agree in the genus, but it is strange according to Ibn Taymiyya that the logicians say that the mutawātirāt, experimental premises, and intuitive premises 'are particular to those who know them,' so a person cannot establish an argument or demonstration upon others with them. So they are not an argument for others besides their possessors, while they consider the sensibles to be universal propositions. The matter is completely opposite, and the proximity of these materials to certainties is not matched by the proximity of the sensibles to them. For example, the mutawātirāt are transmitted by a large number of people and reach the ears of a large number of people as well. As for the sensibles, only those who sense them share in them. Do people share in their tasting of things, or touching them, or smelling them? Indeed, the sensibles, in Ibn Taymiyya's view, are what are particular to those who know them. And a person cannot establish a demonstration with them upon others. If we attempted to trace the philosophical sciences back to some of these materials, it would become clear to us that the mathematical sciences go back to the intuitive premises, and the rational natural sciences and the astronomical sciences like astronomy go back to the experimental premises. And there is nothing certain at all in these latter sciences. Rather, they go back to an experiment or personal experiments of individual humans. And those propositions are not transmitted with certain successive transmission; rather, they can be transmitted by what is called in fiqh 'khabar wāhid' (single-narrator report). And if it is mentioned that a group—not a single individual—performed an experiment, and then this experiment was transmitted from generation to generation, the most we can say is that it is a transmission of the type known as 'special successive transmission' (al-tawātur al-khāṣṣ). And Ibn Taymiyya holds that despite all this, he denies.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the logicians' claim that the materials of demonstration (mawād al-burhān) are universal and certain. The author first notes that the nominalist position (al-madhhab al-ismī) can be discerned in Ibn Taymiyya's approach, as he rejects the external existence of universals. Ibn Taymiyya then proceeds to examine each of the five 'certain materials' identified by logicians: sensibles (hissiyyāt), inner intuitions (wijdāniyyāt), experimental premises (mujarrabāt), successively transmitted reports (mutawātirāt), and intuitive premises (hadsiyyāt). For the sensibles, Ibn Taymiyya argues that sense perception is particular, not universal; people may agree on the genus of a sensed object (e.g., sweetness) but differ on the specific instance (e.g., this specific taste). Inner intuitions are even more particular, as no two people share the exact same pain or pleasure. Experimental premises rely on experiment, which is always on a particular thing. Mutawātirāt involve reports of specific events, and hadsiyyāt, if they exist, resemble experimental premises. Ibn Taymiyya highlights a contradiction in the logicians' position: they consider sensibles as universal propositions but claim that mutawātirāt and experimental premises are specific to those who know them. He argues the opposite: sensibles are actually private to the perceiver, while mutawātirāt are shared by many. Finally, the author applies this critique to philosophical sciences: mathematics depends on hadsiyyāt (hence not certain), and natural sciences and astronomy depend on experimental premises, which are no more certain than a single-narrator report (khabar wāhid) in fiqh. Even if an experiment is performed by a group and transmitted across generations, it only reaches the level of 'special tawātur' (al-tawātur al-khāṣṣ), not certain knowledge.

Key Terms
الحسيات
الوجدانيات
المجربات
المتواترات
الحدسيات
البرهان

Ibn Taymiyya's Inversion of Premise Classification
Ibn Taymiyya argues that mutawatirat and mujarrabat are not universal; hissiyat are actually particular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the logicians' claim that mutawatirat and mujarrabat are particular while hissiyat are universal, and evaluate its logical implications.

Core Idea
What logicians call universal (hissiyat) is actually particular; what they call particular (mutawatirat) is more universal.

In Aristotelian logic, certain premises are classified as universal (hissiyat) or particular to those who know them (mutawatirat, mujarrabat). Ibn Taymiyya challenges this classification, arguing that sensibles are deeply personal and cannot be shared as certain knowledge, while transmitted reports are widely accessible. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and its implications for demonstration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Logicians' Classification
Find the logicians' claim that mutawatirat, mujarrabat, and hadsiyat are particular to those who know them and cannot serve as proof for others, while hissiyat are considered universal propositions.
2
Find Ibn Taymiyya's Analogy
Locate where Ibn Taymiyya compares agreement on the genus of sensibles (e.g., taste) with agreement on the genus of mutawatirat, while differing on particular instances.
This analogy is used to show that both types have a similar structure of generality and particularity.
3
Locate the Inversion
Identify the point where Ibn Taymiyya says the matter is 'completely opposite'—he argues that sensibles are actually particular because only the one who senses has direct access, while mutawatirat are transmitted to many.
4
Assess the Critique's Strength
Consider whether Ibn Taymiyya's argument successfully undermines the logicians' classification. Does his inversion hold logically? What are its consequences for the theory of demonstration?
Think about whether hissiyat can still be used as premises in some way, even if they are particular.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يمكن اعتبارها كلية يقينية . ولكن على فرض هذا فهي تشبه المجربات . وهناك من المناطقة من يجمع تلك المواد الثلاثة الأخيرة تحت اسم التجريبيات . ويقول ابن تيمية إنه كما أن الناس يتفقون في إحساسهم لأجناس بعض المحسوسات مع اختلافهم في عينها ، فإنهم أيضاً يتفقون في معرفة جنس المتواترات أو المجربات ، مع اختلافهم في تصور عينها . فمع أنه قد يتواتر لدى قوم أو يجرب ما لم يتواتر عند غيرهم أو يجربوه . فإنهم قد يتفقون في الجنس ، ولكن من الغريب عند ابن تيمية أن يقول المناطقة بأن المتواترات والمجربات والحدسيات « يختص بها من علمها » فلا يقيم الإنسان بها الحجة أو البرهان على غيره . فهي ليست حجة على غير أصحابها ، بينما يعتبرون الحسيات قضايا كلية . والمسألة عكسية تماماً وقرب هذه المواد من اليقينيات لا يضارعه قرب الحسيات منها . إن المتواترات مثلاً تنقل بواسطة عدد كبير من الناس ، وتصل إلى أسماع عدد كبير من الناس أيضاً . أما الحسيات فلا يشترك فيها إلا من أحسها . هل يشترك الناس في تذوقهم للأشياء أو لمسهم لها أو شمها : إن الحسيات في الواقع عند ابن تيمية هي التي يختص بها من علمها . ولا يستطيع انسان أن يقيم البرهان بها على غيره .
They cannot be considered universal and certain. But on this assumption, they resemble the experimental. And among the logicians there are those who gather those last three materials under the name 'the experimental'. Ibn Taymiyya says that just as people agree in their sensation of the genera of some sensibles while differing in their particular, they also agree in knowing the genus of the mutawatirat or mujarrabat, while differing in conceiving their particular. So although something may be mutawatir among a people or experienced by them that is not mutawatir or experienced by others, they may agree in the genus. But it is strange according to Ibn Taymiyya that the logicians say that the mutawatirat, mujarrabat, and hadsiyat 'are particular to those who know them', so a person cannot establish with them an argument or demonstration against others. So they are not an argument for anyone other than their owners, while they consider the sensibles as universal propositions. The matter is completely opposite, and the proximity of these materials to certainties is not comparable to the proximity of sensibles to them. The mutawatirat, for example, are transmitted by a large number of people, and reach the ears of a large number of people as well. As for the sensibles, only those who sense them share in them. Do people share in their tasting of things, or their touching them, or smelling them? Indeed, according to Ibn Taymiyya, the sensibles are the ones that are particular to those who know them. And no person can establish a demonstration based on them for others.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the Aristotelian logicians' classification of certain premises used in demonstration. The author begins by stating that these materials (mutawatirat, mujarrabat, hadsiyat) cannot be considered universal and certain, but at most they resemble the experimental (mujarrabat). Ibn Taymiyya then draws an analogy: just as people agree on the genus of sensible things (e.g., the category of 'taste') while differing in the particular instance (e.g., the taste of this specific apple), they similarly agree on the genus of mutawatirat or mujarrabat while differing in the particular. For example, a report may be mutawatir among one community but not another. However, the logicians claim that these materials are 'particular to those who know them' and thus cannot serve as proof for others. Ibn Taymiyya finds this strange because the logicians simultaneously consider sensibles (hissiyat) as universal propositions. He argues the opposite: sensibles are actually particular—only the person who tastes, touches, or smells has direct knowledge. The mutawatirat, by contrast, involve transmission by many people and reach many ears, making them more suitable for universal proof. Therefore, it is the sensibles that are truly particular and cannot support demonstration for others.

مجربات

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya reverses the standard classification: sensibles are particular, while transmitted reports (mutawatirat) are more universal, thus undermining the logicians' claim that demonstration can be built solely on universal premises.

Ibn Taymiyya's Epistemic Classification of Sciences
Ibn Taymiyya traces philosophical sciences to hadsiyat or mujarrabat and denies their certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's classification of philosophical sciences into hadsiyat and mujarrabat and explain why he denies them certainty.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya reduces philosophical sciences to subjective intuition and personal experience, stripping them of certainty.

In the previous lessons, Ibn Taymiyya introduced the epistemic materials that yield certainty. Now he applies this framework to the philosophical sciences themselves. He argues that mathematics and natural sciences are not based on certain tawatur but on intuition and personal experiments, transmitted at the level of khabar al-wahid. This is a radical challenge to the Aristotelian claim that these sciences produce certain knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of philosophical sciences
Locate where Ibn Taymiyya divides sciences into mathematical (riyadiyya) and natural/astronomical (tabi'iyya falakiyya). What does he trace each one back to?
Look for the terms 'hadsiat' and 'mujarrabat'.
2
Analyze the basis of these sciences
For each category, identify the epistemic source: intuition for math, personal experience for natural sciences. Why does he say they lack certainty?
Consider that personal experiences vary and are not universally verifiable.
3
Examine the transmission mechanism
How are the propositions of these sciences transmitted? Ibn Taymiyya compares them to khabar al-wahid (single-narrator report) or at best 'special tawatur'. What does this imply about their epistemic status?
Special tawatur means transmission limited to a particular group, not universally attested.
4
Contrast with religious transmission
What does Ibn Taymiyya say about the Attitude of Aristotelian logicians? They reject the tawatur of religious traditions while accepting the transmission of their own sciences. What inconsistency does he point out?
The religious traditions (athar) are based on reliable transmission, whereas philosophical traditions are not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو حاولنا أن نرجع علوم الفلسفة إلى شيء من هذه المواد ، لتبين لنا أن العلوم الرياضية تعود إلى الحدسيات ، والعلوم العقلية الطبيعية والعلوم الفلكية كعلم الهيئة إلى المجربات . وليس في هذه العلوم الأخيرة شيء يقيني البتة . ولكن تعود إلى تجربة أو تجارب شخصية لأفراد من البشر . ولا تتواتر تلك القضايا تواتراً يقينياً بل يمكن أن تنقل بواسطة ما يسمى في الفقه « خبر الواحد » وإذا ما ذكر أن جماعة لا فرداً واحداً قاموا بعمل تجربة من التجارب ، ثم نقلت هذه التجربة من جيل إلى جيل . فغاية ما نستطيع أن نقول إنه تواتر من النوع المعروف « بالتواتر الخاص » ويرى ابن تيمية أنه مع كل هذا ، ينكر المناطقة الأرسططاليسيون تواتر الآثار وهي ما قام اليقين عليها . بينما تواتر آثارهم الفلسفية أو العلمية لا يستند على أصدق الدلائل الحسية ، كما تستند أيضاً أخبار الشرع وآثاره
And if we tried to trace the philosophical sciences back to some of these materials, it would become clear to us that the mathematical sciences go back to the hadsiyat, and the rational natural sciences and the astronomical sciences such as astronomy go back to the mujarrabat. There is nothing certain whatsoever in these latter sciences. Rather, they go back to a personal experience or experiences of individuals among humans. And those propositions are not transmitted with a certain tawatur, but rather can be transmitted by what is called in fiqh 'khabar al-wahid' (single-narrator report). And if it is mentioned that a group, not a single individual, performed an experiment, and then that experiment was transmitted from generation to generation, the utmost we can say is that it is tawatur of the type known as 'special tawatur'. And Ibn Taymiyya holds that despite all this, the Aristotelian logicians deny the tawatur of the Athaar (traditions) which are what certainty is built upon. While the tawatur of their philosophical or scientific traditions is not based on the most truthful sensory indications, as the reports of sacred law and its traditions are also based.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage applies Ibn Taymiyya's epistemic critique to the classification of sciences. The author says that if we examine the philosophical sciences, we find that mathematics (e.g., geometry, arithmetic) derive from hadsiyat (intuitive propositions), while the natural sciences (physics) and astronomy (al-hay'a) derive from mujarrabat (empirical propositions). Crucially, Ibn Taymiyya denies that these sciences yield certainty. They are based on personal experiences of individuals, and their propositions are transmitted not with certain tawatur (widespread transmission) but rather at the level of khabar al-wahid (single-narrator reports in fiqh). Even if multiple people performed an experiment and it was transmitted across generations, it only reaches the level of 'special tawatur', which is not absolutely certain. Ibn Taymiyya then points out a contradiction: Aristotelians reject the tawatur of religious traditions (which are the basis of certainty in religion), yet they accept the tawatur of their own philosophical and scientific ideas, which are not based on the most reliable sensory evidence.

Key Terms
حدسيات (hadsiat): Intuitive propositions; Ibn Taymiyya says mathematical sciences are based on them.
مجربات (mujarrabat): Empirical propositions; natural sciences and astronomy are based on personal experiences.
خبر الواحد (khabar al-wahid): A report from a single narrator in fiqh; used here to describe the transmission of scientific claims. [Note: Standard usul al-fiqh term; here applied metaphorically to scientific transmission.]
تواتر خاص (tawatur khas): A level of transmission that is not absolutely certain but only 'special' to a particular group.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya denies certainty to philosophical sciences by tracing them to subjective intuition or personal experience, and argues their transmission does not reach the level of certain tawatur.

Ibn Taymiyya's Concession: Awwaliyat as Universal in Mathematics
Ibn Taymiyya acknowledges that primary principles (awwaliyat) are universal and useful in mathematics and religious research.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's concession regarding the universality of awwaliyat and identify its implications for the validity of demonstration in mathematics.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya accepts universal axioms in mathematics, but only as mental constructs.

In this passage, we see a crucial turn in Ibn Taymiyya's critique of Greek logic. After a thorough rejection, he concedes that certain primary principles (awwaliyat) are indeed universal and that demonstration based on them is valid, but exclusively within the realm of mathematics. This refined position helps us understand the precise boundaries of his anti-logic stance.

Guided Path
1
Locate the concession
Find the sentence where Ibn Taymiyya shifts from negation to acknowledgment. It begins with 'But Ibn Taymiyya does not continue in his negative stance to the end.'
2
Identify the two areas of benefit
He acknowledges benefit in two respects: firstly in awwaliyat (intellectual axioms), and secondly in religious research. Note the order.
3
Extract the key examples
He quotes examples: 'one is half of two' and 'things equal to the same thing are equal'. These are mathematical truths.
4
Understand the qualification
He says these are 'mental estimations' (muqaddarat fi al-dhihn), not existing as universals in external reality. This limits their scope.
5
Reflect on the scope
Why does he limit demonstration to these axioms? Because he argues that only these are universally certain, and even then only in the mind, not in the external world.

But Ibn Taymiyya does not continue in his negative stance to the end. Rather, he returns to acknowledging the benefit of this universal proposition in demonstration in two respects: firstly, in the awwaliyat or intellectual axioms. Then secondly, he acknowledges its benefit in religious research. As for the awwaliyat, Ibn Taymiyya first acknowledges that their propositions are universal, and secondly, he affirms that demonstration is beneficial in mathematical science. He says, 'Nothing remains with them except the awwaliyat, which are the intellectual axioms and the universal primaries. They are only absolute propositions concerning numbers, quantities, and the like – such as their saying “one is half of two” and “things equal to the same thing are equal” and so on – and they are mental estimations, not in external existence as universals.' And these awwaliyat are the premises and propositions of mathematical science. And Ibn Taymiyya holds that 'their demonstrations do not indicate anything with a constant, certain indication free from corruption except
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage marks a shift in Ibn Taymiyya's position. After his extensive critique, he concedes that certain universal propositions (the awwaliyat, or intellectual axioms) are indeed universal and beneficial. He identifies two areas where they are useful: first, in the awwaliyat themselves (such as the law of non-contradiction and basic mathematical truths), and second, in religious research. He specifically acknowledges that the propositions of awwaliyat are universal, and that demonstration (burhan) is useful in mathematics. He quotes an example: 'one is half of two' and 'things equal to the same thing are equal'. However, he notes that these are mental estimations (muqaddarat fi al-dhihn), not actually existing as universals in the external world. He adds that the demonstrations of the logicians only indicate something with certainty in such areas. This partial concession shows that Ibn Taymiyya is not completely rejecting demonstration, but limiting its scope to a narrow set of axioms.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Classification of Certain Materials
Ibn Taymiyya argues that the Aristotelian division of certain knowledge is not exhaustive and is conventional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's argument that the Aristotelian classification of certain knowledge (al-mawadd al-yaqiniyya) is not exhaustive and evaluate its validity.

Core Idea
Aristotelian certain knowledge classifications are incomplete, not exhaustive.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the claim that the Aristotelian logicians have captured all forms of certain knowledge. He argues that their division of 'certain materials' is not exhaustive and lacks evidence for its completeness. This lesson unpacks his critique and shows how it applies to broader logical classifications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of critique
Find the Aristotelian claim about certain materials (al-mawadd al-yaqiniyya) being the foundation of demonstration. Note that Ibn Taymiyya questions its exhaustiveness.
2
Extract the main argument
Ibn Taymiyya states that there is no evidence to negate other forms of certain knowledge beyond those listed. This makes the division 'not confined' (ghayr munhasir).
Look for the phrase 'لا دليل على نفي ما سوى هذه المواد'
3
Analyze the consequence
The instability of the classification leads to individuals falling under multiple names or a single individual being subdivided arbitrarily.
This shows the classification is not stable.
4
Generalize the critique
Ibn Taymiyya extends this criticism to all Aristotelian divisions, such as the categories and the types of reasoning (qiyas, istiqra', tamthil).
The same logic of non-exhaustiveness applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المناطقة الأرسططاليسيون تواتر الآثار وهي ما قام اليقين عليها . بينما تواتر آثارهم الفلسفية أو العلمية لا يستند على أصدق الدلائل الحسية ، كما تستند أيضاً أخبار الشرع وآثاره النقد الأخير لهذه المواد اليقينية الكلية أن تقسيمها منتشر غير منحصر ، لأنه لا دليل لدى الأرسططاليسيين على نفي ما سوى هذه المواد ـ ويثبت هذا النقد عدم استقرار هذه المواد ـ فيندرج بعض أفراد هذه المواد تحت اسم واحد ، أو ينقسم فرد أو فرع منها إلى أقسام ، ودعوى عدم الحصر أو دعوى انتشار التقاسيم حجة يوجهها ابن تيمية إلى سائر تقاسيم منطق أرسطو . فتقسيم المقولات تقسيم غير منحصر ، وتقسيم طريق الاستدلال إلى ثلاثة : القياس والإستقراء والتمثيل تقسيم منتشر لا يشمل كل طرق الاستدلال ، ويشير هذا النقد إلى اتجاه عام في نقد ابن تيمية ، هو أن هذا المنطق شيء وضعي اصطلاحي وليس قانوناً عقلياً ملزماً فتقاسيمه لم تستقر بعد ، ولم تصل إلى درجة اليقين النهائي . بل هي شيء نسبي بين النظار ، يونانيين كانوا أو مسلمين .
The Aristotelian logicians [claim] the multiplicity of transmissions are the things upon which certainty is established. While the multiplicity of their philosophical or scientific transmissions is not based on the truest sensory evidences, just as the reports of the Sharia and its effects are also based. The final critique of these universal certain materials is that their division is disseminated and not confined, because there is no evidence with the Aristotelians to negate what is other than these materials. And this critique establishes the instability of these materials. So some individuals of these materials fall under one name, or an individual or branch of them divides into sections. The claim of non-confinement or the claim of dissemination of divisions is an argument that Ibn Taymiyya directs to all divisions of Aristotle's logic. Thus the division of categories is a division that is not confined, and the division of the method of inference into three: qiyas, istiqra', and tamthil is a disseminated division that does not include all methods of inference. And this critique points to a general direction in Ibn Taymiyya's critique: that this logic is a conventional artificial thing and not a necessary rational law, so its divisions have not yet stabilized and have not reached the level of final certainty. Rather, they are relative among theorists, whether they were Greeks or Muslims.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the Aristotelian classification of certain knowledge (al-mawadd al-yaqiniyya). The author begins by stating that the Aristotelian logicians consider certain knowledge to be based on repeated transmission (tawatur) and sensory evidence. However, the critique argues that the division of these certain materials is not exhaustive (ghayr munhasir). Ibn Taymiyya points out that there is no evidence to negate the existence of other types of certain knowledge beyond those listed. This instability leads to problems such as multiple individuals falling under one name or a single individual dividing into sections. The claim of non-exhaustiveness is used by Ibn Taymiyya against all Aristotelian classifications, including the division of categories (maqulat) and the division of reasoning into qiyas, istiqra', and tamthil. The critique reveals Ibn Taymiyya's general orientation: logic is a conventional artificial system, not a necessary rational law; its divisions have not reached final certainty but are relative among thinkers, whether Greek or Muslim.

Key Terms
المواد اليقينية
تقسيم
المناطقة الأرسططاليسيون
غير منحصر

A) Because it relies on senses
B) Because there is no evidence to exclude other forms
C) Because it is based on revelation
D) Because it is too broad

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique shifts the burden of proof onto the Aristotelians to demonstrate that their classification is complete, revealing it as a conventional construct rather than a necessary rational law.

Ibn Taymiyya: Logic as Conventional and Relative
Ibn Taymiyya argues that the Aristotelian division of certain knowledge is not exhaustive and is conventional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's conclusion that Aristotelian logic is a conventional (wad'i) and relative system, not a necessary rational law.

Core Idea
Logic is conventional, not a universal rational law.

In the second part of the passage, Ibn Taymiyya generalizes his critique: the whole of Aristotelian logic is a conventional artificial construct, not an absolute rational imperative. Its divisions lack stability and final certainty. This lesson focuses on that overarching claim and its implications for Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Locate the general claim
Find the sentence where Ibn Taymiyya says 'هذا المنطق شيء وضعي اصطلاحي وليس قانوناً عقلياً ملزماً'.
2
Understand 'wad'i istilahi'
'Wad'i' means man-made or conventional, and 'istilahi' means terminological. It is not a necessary law of the intellect.
This challenges the universalism of Aristotelian logic.
3
Identify the reasons given
The divisions have not stabilized and have not reached final certainty; they are relative among theorists.
The lack of stability is evidence of conventionality.
4
Apply the claim to Islamic thought
If logic is conventional, Muslims are free to use or modify it based on Sharia and true knowledge.

Ibn Taymiyya on Mathematical Certainty and Universals
Ibn Taymiyya acknowledges the utility of first principles and mathematics, and recounts three philosophical schools on universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's positive claim about mathematical certainty and his critique of the three schools on universals.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya affirms mathematical certainty while rejecting external universals.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya does not completely reject the utility of demonstration (burhan). He concedes that first principles (al-awwaliyat) are universal and that demonstration yields certain knowledge in mathematics. However, he still rejects the metaphysical claims about universals made by three philosophical schools. This lesson examines his nuanced position.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ibn Taymiyya's positive recognition of first principles
Find in the passage where Ibn Taymiyya says that first principles are universal and necessary. Note his example: 'one is half of two' and 'things equal to the same thing are equal to each other.'
He accepts these as mental estimations, not external realities.
2
Locate his affirmation of mathematical certainty
Read where he says that demonstration is only certainly valid in arithmetic and geometry. These are sciences of discrete and continuous quantity.
He gives examples like addition, division, multiplication, and ratios.
3
Trace his historical narrative of three schools on universals
Ibn Taymiyya recounts Pythagoreans (abstract numbers), Platonists (abstract essences), and Aristotelians (essences conjoined with individuals). Identify the progression.
He says each school corrected the previous but still erred.
4
Summarize his rejection of all three
He explicitly calls each view mistaken. He offers no positive alternative here, only critique.
His rejection is consistent with his view that universals exist only in the mind.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يستنتج ابن تيمية أن المواد اليقينية التي تستخدم في البرهان كمقدمات له ليست كلية . ولكن ابن تيمية لا يستمر في موقفه السالب إلى النهاية . بل يعود إلى الاعتراف بفائدة هذه القضية الكلية في البرهان في ناحيتين : أولاً في الأوليات أو البديهيات العقلية . ثم يعترف ثانياً بفائدتها في الأبحاث الدينية . أما في الأوليات فإن ابن تيمية يعترف أولاً بأن قضاياها كلية ، ويقرر ثانياً أن البرهان يفيد في العلم الرياضي. يقول «لم يبق معهم إلا الأوليات التي هي البديهيات العقلية والأوليات الكلية . إنما هي قضايا مطلقة في الأعداد والمقادير ونحوها – مثل قولهم الواحد نصف الاثنين والأشياء المساوية لشيء واحد متساوية ونحو ذلك – وهي مقدرات في الذهن ليست في الخارج كلية » وهذه الأوليات هي مقدمات العلم الرياضي وقضاياه . ويرى ابن تيمية « أن براهينهم لا تدل على شيء دلالة مطردة يقينية سالمة عن الفساد إلا في هذه المواد الرياضية ، فإن علم الحساب الذي هو علم بالكم المنفصل والهندسة التي هي علم بالكم المتصل علم يقيني لا يحتمل النقيض البتة مثل جمع الأعداد وقسمتها وضربها ونسبة بعضها إلى بعض » ويضرب ابن تيمية أمثلة رياضية متعددة . ثم يقرر أنها أمور معقولة مما يشترك فيها العقلاء ، وأن الناس جميعاً لا بد أن يعرفوا منها شيئاً – وقضاياها في حقيقة الأمر « كلية واجبة القبول لا تنتقض البتة » ويرى ابن تيمية أن الرياضيات كانت منشأ الفلسفة « وهذا كان مبدأ فلسفتهم التي وضعها فيثاغورس . وكانوا يسمون أصحاب العدد وكانوا يظنون أن الأعداد المجردة موجودة خارجة عن الذهن . ثم تبين لأفلاطون وأصحابه غلط ذلك ، وظنوا الماهيات المجردة كالإنسان والفرس المطلق موجودات خارج الذهن . وأنها أزلية أبدية . ثم تبين لأرسطو وأصحابه غلط ذلك فقالوا : بل هذه الماهيات المطلقة موجودة في الخارج مقارنة لوجود الأشخاص ، ومشى من مشى من اتباع أرسطو المتأخرين على هذا وهو غلط أيضاً » عرض ابن تيمية اذن للمذاهب الثلاثة – الفيثاغورية القائلة بالأعداد المجردة ، والأفلاطونية القائلة بالماهيات المجردة والأرسططاليسية القائلة بالماهيات المطلقة المقارنة لوجود الأشخاص ، ورفض كل هذه الأقوال .
Ibn Taymiyya concludes that the certain materials used in demonstration as premises are not universal. But Ibn Taymiyya does not continue in his negative stance to the end. Rather, he returns to acknowledging the benefit of this universal proposition in demonstration in two aspects: first in the first principles or rational self-evident truths. Then he acknowledges secondarily its benefit in religious investigations. As for the first principles, Ibn Taymiyya first acknowledges that their propositions are universal, and secondarily decides that demonstration is useful in mathematical science. He says: 'Nothing remains with them except the first principles which are the rational self-evident truths and the universal first principles. They are absolute propositions in numbers and magnitudes and the like – such as their saying the one is half of two and things equal to one thing are equal to each other and the like – and they are estimations in the mind, not universal in the external world.' And these first principles are the premises of mathematical science and its propositions. And Ibn Taymiyya holds that 'their demonstrations indicate nothing with a consistent certain indication free from corruption except in these mathematical materials, for the science of arithmetic which is the science of discrete quantity and geometry which is the science of continuous quantity is certain knowledge that does not accept contradiction at all, like the addition of numbers, their division, multiplication, and the ratio of some to others.' And Ibn Taymiyya gives multiple mathematical examples. Then he decides that they are intelligible matters in which the intelligent share, and that all people must know something of them – and their propositions in reality are 'universal, necessary of acceptance, never invalidated.' And Ibn Taymiyya holds that mathematics was the origin of philosophy: 'And this was the beginning of their philosophy that Pythagoras established. And they were called the people of number, and they thought that abstract numbers exist external to the mind. Then it became clear to Plato and his followers that this was mistaken, and they thought that abstract essences like absolute man and horse are existents external to the mind, and that they are eternal and everlasting. Then it became clear to Aristotle and his followers that this was mistaken, so they said: rather, these absolute essences exist in the external world conjoined with the existence of individuals, and some of the later followers of Aristotle followed this, and it is also mistaken.' Thus Ibn Taymiyya presented the three schools – the Pythagorean which says abstract numbers, the Platonic which says abstract essences, and the Aristotelian which says absolute essences conjoined with the existence of individuals – and he rejected all these statements.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya acknowledges the value of certain universal propositions in demonstration (burhan). He concedes that first principles (awwaliyat) or self-evident truths are universal and that demonstration is useful in mathematical science. He quotes himself: only first principles remain, which are absolute propositions about numbers and magnitudes, such as 'one is half of two' and 'things equal to the same thing are equal to each other.' These are mental estimations, not existing externally as universals. Ibn Taymiyya asserts that the demonstrations of the logicians are only certainly valid in mathematical materials: arithmetic (science of discrete quantity) and geometry (science of continuous quantity) are certain and cannot be contradicted. He gives examples of operations like addition, division, multiplication, and ratios. He then states that these are rational matters shared by all intelligent people, and their propositions are universally necessary and never invalid. He traces the origin of philosophy to mathematics, noting that Pythagoreans posited abstract numbers existing externally, then Plato and his followers posited abstract essences (like absolute man and horse) as eternal entities, then Aristotle and his followers corrected that by claiming these absolute essences exist in external things along with individuals, a view later followers maintained but which Ibn Taymiyya also rejects. Thus he presents three schools and rejects them all.

البرهان
المذاهب الثلاثة

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya accepts mathematical certainty while rejecting the existence of universals outside the mind, demonstrating his nuanced critique of Greek logic.

Ibn Taymiyya on the Epistemic Priority of Particulars
Ibn Taymiyya's epistemic hierarchy of certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that knowledge of particulars precedes and is more certain than universal knowledge.

Core Idea
Particulars are epistemically prior: we know individuals before species, and species before genera.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the Aristotelian assumption that universals are the foundation of certain knowledge. In this passage, he argues that our natural disposition (fitra) grasps particulars more directly and with greater certainty than universals. This lesson unpacks his hierarchical argument and its implications for the structure of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the epistemic hierarchy
Locate the passage where Ibn Taymiyya ranks certainty: individuals > species > genera. Note that he says 'certainty about individuals in sensory matters is greater than their certainty about universals.'
2
Extract the self-knowledge example
Find the example: a person knows he himself is sentient and voluntarily mobile before knowing that all humans are like that. This is the starting point for understanding how universal knowledge grows from particular experience.
The example is given in the second half of the Arabic passage.
3
Trace the chain of knowledge
Follow the sequence: from 'I know I am sentient' to 'all humans are sentient' to 'all animals are sentient.' Each step extends from the previous particular, not from an innate universal.
Ibn Taymiyya uses this to show that universal knowledge is derivative, not foundational.
4
Formulate the argument against Aristotelian priority
Summarize Ibn Taymiyya's claim: 'It is not permissible to say that knowledge of persons depends on knowledge of species and genera.' Instead, particular knowledge is the bedrock, and universals are abstractions built from repeated particulars.
This directly opposes the standard Mantiq view that definition and demonstration require prior universal concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن العلم بالأعيان المعينة وبأنواع الكليات لا يحدث في النفس بالبديهة والضرورة فحسب بل « إن جزم النظار بالشخصيات في الحسيات أعظم من جزمهم بالكليات ، وجزمهم بكليات الأنواع أعظم من جزمهم بكليات الأجناس ، والعلم بالجزئيات أسبق إلى الفطرة ، فجزم الفطرة بها أقوى . ثم كلما قوي العقل اتسعت الكليات » وحينئذ فلا يجوز أن يقال إن العلم بالأشخاص موقوف على العلم بالأنواع والأجناس ، وعلى أن العلم بالأنواع موقوف على العلم بالأجناس ، إنه قد يعلم الإنسان أنه حساس متحرك بالإرادة قبل أن يعلم أن كل إنسان كذلك . ويعلم أن الإنسان كذلك ، قبل أن يعلم أن كل حيوان كذلك . فلم يبق علمه بأن غيره من الحيوان حساس متحرك بالإرادة موقوفاً على البرهان . وإذا علم حكم سائر الناس وسائر الحيوان فالنفس تحكم بذلك ـ كما قلنا ـ بواسطة علمها : أن ذلك الغائب مثل هذا الشاهد . أو أنه يساويه في السبب الموجب لكونه متحركاً(١) أي أننا نستخدم طريقاً ينتقل بنا من جزئي إلى جزئي . وهذا الطريق في منطق أرسطو ظني ، بينما قياس الغائب على الشاهد في الواقع إذا ما نظمته التجربة ، وحددته مسالك العلة ، أصبح موصلاً إلى اليقين وهذا ما لم يعرفه منطق أرسطو . يتبين لنا الآن أن ابن تيمية نقد القضية الكلية من حيث هي عنصر من عناصر البرهان : ثم استطرد إلى الكلام في مسائل الكليات والجزئيات من وجهة ميافيزيقية وابستمولوجية . فشك في قيمة الكليات جميعها من حيث هي عالم معقول . وقد استعان في هذا النقد بحجج من الشكاك وبأقوال يرجح أنها من الرواقية . ثم وضع بجانب هذه الحجج نقداً مبتكراً ، وحاول أن يصوغ كل ذلك في صورة إسلامية . ثم حاول أن يحدد مصدر العلم وطريقه وكان هذا المصدر أو هذا الطريق عنده هو التجربة ولذلك كان العلم الوحيد النافع لديه هو العلم الجزئي .
Indeed, knowledge of specific individuals and of types of universals does not occur in the soul by intuition and necessity alone, but rather: 'The certainty of thinkers about individuals in sensory matters is greater than their certainty about universals, and their certainty about universals of species is greater than their certainty about universals of genera, and knowledge of particulars is more prior to natural disposition, so the certainty of natural disposition with them is stronger. Then, whenever the intellect strengthens, universals expand.' At that time it is not permissible to say that knowledge of persons depends on knowledge of species and genera, and that knowledge of species depends on knowledge of genera. Indeed, a person knows that he is sentient and voluntarily mobile before he knows that every human is like that. And he knows that the human is like that before he knows that every animal is like that. So his knowledge that other animals are sentient and voluntarily mobile is not dependent on demonstration. And when he knows the judgment of other people and other animals, the soul judges that—as we said—by virtue of its knowledge that that absent thing is like this witnessed thing, or that it equals it in the cause necessitating its being mobile. (1) That is, we use a path that transfers us from a particular to a particular. And this path in Aristotelian logic is probabilistic, whereas the reasoning from the absent to the witnessed in reality, if organized by experience and determined by the modes of causality, becomes a means to certainty—and this is what Aristotelian logic did not know. It now becomes clear to us that Ibn Taymiyya criticized the universal proposition insofar as it is an element of demonstration; then he proceeded to discuss the issues of universals and particulars from a metaphysical and epistemological perspective. He doubted the value of all universals insofar as they are an intelligible world. He aided himself in this critique with arguments from the skeptics and with sayings that are likely from the Stoics. Then, alongside these arguments, he placed an innovative critique, and tried to formulate all of that in an Islamic form. Then he tried to determine the source of knowledge and its path, and this source or path in his view was experience, and therefore the only useful knowledge for him was particular knowledge.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's epistemological critique of the Aristotelian priority of universals over particulars. It opens with a direct quotation from Ibn Taymiyya arguing that certainty regarding sensory individuals is stronger than certainty regarding universals, and that knowledge of particulars is naturally prior. The reasoning is empirical: a person knows he himself is sentient and volitionally mobile before knowing that all humans share this attribute, and knows that about humans before knowing it about all animals. Thus, knowledge of another individual animal's sentience does not require a demonstrative proof; rather, it comes through a comparison of the absent (al-gha'ib) to the present (al-shahid) based on similarity or shared cause. The author notes that in Aristotelian logic this analogical path yields only probability, but when organized by experience (tajriba) and causal analysis, it can achieve certainty—something Aristotle's logic missed. The passage then summarizes Ibn Taymiyya's overall project: he attacked the universal proposition as the material of demonstration, doubted the value of universals altogether, borrowed from skeptics and Stoics, but also added original criticism, trying to cast it in an Islamic framework. Ultimately, Ibn Taymiyya identified experience as the source of knowledge, making particular knowledge the only useful kind. This section is a critical exposition, not a formal syllogism, but rather an epistemological argument defending the priority of particular knowledge.

Experience and Analogical Reasoning as Sources of Certainty
Ibn Taymiyya's defense of analogical reasoning as certain when grounded in experience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's revalorization of analogical reasoning (qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid) as a source of certainty when grounded in experience (tajriba).

Core Idea
Analogy from the witnessed to the absent, when organized by experience, yields certainty — not just probability.

In Aristotelian logic, reasoning from the witnessed to the absent (qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid) is considered merely probabilistic (zanni). Ibn Taymiyya challenges this, arguing that when such reasoning is disciplined by repeated experience (tajriba) and causal analysis, it can produce certain knowledge. This lesson examines his argument and its implications for the nature of proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of inference Ibn Taymiyya endorses
Focus on the phrase 'قياس الغائب على الشاهد' (reasoning from the absent to the witnessed). Ibn Taymiyya uses this to explain how we know other individuals are sentient: we compare them to ourselves based on similarity or shared cause.
This is analogical reasoning, not deductive syllogism.
2
Understand Aristotle's view on this inference
Note the author's statement that in Aristotelian logic, this path is 'ظني' (probabilistic). Aristotle required universal premises for demonstration, and analogical inference alone does not meet that standard.
3
Find the conditions that make it certain according to Ibn Taymiyya
Look for the conditions: 'إذا ما نظمته التجربة، وحددته مسالك العلة' (if organized by experience and determined by the modes of causality). Tajriba (repeated observation) and identification of causal pathways transform analogy into certainty.
This is Ibn Taymiyya's key addition to the logical tradition.
4
Relate this to his critique of Aristotelian demonstration
Ibn Taymiyya argues that Aristotelian logic missed this possibility. By grounding analogy in experience and causal determination, one can achieve yaqin (certainty) without needing universal premises. This undercuts the Aristotelian demand for syllogistic demonstration as the only certain method.

إن العلم بالأعيان المعينة وبأنواع الكليات لا يحدث في النفس بالبديهة والضرورة فحسب بل « إن جزم النظار بالشخصيات في الحسيات أعظم من جزمهم بالكليات ، وجزمهم بكليات الأنواع أعظم من جزمهم بكليات الأجناس ، والعلم بالجزئيات أسبق إلى الفطرة ، فجزم الفطرة بها أقوى . ثم كلما قوي العقل اتسعت الكليات » وحينئذ فلا يجوز أن يقال إن العلم بالأشخاص موقوف على العلم بالأنواع والأجناس ، وعلى أن العلم بالأنواع موقوف على العلم بالأجناس ، إنه قد يعلم الإنسان أنه حساس متحرك بالإرادة قبل أن يعلم أن كل إنسان كذلك . ويعلم أن الإنسان كذلك ، قبل أن يعلم أن كل حيوان كذلك . فلم يبق علمه بأن غيره من الحيوان حساس متحرك بالإرادة موقوفاً على البرهان . وإذا علم حكم سائر الناس وسائر الحيوان فالنفس تحكم بذلك ـ كما قلنا ـ بواسطة علمها : أن ذلك الغائب مثل هذا الشاهد . أو أنه يساويه في السبب الموجب لكونه متحركاً(١) أي أننا نستخدم طريقاً ينتقل بنا من جزئي إلى جزئي . وهذا الطريق في منطق أرسطو ظني ، بينما قياس الغائب على الشاهد في الواقع إذا ما نظمته التجربة ، وحددته مسالك العلة ، أصبح موصلاً إلى اليقين وهذا ما لم يعرفه منطق أرسطو . يتبين لنا الآن أن ابن تيمية نقد القضية الكلية من حيث هي عنصر من عناصر البرهان : ثم استطرد إلى الكلام في مسائل الكليات والجزئيات من وجهة ميافيزيقية وابستمولوجية . فشك في قيمة الكليات جميعها من حيث هي عالم معقول . وقد استعان في هذا النقد بحجج من الشكاك وبأقوال يرجح أنها من الرواقية . ثم وضع بجانب هذه الحجج نقداً مبتكراً ، وحاول أن يصوغ كل ذلك في صورة إسلامية . ثم حاول أن يحدد مصدر العلم وطريقه وكان هذا المصدر أو هذا الطريق عنده هو التجربة ولذلك كان العلم الوحيد النافع لديه هو العلم الجزئي .
