Ibn Taymiyya's Rejection of the Dhati/Lazim Distinction
Critical analysis of the dhati/lazim distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument against the essential/accidental distinction and identify his reasons for calling it artificial.

Core Idea
The essential/accidental distinction is baseless because both types often share the same need for proof.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges a cornerstone of Aristotelian logic: the division of attributes into essential (dhati) and accidental necessary (lazim). In this passage, he argues that this division is artificial and that what logicians call 'essential' is no more fundamental than 'necessary' attributes. Let's see his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two attribute types
Find where Ibn Taymiyya mentions 'dhati' and 'lazim'. Note that he says the syllogistic process aims to establish either of these.
2
Compare their need for a middle term
Ibn Taymiyya says the essential does not need a middle term, while necessary attributes may or may not. But then he argues that both are similar: some need a middle term, some do not.
3
Note his claim about specificity
He asserts that necessary attributes (lazim) are more specific and give a more complete conception than what logicians consider essential.
4
Find the conclusion
He concludes that the distinction is artificial (sina'i mutakallaf) and has no basis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحاجب الأصولي المشهور بالتعليل ولذلك وجه نقده الحاد إليه . ورأى أن القضايا البديهية ، أو الضرورية لسنا في حاجة إلى الحد الأوسط في التوصل إليها . ولعله أدرك أننا نصل اليها بحدس عقلي مطلق ، معصوم من الصواب والخطأ أو فوقهما . أما في غير القضايا البديهية والضرورية ، فان ابن تيمية يرى أن العملية القياسية هي لإثبات الذاتي أو العرضي اللازم للموضوع . أما الذاتي فلا يحتاج فيه إلى حد أوسط أو دليل أو تعليل ، إنما هو بين بنفسه . أما اللوازم ، فمنها ما يحتاج إلى وسط ومنها ما لا يحتاج ومنها ما يحتاج في إثباته وتدبره إلى مدة طويلة أو مدة قصيرة ، ومنها ما يفتقر إلى وسط واحد ، ومنها ما يفتقر إلى أكثر من وسط . ويرى ابن تيمية أن التفريق بين الذاتي واللازم إنما هو تفريق صناعي متكلف لا يقوم على أساس . فالذاتيات واللوازم تتشابه في أن البعض منها قد يحتاج إلى حد أوسط لإثباتها والبعض لا يحتاج ثم أن اللوازم أكثر تحديداً وأتم تصوراً للشيء مما يعتبره المناطقة بالذاتيات . أما إذا كانت الغاية من الحد الأوسط هو الدليل الذي يعلل به الباحث الثبوت الذهني ، لا الخارجي ، فان ابن تيمية يرى أن هذا أمر يختلف فيه الناس . ويقول ابن تيمية « ولا ريب أن ما يستدل به سواء سمي قياساً أو برهاناً أو غير ذلك . قد يكون هو علة لثبوت الحكم في نفس الأمر ، ويسمى قياس العلة و « برهان العلة » وبرهان « لم » وقد لا يكون كذلك ، وهو الدليل المطلق ، ويسمى قياس الدلالة وبرهان الدلالة وبرهان إن . وهذا مراد ابن سينا وغيره بالوسط(١) . ويكاد ابن تيمية هنا أن يعترف بفائدة القياس ـ لا من ناحية التوصل إلى التصديق ، إلى العلم في ذاته ، وإنما من ناحية تربوية عقلية ، فالعملية القياسية هي تعليل فقط ذهني لشيء في الخارج ، ولكنها لا تصل أبداً إلى إقامة شيء أو علاقة خارجية موجودة فعلاً ، ولذلك يلجأ المناطقة إليها لإثبات الحكم أو تعليله في نفسه . ولكن ابن تيمية يرى أنهم يختلفون حتى في هذا فالبعض يستخدم ما أسماه المناطقة قياس العلة أو برهان العلة أو برهان اللم : أي ذكر ما يجاب به عن لم ، والبعض يستخدم قياس أو برهان الدلالة أو برهان إن . وبرهان الإن : هو برهان الدليل على أن الحد الأكبر موجود للأصغر من غير بيان علته . تتفاوت قوى الناس الذهنية في هذا وتختلف ، فالبعض يستخدم هذا البرهان والبعض يستخدم ذاك . فليس هناك إذن قانون يحتم طريقاً واحداً لاقتناص الذاتيات للشيء أو اللازم له ، كما أن هذا التقسيم الأخير إلى ذاتيات ولوازم غير قائم على أساس .
The famous usuli scholar [al-Amidi?] known for causal explanation, and therefore his sharp criticism was directed at it. He held that self-evident or necessary propositions do not need the middle term to reach them. Perhaps he realized that we reach them by absolute rational intuition, infallible in truth and falsehood or beyond both. As for non-self-evident and non-necessary propositions, Ibn Taymiyya holds that the syllogistic process is to establish the essential (dhati) or the accidental necessary (lazim) attribute of the subject. The essential does not require a middle term or evidence or explanation; it is evident by itself. As for the necessary attributes, some need a middle term, some do not, some require a long or short period to establish and contemplate, some require a single middle term, and some require more than one. Ibn Taymiyya holds that the distinction between the essential and the necessary is an artificial, forced distinction with no basis. The essential and the necessary are similar in that some of them may need a middle term to prove them and some do not; moreover, the necessary attributes are more specific and give a more complete conception of the thing than what the logicians consider essential. As for the purpose of the middle term being the evidence by which the researcher explains mental existence, not external existence, Ibn Taymiyya holds that this is a matter on which people differ. Ibn Taymiyya says: 'There is no doubt that what is used as evidence, whether called syllogism or demonstration or otherwise, may be the cause of the judgment's affirmation in reality, and is called syllogism of cause and demonstration of cause and demonstration of 'why' (limm) and may not be so, and it is the absolute evidence, called syllogism of indication and demonstration of indication and demonstration of 'that' (inn). This is what Ibn Sina and others meant by the middle term.' Ibn Taymiyya here almost acknowledges the benefit of the syllogism – not for reaching assent (tasdiq), for knowledge in itself, but from an intellectual educational perspective: the syllogistic process is only a mental explanation of something external, but it never establishes an actually existing external thing or relation. Therefore logicians resort to it to prove or explain the judgment itself. But Ibn Taymiyya holds that they differ even in this; some use what the logicians called syllogism of cause (qiyas al-'illa) or demonstration of cause (burhan al-'illa) or demonstration of 'why' (burhan al-limm), i.e., mentioning what answers 'why', and others use syllogism or demonstration of indication (burhan al-dalala) or demonstration of 'that' (burhan inn). The demonstration of 'that' is the evidence that the major term exists for the minor without explaining its cause. People's mental powers vary in this, so some use this demonstration and others that. Therefore there is no law that imposes a single way to capture the essentials of a thing or its necessary attributes, just as this last division into essentials and necessary attributes is baseless.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's critical analysis of the role of the middle term in syllogistic reasoning and his rejection of the logicians' distinction between essential (dhati) and accidental necessary (lazim) attributes. The author begins by noting that for self-evident (badihi) or necessary (daruri) propositions, the middle term is unnecessary; they are grasped by pure rational intuition. For other propositions, Ibn Taymiyya holds that the syllogistic process aims to establish either an essential attribute (dhati) or a necessary accidental attribute (lazim) of the subject. However, he argues that essential attributes do not require a middle term—they are self-evident. As for necessary attributes, some require a middle term, some do not, and some require varying time and multiple middle terms. Ibn Taymiyya then attacks the very distinction between dhati and lazim, calling it artificial and baseless. He points out that both types of attributes are similar: some need a middle term, some do not. Moreover, he claims that attributes considered necessary (lazim) are often more specific and give a more complete conception than what logicians call essential. The passage then shifts to the purpose of the middle term. If the middle term is intended as an explanatory cause for mental existence (not external), Ibn Taymiyya observes that people differ in this. He quotes himself: whatever is used as evidence—whether called syllogism (qiyas) or demonstration (burhan)—may be a cause (illa) of the judgment in reality (called qiyas al-'illa, burhan al-'illa, burhan limm) or may not be (called qiyas al-dalala, burhan al-dalala, burhan inn). This distinction between demonstration of 'why' (limm) and demonstration of 'that' (inn) is attributed to Ibn Sina. Ibn Taymiyya almost concedes a pedagogical benefit of syllogism: it is a mental explanation of an external matter, but it never establishes actual external relations. Logicians use it to prove or explain a judgment, but people differ in their use of these types of demonstration. Ultimately, Ibn Taymiyya concludes that there is no universal law that forces a single method to capture essential or necessary attributes, and the division into dhati and lazim is unfounded.

Key Terms
الذاتي
اللازم
الحد الأوسط

The Two Types of Demonstration: Burhan Limm and Burhan Inn
Examination of the two types of demonstration according to Ibn Taymiyya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between demonstration of 'why' (burhan limm) and demonstration of 'that' (burhan inn) as discussed by Ibn Taymiyya.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya acknowledges that different thinkers use different types of demonstration depending on the case.

Ibn Taymiyya explains that the middle term can serve two roles: as a cause (illa) of the judgment or merely as an indicator (dalil). This corresponds to the distinction between burhan limm (demonstration of 'why') and burhan inn (demonstration of 'that'). He notes that people differ in which they employ.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two types of evidence
Find where Ibn Taymiyya mentions 'qiyas al-'illa' and 'qiyas al-dalala'. He says evidence may be a cause of the judgment or not.
2
Define the two demonstrations
Burhan al-'illa (or burhan limm) shows why the judgment holds; burhan al-dalala (or burhan inn) only shows that it holds without explaining why.
3
Note the variability among people
Ibn Taymiyya says people's mental powers vary so some use one type, others the other.
4
Understand the conclusion
There is no single law forcing one method; the division is not absolute.

الحاجب الأصولي المشهور بالتعليل ولذلك وجه نقده الحاد إليه . ورأى أن القضايا البديهية ، أو الضرورية لسنا في حاجة إلى الحد الأوسط في التوصل إليها . ولعله أدرك أننا نصل اليها بحدس عقلي مطلق ، معصوم من الصواب والخطأ أو فوقهما . أما في غير القضايا البديهية والضرورية ، فان ابن تيمية يرى أن العملية القياسية هي لإثبات الذاتي أو العرضي اللازم للموضوع . أما الذاتي فلا يحتاج فيه إلى حد أوسط أو دليل أو تعليل ، إنما هو بين بنفسه . أما اللوازم ، فمنها ما يحتاج إلى وسط ومنها ما لا يحتاج ومنها ما يحتاج في إثباته وتدبره إلى مدة طويلة أو مدة قصيرة ، ومنها ما يفتقر إلى وسط واحد ، ومنها ما يفتقر إلى أكثر من وسط . ويرى ابن تيمية أن التفريق بين الذاتي واللازم إنما هو تفريق صناعي متكلف لا يقوم على أساس . فالذاتيات واللوازم تتشابه في أن البعض منها قد يحتاج إلى حد أوسط لإثباتها والبعض لا يحتاج ثم أن اللوازم أكثر تحديداً وأتم تصوراً للشيء مما يعتبره المناطقة بالذاتيات . أما إذا كانت الغاية من الحد الأوسط هو الدليل الذي يعلل به الباحث الثبوت الذهني ، لا الخارجي ، فان ابن تيمية يرى أن هذا أمر يختلف فيه الناس . ويقول ابن تيمية « ولا ريب أن ما يستدل به سواء سمي قياساً أو برهاناً أو غير ذلك . قد يكون هو علة لثبوت الحكم في نفس الأمر ، ويسمى قياس العلة و « برهان العلة » وبرهان « لم » وقد لا يكون كذلك ، وهو الدليل المطلق ، ويسمى قياس الدلالة وبرهان الدلالة وبرهان إن . وهذا مراد ابن سينا وغيره بالوسط(١) . ويكاد ابن تيمية هنا أن يعترف بفائدة القياس ـ لا من ناحية التوصل إلى التصديق ، إلى العلم في ذاته ، وإنما من ناحية تربوية عقلية ، فالعملية القياسية هي تعليل فقط ذهني لشيء في الخارج ، ولكنها لا تصل أبداً إلى إقامة شيء أو علاقة خارجية موجودة فعلاً ، ولذلك يلجأ المناطقة إليها لإثبات الحكم أو تعليله في نفسه . ولكن ابن تيمية يرى أنهم يختلفون حتى في هذا فالبعض يستخدم ما أسماه المناطقة قياس العلة أو برهان العلة أو برهان اللم : أي ذكر ما يجاب به عن لم ، والبعض يستخدم قياس أو برهان الدلالة أو برهان إن . وبرهان الإن : هو برهان الدليل على أن الحد الأكبر موجود للأصغر من غير بيان علته . تتفاوت قوى الناس الذهنية في هذا وتختلف ، فالبعض يستخدم هذا البرهان والبعض يستخدم ذاك . فليس هناك إذن قانون يحتم طريقاً واحداً لاقتناص الذاتيات للشيء أو اللازم له ، كما أن هذا التقسيم الأخير إلى ذاتيات ولوازم غير قائم على أساس .
The famous usuli scholar [al-Amidi?] known for causal explanation, and therefore his sharp criticism was directed at it. He held that self-evident or necessary propositions do not need the middle term to reach them. Perhaps he realized that we reach them by absolute rational intuition, infallible in truth and falsehood or beyond both. As for non-self-evident and non-necessary propositions, Ibn Taymiyya holds that the syllogistic process is to establish the essential (dhati) or the accidental necessary (lazim) attribute of the subject. The essential does not require a middle term or evidence or explanation; it is evident by itself. As for the necessary attributes, some need a middle term, some do not, some require a long or short period to establish and contemplate, some require a single middle term, and some require more than one. Ibn Taymiyya holds that the distinction between the essential and the necessary is an artificial, forced distinction with no basis. The essential and the necessary are similar in that some of them may need a middle term to prove them and some do not; moreover, the necessary attributes are more specific and give a more complete conception of the thing than what the logicians consider essential. As for the purpose of the middle term being the evidence by which the researcher explains mental existence, not external existence, Ibn Taymiyya holds that this is a matter on which people differ. Ibn Taymiyya says: 'There is no doubt that what is used as evidence, whether called syllogism or demonstration or otherwise, may be the cause of the judgment's affirmation in reality, and is called syllogism of cause and demonstration of cause and demonstration of 'why' (limm) and may not be so, and it is the absolute evidence, called syllogism of indication and demonstration of indication and demonstration of 'that' (inn). This is what Ibn Sina and others meant by the middle term.' Ibn Taymiyya here almost acknowledges the benefit of the syllogism – not for reaching assent (tasdiq), for knowledge in itself, but from an intellectual educational perspective: the syllogistic process is only a mental explanation of something external, but it never establishes an actually existing external thing or relation. Therefore logicians resort to it to prove or explain the judgment itself. But Ibn Taymiyya holds that they differ even in this; some use what the logicians called syllogism of cause (qiyas al-'illa) or demonstration of cause (burhan al-'illa) or demonstration of 'why' (burhan al-limm), i.e., mentioning what answers 'why', and others use syllogism or demonstration of indication (burhan al-dalala) or demonstration of 'that' (burhan inn). The demonstration of 'that' is the evidence that the major term exists for the minor without explaining its cause. People's mental powers vary in this, so some use this demonstration and others that. Therefore there is no law that imposes a single way to capture the essentials of a thing or its necessary attributes, just as this last division into essentials and necessary attributes is baseless.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's critical analysis of the role of the middle term in syllogistic reasoning and his rejection of the logicians' distinction between essential (dhati) and accidental necessary (lazim) attributes. The author begins by noting that for self-evident (badihi) or necessary (daruri) propositions, the middle term is unnecessary; they are grasped by pure rational intuition. For other propositions, Ibn Taymiyya holds that the syllogistic process aims to establish either an essential attribute (dhati) or a necessary accidental attribute (lazim) of the subject. However, he argues that essential attributes do not require a middle term—they are self-evident. As for necessary attributes, some require a middle term, some do not, and some require varying time and multiple middle terms. Ibn Taymiyya then attacks the very distinction between dhati and lazim, calling it artificial and baseless. He points out that both types of attributes are similar: some need a middle term, some do not. Moreover, he claims that attributes considered necessary (lazim) are often more specific and give a more complete conception than what logicians call essential. The passage then shifts to the purpose of the middle term. If the middle term is intended as an explanatory cause for mental existence (not external), Ibn Taymiyya observes that people differ in this. He quotes himself: whatever is used as evidence—whether called syllogism (qiyas) or demonstration (burhan)—may be a cause (illa) of the judgment in reality (called qiyas al-'illa, burhan al-'illa, burhan limm) or may not be (called qiyas al-dalala, burhan al-dalala, burhan inn). This distinction between demonstration of 'why' (limm) and demonstration of 'that' (inn) is attributed to Ibn Sina. Ibn Taymiyya almost concedes a pedagogical benefit of syllogism: it is a mental explanation of an external matter, but it never establishes actual external relations. Logicians use it to prove or explain a judgment, but people differ in their use of these types of demonstration. Ultimately, Ibn Taymiyya concludes that there is no universal law that forces a single method to capture essential or necessary attributes, and the division into dhati and lazim is unfounded.

Key Terms
قياس العلة
برهان الدلالة
برهان إن
الحد الأوسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya's discussion, what is the difference between burhan al-'illa and burhan al-dalala?
A) Burhan al-'illa explains the cause of the judgment, while burhan al-dalala only indicates the judgment without cause.
B) Burhan al-'illa is used for essential attributes, burhan al-dalala for accidental.
C) Burhan al-'illa is invalid, while burhan al-dalala is valid.
D) There is no real difference; they are the same.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya recognizes two types of demonstration: causal and indicative, and notes that people differ in their use, rejecting a single mandatory method.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following argument as burhan inn or burhan limm: 'The world is changing, therefore it has a creator (because all changing things have a creator).' Explain why.
?
Give an example from fiqh where a ruling is proven by indicative demonstration (burhan inn) vs. causal demonstration (burhan limm).

Demonstration -- Burhan
intermediate

The Pedagogical Role of Syllogism According to Ibn Taymiyya
Analysis of Ibn Taymiyya's qualified acceptance of syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's view that syllogism is a pedagogical tool for mental explanation rather than discovery of external reality.

Core Idea
Syllogism educates the mind by explaining mental existence, not by establishing external facts.

Ibn Taymiyya nearly concedes a benefit of syllogistic reasoning: it can serve an intellectual educational purpose. However, he insists it does not establish external relations, only mental explanations. Let's explore this subtle concession.

Guided Path
1
Find the concession
Locate where the author says Ibn Taymiyya 'almost acknowledges' the benefit. The phrase: 'ويكاد ابن تيمية هنا أن يعترف بفائدة القياس'.
2
Identify the purpose
The syllogistic process is described as 'تعليل فقط ذهني لشيء في الخارج' — a mental explanation of something external.
3
Note the limitation
Ibn Taymiyya says it does not establish an external relation that actually exists.
4
Understand the consequence
Thus syllogism is useful for organizing knowledge and teaching, not for discovering new facts.

Source Text

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Universal Affirmative Requirement in Demonstration
Author critiques the requirement of a universal affirmative premise in the demonstrative syllogism according to Ibn Taymiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's objection to the requirement of a universal affirmative premise in demonstrative syllogisms and evaluate its implications for the certainty of knowledge.

Core Idea
For Ibn Taymiyya, a universal premise itself already yields the conclusion; the syllogistic form is superfluous for certain knowledge.

In this passage, we encounter Ibn Taymiyya's critical engagement with the Aristotelian claim that certain knowledge (al-'ilm al-yaqini) is only attainable through demonstration (burhan), and that demonstration necessarily includes a universal affirmative premise. Ibn Taymiyya argues that for self-evident or necessary propositions—what he calls 'pure rationalities' (al-'aqliyyat al-mahda)—the syllogistic process is unnecessary. If we already possess the universal proposition, the conclusion is already known, making the syllogism redundant. This challenges the foundation of the logicians' theory of demonstration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of critique
What exactly are the Aristotelian logicians claiming according to Ibn Taymiyya? Locate the statement: they affirm that theoretical certain sciences are only obtained by demonstration, and demonstration must contain a universal affirmative proposition.
2
Distinguish the two categories of propositions
Ibn Taymiyya distinguishes between self-evident/necessary propositions ('aqliyyat mahda) and those requiring demonstration. What is the difference? For the former, no syllogism is needed; for the latter, demonstration might be needed but the universal premise is already sufficient.
3
Analyze Ibn Taymiyya's argument
He says: 'There is no proposition among these universal propositions that is made a premise in a demonstration except that the knowledge of the conclusion is possible.' Explain why this undermines the necessity of the syllogistic form. The universal premise itself contains the conclusion, so the syllogism adds nothing.
4
Evaluate the implication for logical theory
If Ibn Taymiyya is correct, what happens to the traditional concept of burhan? Does it collapse? Or is it still useful for non-self-evident matters? The passage suggests that for pure rationalities, burhan is redundant, but it might still be needed for other types of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۲ - نقد اشتراط قضية كلية موجبة في القياس الشمولي : يرى ابن تيمية أن المناطقة الأرسططاليسيين يقررون أن العلوم اليقينية النظرية لا تحصل إلا بالبرهان . وأن البرهان وهو « قياس شمولي » لا بد من وجود قضية كلية موجبة فيه . وبدون هذا لا نصل إلى العلم اليقيني وقد سبق أن تكلمنا في الفصل السابق عن نقد ابن تيمية للقضية للكلية الموجبة اليقينية ، في مادتها وصورتها . ولكن نحن هنا أمام نقد للبرهان عند المناطقة من حيث اشتراط وجود هذه القضية فيه . وهنا نستعيد إلى حد ما : بعض نقده مرتبطاً هنا بالعملية القياسية . أما البديهيات أو الضروريات أو ما يسميها ابن تيمية بالعقليات المحضة فلا نحتاج فيها إلى العملية القياسية ، وذلك إذا سلمنا بوجود القضية الكلية كحقيقة منطقية عقلية . إذا توصلنا إلى القضية الكلية فيها وتكونت ، فلسنا في حاجة إلى نظم قياسي للتوصل إلى نتيجة . يقول ابن تيمية « ما من قضية من هذه القضايا الكلية التي تجعل مقدمة في البرهان ، إلا والعلم بالنتيجة ممكن
2- Critique of the requirement of a universal affirmative proposition in the categorical syllogism: Ibn Taymiyya holds that the Aristotelian logicians affirm that theoretical certain sciences are only obtained by demonstration. And that demonstration, which is a 'categorical syllogism', must contain a universal affirmative proposition. Without this, we do not reach certain knowledge. We have already spoken in the previous chapter about Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the certain universal affirmative proposition, in its matter and form. But here we are facing a critique of demonstration according to the logicians in terms of the requirement of this proposition in it. Here we recall to some extent: some of his critique is related here to the syllogistic process. As for self-evident or necessary propositions, or what Ibn Taymiyya calls pure rationalities, we do not need the syllogistic process in them, that is if we concede the existence of the universal proposition as a rational logical truth. If we arrive at the universal proposition in them and it is formed, we have no need for a syllogistic arrangement to reach a conclusion. Ibn Taymiyya says: 'There is no proposition among these universal propositions that is made a premise in a demonstration except that the knowledge of the conclusion is possible'
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the Aristotelian requirement that a demonstrative syllogism (burhan) must include a universal affirmative premise. The author begins by stating Ibn Taymiyya's position: the Aristotelians claim that certain theoretical sciences are only attained through demonstration, and demonstration, being a categorical syllogism (qiyas shumuli), necessarily contains a universal affirmative proposition (qadiyya kulliyya mujiba). Without it, certain knowledge is not reached. The author then notes that this topic was previously discussed regarding the matter and form of the universal affirmative proposition. Now the focus is on the requirement of such a proposition within demonstration. Ibn Taymiyya argues that for self-evident (badihiyyat) or necessary (daruriyyat) propositions—which he calls pure rationalities ('aqliyyat mahda)—the syllogistic process is unnecessary. This is provided that one concedes the existence of the universal proposition as a rational logical truth. If we have already arrived at and formed the universal proposition, we do not need a syllogistic arrangement to derive the conclusion. The passage ends with a quote from Ibn Taymiyya: every universal proposition that is made a premise in a demonstration is such that knowledge of the conclusion is already possible (presumably without the syllogism). This suggests that for Ibn Taymiyya, the universal proposition itself already contains the conclusion, so the syllogistic form is superfluous for certain knowledge.

القياس الشمولي
العقليات المحضة

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Universal Premise Requirement
Ibn Taymiyya criticizes the universal premise requirement in demonstration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument against the necessity of universal affirmative premises in demonstration and evaluate its epistemological grounds.

Core Idea
Universal premises alone suffice for knowledge; formal syllogism is unnecessary.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the Aristotelian logicians' claim that certain knowledge requires demonstration (burhan) containing a universal affirmative premise. He argues that once we know the universal proposition, we directly know the particular conclusions without needing to arrange a syllogism. This lesson walks through his critique and its implications for the theory of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target doctrine
Find the claim that demonstration must contain a universal affirmative proposition (qadiyya kulliyya mujiba). In the Arabic, look for the phrase 'لا بد من وجود قضية كلية موجبة فيه'.
The target is the Aristotelian logicians.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's key objection
Ibn Taymiyya says: 'ما من قضية من هذه القضايا الكلية التي تجعل مقدمة في البرهان، إلا والعلم بالنتيجة ممكن بدون توسط ذلك البرهان'. This means that for any universal premise, the conclusion can be known without the mediation of demonstration.
He emphasizes that this is frequently the case.
3
Examine his example
He gives the example: if one knows that 'every one is half of every two', then one knows that 'this one is half of these two' directly, without a syllogism. Work through this example to see how the particular follows from the universal.
The example is from arithmetic, but the principle applies generally.
4
Determine the epistemological basis
Ibn Taymiyya distinguishes between self-evident truths ('aqliyyat mahda) and derived knowledge. He argues that for self-evident universals, syllogistic arrangement is redundant.
He does not deny that syllogisms can be valid, only that they are necessary.
5
Assess the scope of the critique
Note that Ibn Taymiyya does not reject syllogistic reasoning entirely; he rejects the claim that formal syllogisms are necessary for all certain knowledge. He allows that some knowledge may require inference, but not all.
This nuance is often missed.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

They differ even in this: some use what the logicians called 'syllogism of cause' or 'demonstration of cause' or 'demonstration of why' – that is, mentioning the answer to 'why' – and some use 'syllogism of indication' or 'demonstration of indication' or 'demonstration that'. The 'demonstration that' is the demonstration of the evidence that the major term exists for the minor without explaining its cause. People's mental powers vary and differ in this, so some use this demonstration and some use that. There is no law that imposes one way to capture the essentials of a thing or what is necessary for it, just as this last division into essentials and accidents is unfounded. Ibn Taymiyya laid the foundations of the philosophy of relativity clearly in this critique of the nature of knowledge. Our knowledge varies and reaches us through different paths and ways. We saw in the previous chapter, while he criticizes the matter of the proposition, that he affirms that some assents reach some people as sensory, empirical, and mutawatir, while they reach others through reflection and inference. 2. Critique of requiring a universal affirmative proposition in the universal syllogism: Ibn Taymiyya holds that the Aristotelian logicians affirm that certain theoretical sciences are only obtained through demonstration. And that demonstration – which is a 'universal syllogism' – must contain a universal affirmative proposition. Without this we do not reach certain knowledge. We have already spoken in the previous chapter about Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the certain universal affirmative proposition, in its matter and form. But here we are faced with a critique of the logicians' demonstration in terms of requiring this proposition in it. And here we retrieve to some extent: some of his critique is connected here to the syllogistic process. As for the self-evident or necessary truths, or what Ibn Taymiyya calls 'pure rationalities', we do not need the syllogistic process in them, if we concede that the universal proposition exists as a rational logical truth. If we arrive at the universal proposition in them and it is formed, we are not in need of a syllogistic arrangement to reach a conclusion. Ibn Taymiyya says: 'There is no universal proposition among these universal propositions that is made a premise in demonstration, except that knowledge of the conclusion is possible without the mediation of that demonstration, indeed that frequently occurs. We are not in need of a middle term and the placing of two premises together to reach a conclusion.' He says: 'If one knows that every one is half of every two, and that every two has half which is one, then he knows that this one is half of these two, and so on in all particular cases, and that without inference – i.e., without placing the mental process in an Aristotelian syllogistic form.'
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the Aristotelian requirement that demonstration (burhan) must contain a universal affirmative premise. The author begins by noting a diversity among logicians regarding the types of demonstration (burhan 'illa vs. burhan dalala), and then shifts to Ibn Taymiyya's main point: that universal premises are not needed for certain knowledge because the conclusion is already known once the universal is known. Ibn Taymiyya argues that self-evident truths ('aqliyyat mahda) do not require syllogistic arrangement; for example, if one knows that every one is half of every two, the particular conclusion that 'this one is half of these two' is known directly without a syllogism. This undermines the Aristotelian claim that demonstration is necessary for certain theoretical science. Ibn Taymiyya's critique is epistemological: he challenges the necessity of the formal syllogistic structure for attaining certainty, emphasizing that knowledge can be immediate and that the universal premise itself suffices. The author frames this as Ibn Taymiyya laying foundations for a 'philosophy of relativity' in knowledge.

القضية الكلية الموجبة
العقليات المحضة

Ibn Taymiyya's critique challenges the necessity of formal syllogism for certain knowledge, arguing that universal premises themselves are sufficient for immediate knowledge of particulars.

Innate Reasoning: Natural Capacity for Logical Inference
Ibn Taymiyya on innate reasoning and the redundancy of formal logical terminology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's argument that humans reason naturally using logical forms without formal terminology, and identify the three forms he mentions.

Core Idea
Innate reasoning (fitra) suffices; formal logical terminology is a supplement, not a necessity.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya challenges the claim that valid reasoning requires the technical apparatus of Greek logic. He insists that human minds are naturally disposed to infer from evidence to conclusions, using forms like universal-to-particular, particular-to-universal, and particular-to-particular, without ever naming them. This lesson unpacks that claim and its implications for Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three inference forms
Ibn Taymiyya states that people infer by qiyas al-shumul (categorical syllogism), istiqra' (induction), and qiyas al-tamthil (analogy). These correspond to inferring from universal to particular, particular to universal, and particular to particular.
These are the basic forms of reasoning recognized in Islamic logic.
2
Note the absence of formal terminology
He emphasizes that people do this 'without formulating their evidences and expressions in the forms of inference'—i.e., without using the technical language of logicians.
3
Contrast with logicians' claim
Ibn Taymiyya asserts that Muslims' expressions are 'better than the expressions of the logicians, clearer in reason, more concise in wording, while the meaning is the same.' This directly contradicts the idea that formal terminology improves reasoning.
He is critiquing the necessity of Greek logical formalism.
4
Analyse the example
He gives two equivalent expressions: a formal syllogism ('This is a human, and every human is created') and a simple entailment statement ('This is a human, and humanity entails these judgments'). Both convey the same inference.
This shows that the logical relationship, not the form, is what matters.
5
Conclude the argument
Ibn Taymiyya concludes that humans naturally arrive at the methods of Greek logic through their own innate capacity (fitra). Formal terminology is not required for valid reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثانياً ـ معرفة المسلمين لصور الإستدلال بفطرتهم . يرى ابن تيمية أن الناس يستدلون بقياس الشمول وبالاستقراء وبقياس التمثيل أي يستدلون بالكلي على الجزئي وبالجزئي على الكلي وبالجزئي على الجزئي . ولكن بدون أن تصوغ أدلتها وعبارتها في صور الإستدلال . يقول « إن أذهان بني آدم تستدل بالأدلة على المدلولات . وإن لم يعبروا عن ذلك بالعبارات المبينة لما في نفوسهم . وقد يعبرون بعبارات مبينة لمعانيهم ، وإن لم يسلكوا اصطلاح طائفة معينة من أهل الكلام ولا المنطق ولا غيرهم » . بل إن عبارت المسلمين خير من عبارات المناطقة وأبين في العقل وأوجز في اللفظ ، والمعنى واحد ، ومن الأمثلة على هذا « إذا قلت هذا إنسان وكل إنسان مخلوق أو حيوان أو حساس أو متحرك بالإرادة ، فإن شئت صورت الدليل على هذه الصورة ، وإن شئت قلت هذا إنسان، والإنسانية مستلزمة لهذه الأحكام »(۲) . . فالمسلمون إذن توصلوا في عباراتهم الخاصة إلى طرق المنطق اليوناني ويظهر هذا في ما يسمى أدلة وأمارات . يقول ابن تيمية « والفقهاء يسمون الجميع دليلاً . ومن أهل الكلام من لا يسمي بالدليل إلا الأول ثم الضابط في الدليل أن يكون مستلزماً للمدلول ، فكلما كان مستلزماً لغيره ، أمكن أن يستدل به عليه . فإن كان التلازم من الطرفين ، أمكن أن يستدل بكل منهما على الآخر . فيستدل بما علمه منها على الآخر الذي لا يعلمه » ويرى ابن تيمية أن الفقهاء يقررون أنه إن كان اللزوم قطعياً ، كان الدليل قطعياً ، وإن كان ظاهراً ـ ولكنه قد يتخلف ـ كان الدليل ظنياً . ومن (۱) ابن تيمية : الرد ص ۱۱۸ - السيوطي : صون ص ۲۲۳ . (۲) السيوطي : صون . ص ۲۹۰ . ٢٤٦
Second: Muslims' knowledge of forms of inference by their innate disposition. Ibn Taymiyya holds that people infer by categorical syllogism (qiyas al-shumul), by induction, and by analogical reasoning (qiyas al-tamthil)—i.e., they infer from the universal to the particular, from the particular to the universal, and from the particular to the particular—but without formulating their evidences and expressions in the forms of inference. He says: 'The minds of the sons of Adam infer by evidences to the things evidenced, even if they do not express that with expressions that clarify what is in their souls. And they may express with expressions that clarify their meanings, even if they do not follow the terminology of a particular group of theologians, nor of logicians, nor of others.' Rather, the expressions of the Muslims are better than the expressions of the logicians, clearer in reason, more concise in wording, while the meaning is the same. Examples of this: 'If you say "This is a human, and every human is created or animal or sentient or volitionally moving," if you wish you formulate the evidence in this form, and if you wish you say "This is a human, and humanity entails these judgments."' So the Muslims thus, in their own expressions, have arrived at the methods of Greek logic, and this appears in what is called evidence and indicators. Ibn Taymiyya says: 'The jurists call all of it "dalil" (evidence). Some theologians do not call by "dalil" except the first one. Then the principle of dalil is that it entails the thing evidenced. Whenever it entails something else, it is possible to infer from it to that thing. If the entailment is from both sides, it is possible to infer from each of them to the other. So one infers from what he knows of them to the other that he does not know.' Ibn Taymiyya holds that the jurists affirm that if the entailment is certain, the evidence is certain; and if it is apparent but may fall short, the evidence is presumptive.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's second main argument: that Muslims possess an innate (fitri) ability to reason using various logical forms—such as universal-to-particular (qiyas al-shumul), particular-to-universal (induction), and particular-to-particular (analogy)—without needing to frame their arguments in the technical language of logicians. Ibn Taymiyya quotes himself (or a source) to emphasize that human minds naturally infer from evidence to conclusions, even without explicit articulation; and even when they do articulate, they may use clear expressions without adhering to the terminology of any specific school (kalam, logic, etc.). He further claims that Muslims' expressions are superior—clearer in reason and more concise in wording—while conveying the same logical content. He provides an example: one can either say 'This is a human, and every human is created' (a categorical syllogism) or simply say 'This is a human, and humanity entails these judgments' (using entailment). The point is that the meaning is identical. Consequently, Muslims have independently arrived at the methods of Greek logic through their own terms, using what they call 'adilla' (evidences) and 'amarat' (indicators). Ibn Taymiyya then clarifies the juristic concept of 'dalil': jurists broadly call any indicator a 'dalil,' while some theologians restrict it to certain types. The defining feature is that a dalil must entail (istilzam) its conclusion. If entailment is reciprocal, inference can go both ways. Finally, he notes that jurists grade evidence: if the entailment is certain (qat'i), the evidence yields certainty; if only apparent (zahir) but possibly fallible, the evidence yields presumption (zann). This underscores Ibn Taymiyya's pragmatic, non-formalistic approach to logic.

Key Terms
فطرة
قياس الشمول
الاستقراء
قياس التمثيل

Key Takeaway
Humans naturally reason using logical forms; formal terminology is not essential for valid inference.

Defining Dalil: Entailment and Certainty in Islamic Jurisprudence
Ibn Taymiyya defines 'dalil' as entailment and explains its gradation in certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'dalil' according to Ibn Taymiyya as entailment (istilzam) and distinguish between certain (qat'i) and presumptive (zanni) evidence based on the strength of entailment.

Core Idea
A 'dalil' is defined by entailment, not by form; its certainty depends on the strength of that entailment.

Ibn Taymiyya clarifies the juristic concept of dalil: any evidence that entails its conclusion qualifies, regardless of whether it fits a formal syllogism. He also explains how jurists grade evidence by the certainty of the entailment. This lesson breaks down his definition and its application in Islamic legal theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining feature of a dalil
Ibn Taymiyya states: 'Then the principle of dalil is that it entails the thing evidenced.' The core criterion is istilzam (entailment), not a particular logical form.
2
Understand reciprocal entailment
If entailment is mutual (two-way), inference can go from either side to the other. This allows flexibility in reasoning.
Example: if A entails B and B entails A, knowing A proves B and vice versa.
3
Note the juristic vs theological usage
Ibn Taymiyya notes that jurists (fuqaha') call all such indicators 'dalil', while some theologians restrict the term to certain types. He adopts the broader juristic view.
4
Distinguish certainty levels
If the entailment is certain (qat'i), the evidence is certain. If it is apparent but may fall short (zanni), the evidence is presumptive. This gradation is crucial for legal rulings.
Certainty in entailment determines the epistemic weight of the dalil.
5
Apply to a concrete example
Consider the earlier example: 'This is a human, and humanity entails being created.' The entailment from 'human' to 'created' is certain (qat'i), so the evidence is certain. But if the entailment were only apparent (e.g., from clouds to rain), the evidence would be presumptive.
This illustrates how the same structure can yield different certainty levels.

(۲) السيوطي : صون . ص ۲۹۰ . ٢٤٦
Second: Muslims' knowledge of forms of inference by their innate disposition. Ibn Taymiyya holds that people infer by categorical syllogism (qiyas al-shumul), by induction, and by analogical reasoning (qiyas al-tamthil)—i.e., they infer from the universal to the particular, from the particular to the universal, and from the particular to the particular—but without formulating their evidences and expressions in the forms of inference. He says: 'The minds of the sons of Adam infer by evidences to the things evidenced, even if they do not express that with expressions that clarify what is in their souls. And they may express with expressions that clarify their meanings, even if they do not follow the terminology of a particular group of theologians, nor of logicians, nor of others.' Rather, the expressions of the Muslims are better than the expressions of the logicians, clearer in reason, more concise in wording, while the meaning is the same. Examples of this: 'If you say "This is a human, and every human is created or animal or sentient or volitionally moving," if you wish you formulate the evidence in this form, and if you wish you say "This is a human, and humanity entails these judgments."' So the Muslims thus, in their own expressions, have arrived at the methods of Greek logic, and this appears in what is called evidence and indicators. Ibn Taymiyya says: 'The jurists call all of it "dalil" (evidence). Some theologians do not call by "dalil" except the first one. Then the principle of dalil is that it entails the thing evidenced. Whenever it entails something else, it is possible to infer from it to that thing. If the entailment is from both sides, it is possible to infer from each of them to the other. So one infers from what he knows of them to the other that he does not know.' Ibn Taymiyya holds that the jurists affirm that if the entailment is certain, the evidence is certain; and if it is apparent but may fall short, the evidence is presumptive.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's second main argument: that Muslims possess an innate (fitri) ability to reason using various logical forms—such as universal-to-particular (qiyas al-shumul), particular-to-universal (induction), and particular-to-particular (analogy)—without needing to frame their arguments in the technical language of logicians. Ibn Taymiyya quotes himself (or a source) to emphasize that human minds naturally infer from evidence to conclusions, even without explicit articulation; and even when they do articulate, they may use clear expressions without adhering to the terminology of any specific school (kalam, logic, etc.). He further claims that Muslims' expressions are superior—clearer in reason and more concise in wording—while conveying the same logical content. He provides an example: one can either say 'This is a human, and every human is created' (a categorical syllogism) or simply say 'This is a human, and humanity entails these judgments' (using entailment). The point is that the meaning is identical. Consequently, Muslims have independently arrived at the methods of Greek logic through their own terms, using what they call 'adilla' (evidences) and 'amarat' (indicators). Ibn Taymiyya then clarifies the juristic concept of 'dalil': jurists broadly call any indicator a 'dalil,' while some theologians restrict it to certain types. The defining feature is that a dalil must entail (istilzam) its conclusion. If entailment is reciprocal, inference can go both ways. Finally, he notes that jurists grade evidence: if the entailment is certain (qat'i), the evidence yields certainty; if only apparent (zahir) but possibly fallible, the evidence yields presumption (zann). This underscores Ibn Taymiyya's pragmatic, non-formalistic approach to logic.

Key Terms
دليل
استلزام
قطعي
ظني

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what is the defining feature of a 'dalil'?
A) It must follow the syllogistic form of the logicians.
B) It must be a universal proposition.
C) It must entail the thing evidenced.
D) It must be certain (qat'i).

Key Takeaway
A dalil is any indicator that entails its conclusion; the strength of the entailment determines the certainty of the evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique: Form vs Material Entailment
Ibn Taymiyya argues that formal syllogism is unnecessary and irrelevant for real knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that formal syllogistic structure is neither necessary nor sufficient for genuine knowledge, and distinguish his concept of material entailment (luzum) from formal validity.

Core Idea
Formal syllogism is either redundant or useless; real proof lies in material entailment, not shape.

Ibn Taymiyya's critique goes to the heart of the claim that syllogistic form guarantees knowledge. He argues that the Aristotelian syllogism adds nothing: what you know via it you already know without it, and what you cannot know without it, it cannot give you. Instead, true evidence (dalil) is defined by Muslim theologians as any necessary condition that leads to its consequent—regardless of number of premises or formal arrangement. This passage challenges the very foundation of formal logic and redirects attention to material entailment (luzum) as the real basis of proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the thesis
Locate the opening claim: 'Everything that can be known by the Aristotelian syllogism can be known without it, and what cannot be known without it cannot be known by it.' This is the core of Ibn Taymiyya's rejection: the syllogism is either unnecessary or powerless.
Notice the directness of the claim; it frames the entire critique.
2
Trace the definition of dalil
Find the passage where Ibn Taymiyya cites the Muslim theologians' definition of 'dalil' (evidence) as anything that guides to what is sought through correct reflection, and that any necessary condition (malzum) is evidence for its consequent (lazim). Contrast this with the logicians' requirement of two premises.
Ibn Taymiyya uses this definition to argue that the number of premises is irrelevant.
3
Understand the critique of universals
Read where Ibn Taymiyya says the syllogism only yields knowledge of universals, while knowledge of particulars is more evident and does not require the 'comprehensive syllogism.' This undermines the logicians' claim that syllogism is necessary for any certain knowledge.
He reverses the priority: particulars are often more certain than the universal rules applied to them.
4
Distinguish form from matter
Identify the section where Ibn Taymiyya criticizes logicians for focusing exclusively on form, neglecting material truth. Then note his alternative: the real basis of proof is material entailment (luzum) — recognizing that one thing necessarily implies another, whether or not this is cast in formal syllogistic shape.
Material entailment is about real-world connections, not abstract validity.

لا بد له من كذا أو أنه إذا كان كذا . . . فقد علم اللزوم . ويعطي(۲)
They mentioned from the forms of syllogism and its matter. Everything that can be known by the Aristotelian syllogism can be known without this syllogism, and what cannot be known without the syllogism cannot be known by this syllogism. 'Thus in their syllogism there is neither the acquisition of knowledge of the unknown that is not known without it, nor any need for it for what can be known without it, so it became ineffective in knowledge, in existence or non-existence.' Everything in it is therefore prolongation, strain of thought, and waste of time. Rather, Ibn Taymiyya considers it 'mental delirium' and states that whenever non-natural methods are imposed on the natural disposition, souls are tortured with no benefit. What is required from evidences and proofs is 'the clarification of knowledge and the clarification of the paths leading to knowledge.' These paths should be straight; a person should tread the clear, known, straight path according to the need of the one seeking evidence. But if there is assigned to him 'someone who makes him take devious paths, i.e., taking a non-straight way, such that he takes him on circular paths and leads him on crooked courses, then he becomes very fatigued until he reaches the straight path—if he reaches it—otherwise he may reach something other than what is sought.' So the evidence and proof according to all Muslim theoreticians is that which guides us to what is sought and brings us to the intended goal; everything that entails something else is used as evidence for it. Therefore the theologians, especially the Mu'tazila, defined 'evidence' as that about which correct reflection leads to knowledge or opinion. Some of them agreed that what leads to knowledge is evidence and what leads to opinion is a sign. The theologians almost unanimously agree that everything that entails something else, such that it is a necessary condition for it, is evidence and proof for it, whether both are existential or non-existential, or one is existential and the other non-existential, or one is non-existential and the other existential. The evidence is always the necessary condition for the evidenced, and the evidenced is a logical consequent of the evidence. Therefore it is absolutely meaningless, as we said before, to require the existence of two premises in the evidence. He had already mentioned Ibn Taymiyya's objection to this in the previous negative context. But here he confirms it emphatically, and holds that evidence may be a single premise; when we know it we know what is sought, and we may need more, and there is no fixed limit that is equal for all people in all sought matters. They differ and vary according to the knowledge of the seeker of evidence about the conditions of the sought, the evidence, and the consequences and necessary conditions thereof. Then Ibn Taymiyya proceeds to criticize the syllogism in its foundation. According to the logicians, it only leads to knowledge of universal matters and does not lead to knowledge of any particular thing, i.e., particulars. Ibn Taymiyya refutes and says: knowledge of particulars is more precise, easier, and more demonstrative and establishes knowledge. Knowledge of its particulars is possible without the 'comprehensive syllogism.' He says: 'Knowledge of concrete things may be more evident than knowledge of universals.' 'And Ibn Taymiyya criticizes the formal aspect of the syllogism, and holds that the logicians confine themselves to adhering to this aspect, completely neglecting the matter, and do not care about its soundness or unsoundness.' Ibn Taymiyya holds that what is sought is knowledge, and the path to it is the evidence. So whoever knows the evidence for what he seeks knows what he seeks, whether he organizes it in the form of a syllogism or not. And whoever does not know its evidence, a syllogism or other will not benefit him. It is ignorance to say that by the syllogism we know sound evidences from unsound ones. There is nothing in the rules of syllogism except the form and shape of the evidence. The syllogism does not address the matter by affirmation or negation, i.e., whether a particular evidence entails its evidenced. There is nothing in the syllogism that investigates the soundness of any of the premises or their unsoundness. It only investigates the form alone. Whereas Ibn Taymiyya holds that the true reality considered in every proof and evidence in the world is material entailment. So whoever knows that this is a necessary consequence of that, he infers from the necessary condition to the consequent, even if he does not utter the word 'entailment' or conceive its meaning. Rather, whoever knows that such-and-such must have such-and-such, or that if it is such-and-such ... then he knows the entailment. And he gives...
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage is a critical exposition of Ibn Taymiyya's argument against the Aristotelian syllogism as presented in Manahij Bahth. The author begins by stating Ibn Taymiyya's claim that the syllogism is either redundant or useless: anything knowable through it is also knowable without it, and what is unknowable without it is also unknowable through it. Hence the syllogism has no effect on knowledge at all; it is mere prolongation, mental strain, and waste of time. Ibn Taymiyya even calls it 'mental delirium.' The core of his critique is that imposing non-natural methods on the natural human disposition (fitra) only tortures the soul without benefit. The true purpose of evidences (adilla) and proofs (barahin) is to clarify knowledge and the paths leading to it. These paths must be straight and clear, not the convoluted and devious paths that formal syllogistic logic forces one to take. The author then notes that, according to all Muslim theoreticians (nuzzar), a 'dalid' (evidence) is anything that guides to what is sought and entails something else. The Mu'tazila defined it as that about which correct reflection leads to knowledge or opinion; some distinguished between 'dalid' (leading to knowledge) and 'amara' (leading to opinion). There is a consensus that any necessary condition (malzum) for something is evidence for that thing (lazim), regardless of existential or non-existential status. Therefore, it is meaningless to insist that evidence always consist of two premises; Ibn Taymiyya affirms that evidence may be a single premise. The number of premises varies according to the knowledge of the seeker. Next, Ibn Taymiyya attacks the syllogism fundamentally: logicians claim it only yields knowledge of universals, not particulars. Ibn Taymiyya counters that knowledge of particulars is actually more precise, easier, and more demonstrative; it does not require the 'comprehensive syllogism' (al-qiyas al-shumuli). He asserts that knowledge of concrete things can be more evident than knowledge of universals. Finally, he criticizes the formal aspect of the syllogism. Logicians focus exclusively on form, neglecting material content. But what truly matters is material entailment (al-luzum al-madi): whether a particular piece of evidence actually entails its claimed conclusion. The syllogism's rules only address the shape of the argument, not the truth or falsehood of premises, nor whether a specific premise actually leads to the conclusion. Ibn Taymiyya insists that real proof and evidence depend on this material entailment. For example, if one knows that 'wine intoxicates' and 'every intoxicant is forbidden,' then the entailment between intoxication and prohibition is material. One does not need to formalize this into a syllogism; the mere recognition that A necessitates B is sufficient for knowledge. This critique undermines the logicians' claim that formal validity alone can distinguish sound arguments from unsound ones.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya argues that the formal syllogism is not the exclusive path to knowledge; real proof depends on material entailment (luzum) rather than syllogistic shape.

Demonstration -- Burhan
advanced
10 min
Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Syllogism and Defense of Analogy
Ibn Taymiyya compares comprehensive syllogism and analogy, concluding that analogy is stronger in most fields.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument for the superiority of analogical reasoning over the comprehensive syllogism in gaining knowledge of external reality, and identify the limited domains where demonstration (burhan) is valid.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya reverses the primacy of syllogism: analogy, not demonstration, is the foundation of certain knowledge about the external world.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya systematically evaluates the comprehensive syllogism (qiyas shumuli) and analogical reasoning (qiyas tamthili) across divine matters, natural sciences, mathematics, and astronomy. He concludes that demonstration (burhan) is only reliable when grounded in revelation or in purely mental abstractions, but for knowledge of existing particulars—the bedrock of human understanding—analogical reasoning, based on experience and sense perception, is superior and more certain. This directly challenges the Aristotelian-logician hierarchy that places categorical syllogism above analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the domains of knowledge discussed
Find the four fields Ibn Taymiyya explicitly evaluates: divine matters (ilahiyyat), natural sciences (tabi‘iyyat), mathematics (riyadiyyat), and astronomy (hay’a). Note his judgment for each.
He also mentions the broader category of 'external existing matters' as the domain where analogy excels.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's judgment on demonstration (burhan) in each domain
For divine matters, demonstration is useless without infallible revelation. For natural sciences, it yields only probable propositions. For mathematics, it is true but deals with mental abstractions, not external reality. For astronomy, some parts may be known by demonstration but most are not.
In each case, demonstration fails to give certain knowledge of external existence.
3
Locate the key epistemological premise
Ibn Taymiyya states that knowledge of specific individuals (juz’iyyat) is stronger and more apparent than knowledge through a universal proposition (kulli). This is the core of his argument.
This reverses the standard logical priority of universals over particulars.
4
Understand the conclusion on analogy vs. syllogism
Since analogical reasoning directly reaches particulars via experience and sense, it is more certain. The comprehensive syllogism depends on a universal premise that itself is grounded in analogy (through observation of similar individuals). Therefore analogy is the foundation of syllogism.
Ibn Taymiyya claims that logicians falsely consider syllogism certain and analogy probable, when in fact the reverse is true for external reality.
5
Assess the internal validity of the argument
Check whether Ibn Taymiyya's conclusion follows from his premises. If knowledge of particulars is indeed more certain than universal knowledge, then analogy—which builds from particulars—provides greater certainty. The argument is consistent within its own epistemological framework.
One might question whether universal knowledge is always less certain, or whether analogy itself relies on universal principles.

ويرى ابن تيمية أن هذين القياسين ـ القياس الشمولي والقياس التمثيلي قد ينتفع بهما ، إذا تلقيت بعض مقدماتها الكلية عن خبر المعصوم ، إذا استعملت في الإلهيات ـ بطريق الأولى ـ كما جاء به القرآن وقد رأينا من قبل كيف اعترف ابن تيمية بالقضية الكلية في نطاق الشرعيات وبدون هذا ، وبدون استمداد قضاياها الكلية من المعصوم مؤيدة بالقرآن فلا ينتفع بالبرهان في الإلهيات . كما أنه لا فائدة له علمية أو برهانية في الطبيعيات . وإنما يفيد قضايا عادية ، قد تنحرف فتكون من باب الأغلب . أما في الرياضيات فقد يفيد البرهان « وأما الرياضي المجرد عن المادة كالحساب والهندسة فهذا حق في نفسه ، ولكن ليس له معلوم في الخارج ، وإنما هو تقدير عدد ومقدار في النفس » فهذا البرهان إذن ـ وإن كان صحيحاً في ذاته ـ إلا أنه لا يوصل إلى حقيقة . إن الحقيقة هي ما تقوم على ما في الخارج ، وهذا البرهان الرياضي المجرد يقوم فقط على ما في النفس ، ولكن إذا انتهى البرهاني الهندسي ـ مثلاً ـ إلى التوصل إلى ـ علم الهيئة ـ أي علم صفات الأفلاك والكواكب وحركاتها ومقادير تلك الحركات ، أي أصبح علماً كمياً ـ فإن ما نصل إليه من هذا قد يعلم بعضه حقاً بالبرهان ، ولكن أكثره لا يعلم بالبرهان . ومابينهم فيه من الاختلاف كثير فالبرهان الرياضي إذن لا يوصل إلى معلومات تزكو بها النفس ، ولا يعلم به الأمور الموجودة المعلومة . إننا نصل إلى هذه الأمور الموجودة المعلومة بقياس التمثيل فقط (۱) . وهو الذي يقوم على التجربة والحس (۲) ويقرر ابن تيمية في وضوح أن قياس التمثيل أقوى وأكثر يقيناً من قياس الشمول وذلك أنه يصل إلى المفردات المعينة للقضية الكلية ، وأن العلم بالأفراد المعينة أقوى من العلم بالقضية الكلية « المقصود بهذه القضية الكلية إما أن يكون العلم بالموجود الخارجي ، أو العلم بالمقدرات الذهنية . أما الثاني ففائدته قليلة ، وأما الأول فما من موجود معين إلا وحكمه بعلم تعينه أظهر وأقوى من العلم به عن قياس كلي يتناوله ، فلا يحصل بالقياس كثير فائدة ، بل يكون ذلك طويلاً » فأساس المعرفة الإنسانية إذن هو معرفة الجزئي ، ونحن نصل إلى هذه المعرفة بالقياس التمثيلي ، فهو أصل القياس الشمولي ، وأصحاب هذا القياس الأخير يدعون خطأ أن التمثيل ظني ، والشمولي يقيني ، بينما يستند هذا الثاني على الأول (۳) .
And Ibn Taymiyya sees that these two types of reasoning—the comprehensive syllogism and the analogical syllogism—may be beneficial if some of their universal premises are received from the report of the infallible, when used in divine matters in the priority manner, as brought by the Quran. And we have seen previously how Ibn Taymiyya acknowledged the universal proposition in the context of religious rulings. Without this, and without deriving its universal premises from the infallible supported by the Quran, demonstration (burhan) is of no benefit in divine matters. Similarly, it has no scientific or demonstrative benefit in natural sciences. Rather, it yields ordinary propositions that may deviate and fall under the majority. As for mathematics, demonstration may be beneficial: 'As for mathematical abstraction from matter like arithmetic and geometry, this is true in itself, but it has no known external reality; rather it is an estimation of number and measure in the mind.' So this demonstration, even if correct in itself, does not lead to truth. Truth is what is based on external reality, and this abstract mathematical demonstration is based only on what is in the mind. But if the geometric demonstration, for example, leads to astronomy—i.e., the science of the properties of celestial spheres and planets and their motions and the magnitudes of those motions, i.e., becomes a quantitative science—then what we reach from this may be partially known truly by demonstration, but mostly it is not known by demonstration. And there is much disagreement among them. So mathematical demonstration does not lead to information that purifies the soul, nor does it make known the existing, known matters. We reach these existing, known matters only by analogical reasoning (Qiyas al-Tamthil), which is based on experience and sense (1)(2). And Ibn Taymiyya clearly states that analogical reasoning is stronger and more certain than comprehensive syllogism because it reaches the specific individuals of the universal proposition, and knowledge of specific individuals is stronger than knowledge of the universal proposition: 'The purpose of this universal proposition is either knowledge of external existence or knowledge of mental estimations. As for the second, its benefit is little; as for the first, there is no specific existing thing whose ruling knowledge of its specificity is more apparent and stronger than knowledge of it through a universal syllogism that includes it. So no great benefit is obtained by syllogism; rather, it is lengthy.' So the basis of human knowledge is the knowledge of the particular, and we reach this knowledge by analogical reasoning. Thus it is the origin of comprehensive syllogism, and the adherents of this latter claim falsely that analogy is probable and comprehensive is certain, while the second depends on the first (3).
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage compares the comprehensive syllogism (qiyas shumuli) and analogical reasoning (qiyas tamthili) across different domains. Ibn Taymiyya begins by noting that both can be beneficial in divine matters only if their universal premises are derived from the report of an infallible source (the Prophet) and supported by the Quran. Without this divine grounding, demonstration (burhan) is useless in theology. In natural sciences, demonstration yields no real benefit; it only gives ordinary, probable propositions. As for mathematics, demonstration is valid in itself (e.g., arithmetic and geometry) but deals only with mental abstractions, not external reality. When applied to astronomy (a quantitative science), some parts may be known by demonstration, but most are not, and there is much disagreement. Therefore, mathematical demonstration does not provide knowledge that purifies the soul or informs us about existing things. For knowledge of external, known matters, we rely solely on analogical reasoning, which is based on experience and sense perception. Ibn Taymiyya then asserts that analogical reasoning is stronger and more certain than comprehensive syllogism because it directly reaches the specific individuals of a universal proposition. Knowledge of specific individuals is more apparent and stronger than knowledge through a universal syllogism. Hence, the foundation of human knowledge is knowledge of particulars, attained through analogy. Analogy is therefore the origin of the comprehensive syllogism, contrary to the logicians who mistakenly claim that analogy is probable and syllogism certain.

القياس الشمولي
القياس التمثيلي

Why does Ibn Taymiyya claim that analogical reasoning is the foundation of comprehensive syllogism?

B) Because the universal premise in a syllogism depends on analogy to establish similarity among individuals.
C) Because logicians say so.
D) Because analogy is easier to use.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya reverses the logicians' hierarchy: analogical reasoning, grounded in experience of particulars, is the foundation of certain knowledge about the external world, while comprehensive syllogism has limited utility and depends on analogy.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Demonstrative Syllogism
Author critiques demonstrative reasoning in three domains.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain Ibn Taymiyya's three arguments against the usefulness of demonstrative syllogism in divine, natural, and mathematical sciences.

Core Idea
Demonstrative syllogism is useless for knowing external reality; it only deals with mental constructs.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya launches a sweeping critique of demonstrative reasoning (burhan). He argues that in divine sciences, the premises come from revelation, so demonstration is redundant. In natural sciences, it yields only probabilistic generalizations. Even in mathematics, demonstration is abstract and does not inform us about the external world. Let us follow his argument carefully to understand why he considers demonstrative syllogism epistemologically limited.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three domains Ibn Taymiyya discusses
Find where he mentions divine sciences (ilahiyyat), natural sciences (tabi'iyyat), and mathematics (riyadiyyat).
He treats each domain separately.
2
Extract his criticism of demonstration in divine sciences
He says the universal premises are derived from the infallible source (al-ma'sum) and supported by the Quran, so demonstration is not needed.
3
Extract his criticism in natural sciences
He says demonstration yields only ordinary propositions that are probabilistic (al-aghall), not certain.
4
Extract his criticism in mathematics
He concedes that mathematical demonstration is valid in itself, but it has no known counterpart in external reality; it is only mental estimation.
The example from astronomy is added later.

وجوده وتحققه في الخارج . وليس كل ما يحكم به الإنسان على ما يقدره ويفرضه في ذهنه ، يكون حكماً صحيحاً على ما يوجد في الخارج ، ولا كل ما يمكن تصور الذهن له ، يكون له وجود في الخارج . بل إن الذهن نفسه قد والإمكان الخارجي . أما الإمكان الذهني . فهو بأن يعرض الشيء على الذهن ، فلا يعلم امتناعه . بل يقول ممكن هذا لا لعلمه بإمكانه ، بل لعدم علمه بامتناعه ، مع أن ذلك الشيء يكون ممتنعاً في الخارج . وأما . فالأقرب إلى
The derivation of its universal premises from the infallible [revelation] supported by the Quran, so demonstrative reasoning is not useful in divine sciences. Likewise, it has no scientific or demonstrative benefit in natural sciences. It only yields ordinary propositions that may deviate and be from the domain of the probable. As for mathematics, demonstration may be useful: 'As for the mathematical abstracted from matter, like arithmetic and geometry, this is true in itself, but it has no known [counterpart] in external reality; it is only an estimation of number and quantity in the mind.' So this demonstration—even if sound in itself—does not lead to truth. Truth is what is based on external reality, and this abstract mathematical demonstration is based only on what is in the mind. However, if the geometric demonstrator ends up reaching 'astronomy'—that is, the science of the properties of the celestial spheres, planets, their motions, and the magnitudes of those motions, i.e., it becomes a quantitative science—then what we reach from this may be truly known through demonstration in part, but most of it is not known through demonstration. Their disagreements about it are many. Hence mathematical demonstration does not lead to information that purifies the soul, nor are the known existing things known through it. We reach these known existing things only through analogical reasoning, which is based on experience and sense. Ibn Taymiyya clearly states that analogical reasoning is stronger and more certain than universal syllogism because it reaches the specific individuals of the universal proposition, and knowledge of specific individuals is stronger than knowledge of the universal proposition. 'What is intended by this universal proposition is either knowledge of the external existent or knowledge of mental estimations. As for the second, its benefit is little. As for the first, there is no specific existent except that its judgment through knowledge of its specificity is more apparent and stronger than knowledge of it through a universal syllogism that includes it, so no great benefit is obtained through syllogism; rather it is lengthy.' So the foundation of human knowledge is knowledge of the particular, and we reach this knowledge through analogical reasoning. It is the origin of universal syllogism. The proponents of this latter claim, erroneously, that analogy is probabilistic and universal syllogism is certain, while the latter depends on the former. If the universal syllogism is not based on analogical reasoning, it has no real domain. This is what logicians call mental possibility and external possibility. Accordingly, the logicians, following this division that is not based on practical benefit, conceive of mental existence and mental possibility. They conceive of an essence abstracted by itself, as it is in itself, without being established in external reality or in the mind—'man as such,' 'existence as such,' 'blackness as such'—and they suppose that this essence abstracted from all positive and negative restrictions is realized in external reality. This is the Aristotelian view, and Ibn Taymiyya sees it as completely mistaken, no less mistaken than Platonic idealism in its abstraction of Forms or the later Pythagoreanism in its abstraction of number. It is not necessary that everything in the mind is realized in external reality. How many assumptions in the mind are never realized in external reality? The assumption of impossibilities in the mind is possible: one can assume an existent that is neither necessary nor possible, neither self-subsistent nor subsistent through another, neither distinct from another nor adjacent to it. All this is possible to assume in the mind, but not everything that the mind assumes can exist and be realized in external reality. Nor is every judgment that a person makes about what he estimates and assumes in his mind a correct judgment about what exists in external reality. Nor does everything that the mind can conceive have existence in external reality. Rather, the mind itself may conceive of things while knowing their impossibility in external reality, and without knowing their impossibility externally or their possibility externally. This is called 'mental possibility.' Ibn Taymiyya divides possibility—as I have said—into two types: mental possibility and external possibility. As for mental possibility, it is that something is presented to the mind, and one does not know its impossibility. Rather, he says 'this is possible' not because he knows its possibility, but because he does not know its impossibility, while that thing is impossible in external reality. As for external possibility... it is knowledge of the possibility of something in external reality. 'This is by knowing its existence in external reality, or the existence of its like, or the existence of what is more distant in existence than it. If the more distant from accepting existence exists, then it is possible in existence. So the closer to
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's critique of demonstrative reasoning (burhan) and his defense of analogical reasoning (qiyas al-tamthil) as the foundation of certain knowledge. The author begins by arguing that demonstration is useless in divine sciences (ilahiyyat) because their universal premises are derived from revelation. Similarly, in natural sciences (tabi'iyyat), demonstration yields only probabilistic, ordinary propositions. In mathematics, demonstration is valid in itself but does not lead to external truth; it deals only with mental estimations. Even when applied to astronomy, most conclusions are uncertain. True knowledge of external realities is achieved only through analogical reasoning, which is based on experience and sense perception. Ibn Taymiyya asserts that analogical reasoning is stronger and more certain than universal syllogism because it provides knowledge of specific individuals, which is more evident than knowledge of a universal proposition. The universal syllogism, if not grounded in analogy, lacks real applicability. The author then connects this critique to the logicians' distinction between mental and external possibility. Logicians erroneously assume that abstract essences (e.g., 'man as such') have external reality, an Aristotelian error akin to Platonic idealism. Ibn Taymiyya emphasizes that not every mental concept corresponds to external existence; the mind can conceive impossibilities. He defines mental possibility as ignorance of impossibility, not positive knowledge, while external possibility requires knowledge of actual existence or its like. This distinction undermines the logicians' reliance on abstract mental constructs.

الإلهيات
الطبيعيات
الرياضيات

According to Ibn Taymiyya, why is demonstrative syllogism not useful in divine sciences (ilahiyyat)?

C) Because the syllogism would lead to infinite regress.
D) Because there is no middle term in divine arguments.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya argues that demonstrative syllogism is useless for attaining knowledge of external realities in theology, nature, and even mathematics; it remains confined to the mind.
