Ibn Taymiyya's Defense of Analogical Reasoning and the Distinction of Possibility
Author defends analogical reasoning and distinguishes mental from external possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that analogical reasoning is more certain than universal syllogism and explain his distinction between mental and external possibility.

Core Idea
Analogical reasoning, grounded in experience, gives direct knowledge of particulars, making it more certain than abstract universal syllogism.

Ibn Taymiyya now argues that analogical reasoning (qiyas al-tamthil) is actually stronger and more certain than universal syllogism (qiyas al-shumul). He asserts that knowledge of specific individuals is more evident than knowledge of a universal proposition. He also introduces a crucial distinction between mental possibility and external possibility, arguing that logicians mistakenly assume that mental abstractions correspond to external realities. Let us examine these arguments step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ibn Taymiyya's claim about analogy vs. universal syllogism
He states that analogical reasoning is stronger and more certain (أقوى وأكثر يقيناً) because it reaches the specific individuals of the universal proposition.
2
Understand his epistemic hierarchy
He argues that knowledge of specific individuals (المفردات المعينة) is stronger than knowledge of the universal proposition that includes them.
This reverses the Aristotelian priority of universals.
3
Find the two types of possibility he distinguishes
He divides possibility into mental (al-imkan al-dhihni) and external (al-imkan al-khariji). Mental possibility is ignorance of impossibility; external possibility requires knowledge of actual existence or its like.
4
Understand his critique of abstract essences
He criticizes the logicians' notion of an essence abstracted from all restrictions (e.g., 'man as such') as a mental construct with no external reality, akin to Platonic Forms.

الرياضيات فقد يفيد البرهان « وأما الرياضي المجرد عن المادة كالحساب والهندسة فهذا حق في نفسه ، ولكن ليس له معلوم في الخارج ، وإنما هو تقدير عدد ومقدار في النفس » فهذا البرهان إذن ـ وإن كان صحيحاً في ذاته ـ إلا أنه لا يوصل إلى حقيقة . إن الحقيقة هي ما تقوم على ما في الخارج ، وهذا البرهان الرياضي المجرد يقوم فقط على ما في النفس ، ولكن إذا انتهى البرهاني الهندسي ـ مثلاً ـ إلى التوصل إلى ـ علم الهيئة ـ أي علم صفات الأفلاك والكواكب وحركاتها ومقادير تلك الحركات ، أي أصبح علماً كمياً ـ فإن ما نصل إليه من هذا قد يعلم بعضه حقاً بالبرهان ، ولكن أكثره لا يعلم بالبرهان . ومابينهم فيه من الاختلاف كثير فالبرهان الرياضي إذن لا يوصل إلى معلومات تزكو بها النفس ، ولا يعلم به الأمور الموجودة المعلومة . إننا نصل إلى هذه الأمور الموجودة المعلومة بقياس التمثيل فقط . وهو الذي يقوم على التجربة والحس ويقرر ابن تيمية في وضوح أن قياس التمثيل أقوى وأكثر يقيناً من قياس الشمول وذلك أنه يصل إلى المفردات المعينة للقضية الكلية ، وأن العلم بالأفراد المعينة أقوى من العلم بالقضية الكلية « المقصود بهذه القضية الكلية إما أن يكون العلم بالموجود الخارجي ، أو العلم بالمقدرات الذهنية . أما الثاني ففائدته قليلة ، وأما الأول فما من موجود معين إلا وحكمه بعلم تعينه أظهر وأقوى من العلم به عن قياس كلي يتناوله ، فلا يحصل بالقياس كثير فائدة ، بل يكون ذلك طويلاً » فأساس المعرفة الإنسانية إذن هو معرفة الجزئي ، ونحن نصل إلى هذه المعرفة بالقياس التمثيلي ، فهو أصل القياس الشمولي ، وأصحاب هذا القياس الأخير يدعون خطأ أن التمثيل ظني ، والشمولي يقيني ، بينما يستند هذا الثاني على الأول . أما إذا لم يستند القياس الشمولي على القياس التمثيلي ، فلن يكون له مجال واقعي . وهو ما يسميه المناطقة الإمكان الذهني والإمكان الخارجي ، فيتصور المناطقة تبعاً لهذا التقسيم الذي لا يقوم على منفعة عملية ، وجوداً ذهنياً وإمكاناً ذهنياً ، فيتصورون ماهية مجردة بنفسها من حيث هي هي من غير أن تكون ثابتة في الخارج أو في الذهن ، الإنسان من حيث هو هو ، والوجود من حيث هو هو والسواد من حيث هو هو ، ويظنون أن هذه الماهية المجردة عن جميع القيود الثبوتية والسلبية متحققة في الخارج . وهذا هو الرأي الأرسططاليسي ويرى ابن تيمية أنه خطأ تماماً ، لا يقل خطأ عن المثالية الأفلاطونية في تجريدها للمثل أو للفيثاغورية المتأخرة في تجريدها للعدد . انه ليس من المحتم أن كل ما في الذهن يتحقق في الخارج وكم في الذهن من مفروضات لا تتحقق أبداً في الخارج ، فافتراض الممتنعات في الذهن ممكن : يستطيع أن يفترض موجوداً لا واجباً ولا ممكناً ولا قائماً بنفسه ولا بغيره ، ولا مبايناً لغيره ولا مجانباً له ، كل هذا ممكن الافتراض في الذهن ولكن ليس كل ما يفرضه الذهن يمكن وجوده وتحققه في الخارج . وليس كل ما يحكم به الإنسان على ما يقدره ويفرضه في ذهنه ، يكون حكماً صحيحاً على ما يوجد في الخارج ، ولا كل ما يمكن تصور الذهن له ، يكون له وجود في الخارج . بل إن الذهن نفسه قد يتصور أشياء مع أنه يعلم بعدم إمكانها في الخارج ، ومع عدم علمه بامتناعها خارجياً وبإمكانها خارجياً . ويسمى هذا « الإمكان الذهني » . ويقسم ابن تيمية ـ كما قلت ـ الإمكان إلى نوعين : الإمكان الذهني والإمكان الخارجي . أما الإمكان الذهني . فهو بأن يعرض الشيء على الذهن ، فلا يعلم امتناعه . بل يقول ممكن هذا لا لعلمه بإمكانه ، بل لعدم علمه بامتناعه ، مع أن ذلك الشيء يكون ممتنعاً في الخارج . وأما الإمكان الخارجي . . . فهو العلم بإمكان الشيء في الخارج . « وهذا يكون بأن يعلم وجوده في الخارج ، أو وجود نظيره ، أو وجود ما هو أبعد في الوجود منه . فإذا كان الأبعد عن قبول الوجود موجوداً ، ممكن الوجود . فالأقرب إلى
The derivation of its universal premises from the infallible [revelation] supported by the Quran, so demonstrative reasoning is not useful in divine sciences. Likewise, it has no scientific or demonstrative benefit in natural sciences. It only yields ordinary propositions that may deviate and be from the domain of the probable. As for mathematics, demonstration may be useful: 'As for the mathematical abstracted from matter, like arithmetic and geometry, this is true in itself, but it has no known [counterpart] in external reality; it is only an estimation of number and quantity in the mind.' So this demonstration—even if sound in itself—does not lead to truth. Truth is what is based on external reality, and this abstract mathematical demonstration is based only on what is in the mind. However, if the geometric demonstrator ends up reaching 'astronomy'—that is, the science of the properties of the celestial spheres, planets, their motions, and the magnitudes of those motions, i.e., it becomes a quantitative science—then what we reach from this may be truly known through demonstration in part, but most of it is not known through demonstration. Their disagreements about it are many. Hence mathematical demonstration does not lead to information that purifies the soul, nor are the known existing things known through it. We reach these known existing things only through analogical reasoning, which is based on experience and sense. Ibn Taymiyya clearly states that analogical reasoning is stronger and more certain than universal syllogism because it reaches the specific individuals of the universal proposition, and knowledge of specific individuals is stronger than knowledge of the universal proposition. 'What is intended by this universal proposition is either knowledge of the external existent or knowledge of mental estimations. As for the second, its benefit is little. As for the first, there is no specific existent except that its judgment through knowledge of its specificity is more apparent and stronger than knowledge of it through a universal syllogism that includes it, so no great benefit is obtained through syllogism; rather it is lengthy.' So the foundation of human knowledge is knowledge of the particular, and we reach this knowledge through analogical reasoning. It is the origin of universal syllogism. The proponents of this latter claim, erroneously, that analogy is probabilistic and universal syllogism is certain, while the latter depends on the former. If the universal syllogism is not based on analogical reasoning, it has no real domain. This is what logicians call mental possibility and external possibility. Accordingly, the logicians, following this division that is not based on practical benefit, conceive of mental existence and mental possibility. They conceive of an essence abstracted by itself, as it is in itself, without being established in external reality or in the mind—'man as such,' 'existence as such,' 'blackness as such'—and they suppose that this essence abstracted from all positive and negative restrictions is realized in external reality. This is the Aristotelian view, and Ibn Taymiyya sees it as completely mistaken, no less mistaken than Platonic idealism in its abstraction of Forms or the later Pythagoreanism in its abstraction of number. It is not necessary that everything in the mind is realized in external reality. How many assumptions in the mind are never realized in external reality? The assumption of impossibilities in the mind is possible: one can assume an existent that is neither necessary nor possible, neither self-subsistent nor subsistent through another, neither distinct from another nor adjacent to it. All this is possible to assume in the mind, but not everything that the mind assumes can exist and be realized in external reality. Nor is every judgment that a person makes about what he estimates and assumes in his mind a correct judgment about what exists in external reality. Nor does everything that the mind can conceive have existence in external reality. Rather, the mind itself may conceive of things while knowing their impossibility in external reality, and without knowing their impossibility externally or their possibility externally. This is called 'mental possibility.' Ibn Taymiyya divides possibility—as I have said—into two types: mental possibility and external possibility. As for mental possibility, it is that something is presented to the mind, and one does not know its impossibility. Rather, he says 'this is possible' not because he knows its possibility, but because he does not know its impossibility, while that thing is impossible in external reality. As for external possibility... it is knowledge of the possibility of something in external reality. 'This is by knowing its existence in external reality, or the existence of its like, or the existence of what is more distant in existence than it. If the more distant from accepting existence exists, then it is possible in existence. So the closer to
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's critique of demonstrative reasoning (burhan) and his defense of analogical reasoning (qiyas al-tamthil) as the foundation of certain knowledge. The author begins by arguing that demonstration is useless in divine sciences (ilahiyyat) because their universal premises are derived from revelation. Similarly, in natural sciences (tabi'iyyat), demonstration yields only probabilistic, ordinary propositions. In mathematics, demonstration is valid in itself but does not lead to external truth; it deals only with mental estimations. Even when applied to astronomy, most conclusions are uncertain. True knowledge of external realities is achieved only through analogical reasoning, which is based on experience and sense perception. Ibn Taymiyya asserts that analogical reasoning is stronger and more certain than universal syllogism because it provides knowledge of specific individuals, which is more evident than knowledge of a universal proposition. The universal syllogism, if not grounded in analogy, lacks real applicability. The author then connects this critique to the logicians' distinction between mental and external possibility. Logicians erroneously assume that abstract essences (e.g., 'man as such') have external reality, an Aristotelian error akin to Platonic idealism. Ibn Taymiyya emphasizes that not every mental concept corresponds to external existence; the mind can conceive impossibilities. He defines mental possibility as ignorance of impossibility, not positive knowledge, while external possibility requires knowledge of actual existence or its like. This distinction undermines the logicians' reliance on abstract mental constructs.

الإمكان الخارجي
الماهية المجردة

A) It relies on revelation rather than reason.
B) It provides knowledge of specific individuals, which is more evident than universal propositions.
C) It avoids the need for a middle term.
D) It is used only in mathematics.

Key Takeaway
For Ibn Taymiyya, analogical reasoning grounded in experience is the foundation of certain knowledge, and the distinction between mental and external possibility shows that many logical abstractions have no real-world correspondence.

Ibn Taymiyya's Quranic Argument for External Possibility
Ibn Taymiyya argues Quran uses external possibility for resurrection and criticizes al-Amidi's conditional method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's critique of al-Amidi's conditional method and explain why external possibility is known through existence.

Core Idea
External possibility is proven by actual existence, not by mental abstraction.

Ibn Taymiyya's radical departure from Aristotelian logic lies in his insistence that possibility is grounded in reality, not in the mind. In this passage, he contrasts the Quran's method of citing historical events with the Ash'ari theologian al-Amidi's use of conditional propositions. You will learn to distinguish between external and mental possibility and to assess the strength of Ibn Taymiyya's critique.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of possibility
Ibn Taymiyya distinguishes between external possibility (al-imkan al-khariji) and mental possibility (al-imkan al-dhihni). External possibility is known through actual existence; mental possibility is abstract conceivability. Note how he defines each in the passage.
Ibn Taymiyya insists the Quran never uses mental possibility for proof.
2
Extract the Quranic examples
List the events Ibn Taymiyya cites: past resurrections, the man struck by the cow, the Companions of the Cave, arguments from first creation, the heavens/earth, and plants. These are concrete, externally verified events.
3
Analyze al-Amidi's conditional method
Al-Amidi and some Ash'aris prove possibility by saying: 'If we suppose this, no impossibility follows.' Ibn Taymiyya rejects this because the conditional is not intuitively known nor proven. He holds that only existence can establish possibility.
The commentator suggests Ibn Taymiyya may have misunderstood the conditional's role.
4
Evaluate the misunderstanding claim
The passage's author states Ibn Taymiyya misunderstood that non-impossibility equals external possibility and that the conditional's supposition transforms impossibility. Consider whether this critique is valid or if Ibn Taymiyya's point stands.
Focus on the epistemological ground: can we know non-impossibility without existence?

الوجود منه أولى . ويرى ابن تيمية أن هذه الطريقة الأخيرة هي طريقة مستمدة من القرآن في بيان إمكان المعاد : وهنا يتبين اتجاه ابن تيمية البارع في ربطه نقد المنطق الأرسططاليسي بعدم إمكان تطبيق هذا المنطق على إلهيات المسلمين . إن القرآن - في رأي ابن تيمية - لا يستخدم أبداً - فكرة الإمكان الذهني المجرد في اثبات عقائده . وإنما يستخدم الإمكان الخارجي . ويضع أمثلة معينة حاسمة . فيثبت إمكان المعاد . بالإمكان الخارجي المتحقق . ويعطي الأمثلة القرآنية على هذا : فقد أخبر الله عمن أماتهم ثم أحياهم . أو عن المضروب بالبقرة ، أو عن أصحاب الكهف . أو يستدل على ذلك بالنشأة الأولى . وأن الإعادة أهون من الابتداء أو الإستدلال بخلق السموات والأرض . فإن خلقها أعظم من اعادة الإنسان . أو الإستدلال بخلق النبات . ويورد ابن تيمية الآيات القرآنية . والقرآن هو السجل الحقيقي الواقعي للوجود . إن حوادثه المتحققة خارجاً تثبت المعاد إثباتاً كاملاً . ويذهب ابن تيمية إلى تقرير أن الإمكان الخارجي يعرف بالوجود لا بمجرد الإمتناع . ويهاجم الآمدي - متكلم الأشاعرة المشهور الذي يذهب هو وبعض متكلمي الأشاعرة - إذا أرادوا إثبات إمكان أمر من الأمور إلى القول بأنه « لو قدرنا هذا ، لم يلزم منه ممتنع » يرى ابن تيمية أن هذه القضية الشرطية غير معلومة . فإن كونه لا يلزم منه محذور . ليس معلوماً بالبديهة ، ولم يقم عليه دليل نظري . ولم يفهم ابن تيمية هنا موقف الآمدي والأشاعرة . لم يفهم أن عدم الإمتناع يساوي الإمكان الخارجي . وأن عدم الإمتناع هو فعلاً الوجود الخارجي ، ولم يفهم أيضاً الشرط في القضية « لو قدرنا هذا . لم يلزم منه ممتنع » إن وضع الشرط في التقدير يحيل الإمتناع أي يثبت تحقق الوجود الخارجي .
Existence is prior to it. Ibn Taymiyya holds that this last method is a method derived from the Quran in explaining the possibility of resurrection: here the brilliant direction of Ibn Taymiyya appears in his linking his critique of Aristotelian logic to the impossibility of applying this logic to the theologies of Muslims. The Quran – in Ibn Taymiyya's view – never uses the idea of abstract mental possibility in proving its doctrines. Rather, it uses external possibility. And it places specific decisive examples. Thus it proves the possibility of resurrection by actualized external possibility. And he gives Quranic examples of this: God informed of those He caused to die then resurrected them, or of the one struck with the cow, or of the Companions of the Cave. Or he infers that from the first creation, and that re-creation is easier than initiation, or inference by the creation of the heavens and the earth – for their creation is greater than the re-creation of man – or inference by the creation of plants. Ibn Taymiyya cites Quranic verses. The Quran is the true realistic record of existence. Its externally actualized events prove resurrection completely. Ibn Taymiyya proceeds to establish that external possibility is known by existence, not by mere non-necessity. He attacks al-Amidi – the famous Ash'ari theologian who, along with some Ash'ari theologians, when they want to prove the possibility of something, say: 'If we suppose this, no impossibility follows from it.' Ibn Taymiyya holds that this conditional proposition is unknown. For its being such that no harm follows from it is not known by intuition, nor has a theoretical proof been established for it. Ibn Taymiyya did not understand here the position of al-Amidi and the Ash'aris. He did not understand that non-impossibility equals external possibility, and that non-impossibility is actually external existence, and he did not understand the condition in the proposition 'If we suppose this, no impossibility follows from it.' The placing of the condition in supposition transforms impossibility, i.e., it establishes the actualization of external existence.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is a critical exposition of Ibn Taymiyya's methodology in refuting Aristotelian logic as applied to Islamic theology. The passage opens by stating Ibn Taymiyya's view that the Quran employs a method based on external possibility (al-imkan al-khariji) to establish doctrines like the resurrection, not abstract mental possibility (al-imkan al-dhihni). Ibn Taymiyya then lists Quranic examples: stories of those resurrected, the man struck by the cow (from Surah al-Baqarah), the Companions of the Cave, and arguments from first creation, the greater difficulty of creating the heavens and earth compared to man, and the creation of plants. For Ibn Taymiyya, the Quran is the real record of existence, and its externally actualized events prove resurrection fully. He then asserts that external possibility is known through actual existence, not mere lack of impossibility. He criticizes al-Amidi, who uses a conditional proposition of the form 'If we suppose this, no impossibility follows' to prove possibility. Ibn Taymiyya argues that such a conditional is not intuitively known nor theoretically proven. However, the author (of Manahij Bahth) remarks that Ibn Taymiyya misunderstood al-Amidi: non-impossibility equals external possibility, and the conditional's supposition actually transforms impossibility into actualized existence.

Key Terms
الإمكان الخارجي
الإمكان الذهني
القضية الشرطية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) By using abstract mental possibility arguments.
B) By citing historically verified external events.
C) By employing Aristotelian syllogisms.
D) By relying on the principle of non-impossibility.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique shows that for theological truths, possibility must be grounded in actual existence, not mere logical non-contradiction.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Demonstration: The Clear vs. the Unclear
Author explains Ibn Taymiyya's nominalist epistemology and his critique of syllogistic demonstration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's objection that using universal premises to prove particular conclusions violates the logical rule against explaining the clear by the unclear.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya inverts the epistemological hierarchy: particulars are clearer than universals, so demonstration fails.

Ibn Taymiyya launches a radical critique of Aristotelian demonstration (burhan). He challenges the very foundation of syllogistic logic: that universals can give certain knowledge of particulars. In this passage, he argues that logicians illegitimately assume universals are clearer than the individuals they are supposed to prove. This lesson unpacks his argument and its implications for Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ibn Taymiyya's epistemological starting point
He is a nominalist: only particulars exist externally. Universals are mental constructs with no guaranteed external correspondent. Find the sentence where he says logicians speak 'about things they do not know have a known external instance'.
This is the foundation of his critique.
2
Extract his critique of logicians' use of universals
Logicians claim to study universals but cannot provide examples. He accuses them of speaking without knowledge (bighayri 'ilm). Note his emphasis on the need for external instances (ma'lum fi al-kharij).
3
Understand his claim about inference direction
Ibn Taymiyya asserts that individuals of universals are more manifest than the universals themselves. Therefore, inferring from universals to individuals is inferring from the hidden (khafi) to the clear (jali) – the opposite of what demonstration should do.
This is his central logical objection.
4
Analyze his restriction of certain demonstration
He admits demonstration yields a certain universal proposition only if premises are self-evident (badihiyyat) whose contradictories are impossible. Find the list of non-self-evident fields he gives: natural, medical, grammatical. These are subject to refutation.
This limits burhan to a very narrow domain.
5
Recognize the contradiction he exposes
Logicians reject using the clear to define the unclear (in definition/hadd), but in demonstration they do exactly that – using hidden universals to prove clear particulars. This contradicts their own principles.
He marvels at this inconsistency.

هذه النزعة الإسمية الحسية من ناحية والعملية البراجماطيقية من ناحية تسود تفكير هذا المفكر العجيب ، فلا يرى موجوداً في الخارج سوى الجزئي ، ويرى أن ما في الذهن من خرافات الميتافيزيقا لا توصل إلى شيء . إن المناطقة يعجزون عن التمثيل عندهم ، ويدعون فقط أنهم يخوضون في الكليات ، وهم في الحق ، يتكلمون بغير علم « فيما لا يعلمون أن له معلوماً في الخارج ، بل فيما ليس له معلوم في الخارج ، وفيما قد يمتنع أن يكون له معلوم في الخارج . وإلا فالعلم بالأمور الموجودة ، إذا كان كلياً ، كان له معلومات ثابتة في الخارج »(۱) . ويقرر ابن تيمية أن « الاستدلال بالكليات على أفرادها هو استدلال بالخفي على الجلي » أو على « الأجلى بالأخفى » إن أفراد الكليات هي أشد جلاء ووضوحاً ، وهي كما يؤكد دائماً تعلم بقياس التمثيل . أما أن البرهان يفيد قضية كلية . فيعترف ابن تيمية بهذا . ولكنه يرى أنه يفيد قضية كلية يقينية ، إذا ما كان مؤلفاً من قضايا بديهية . وهي ما يسميها المقدمات اليقينية المحضة الواجب قبولها والتي يمتنع نقيضها . أما سوى ذلك كالقضايا الطبيعية والطبية والنحوية . فهذه ما يمكن نقضها كثيراً ولا يجزم العقل بانتقاضها إلا بشروط . ومن الثابت أن القضايا العادية أي التجريبية كثيراً ما تنقض . وإذا جاز انتقاض القضية الكلية لم تكن عند المناطقة مادة للبرهان ، بل يعتبرونها جدلية وخطابية . وتناسى المناطقة أن معظم القضايا التي في مجال الطاقة البشرية هي قضايا عادية ، هي قضايا التجربة فأي قيمة للبرهان إذن ؟ . أن يستند على قضايا كلية خفية أو أكثر خفاء من قضايا جزئية جلية أو أكثر جلاء(۲) ويعجب ابن تيمية أن المناطقة لا يقبلون في صناعة الحد أن يعرف الجلي بالخفي . ويعتبرون هذا عيباً منطقياً بينما هو في البرهان أشد عيباً . إن غاية البرهان بيان المدلول عليه وتعريفه وكشفه وإيضاحه . فإذا كان هذا المدلول « أوضح » ، كان عمل برهان المناطقة هو بياناً للجلي بالخفي . وهذا ما يناقض شروطهم المنطقية نفسها (۳) . ولكن بالرغم من هذا فإن ابن تيمية يعترف بأن المقدمات الخفية قد تنفع بعض الناس كما تنفع في المناظرة ، ويقرر أن الجلاء والخفاء من الأمور
This nominalist-empiricist tendency on one hand, and the pragmatic tendency on the other, dominate the thought of this strange thinker. So he sees in external reality only the particular, and he sees that what is in the mind from the myths of metaphysics leads to nothing. The logicians are unable to give examples, and they only claim that they delve into universals, while in truth they speak without knowledge 'about things they do not know have a known external [instance], rather about things that have no known external [instance], and things that may be impossible to have an external known [instance]. Otherwise, knowledge of existing things, if it is universal, has established known [instances] in external reality.' And Ibn Taymiyya asserts that 'inference from universals to their individuals is inference from the hidden to the clear' or 'from the clearer to the more hidden'. The individuals of universals are more manifest and clear, and they are, as he always affirms, learned by analogical reasoning (qiyas al-tamthil). As for demonstration yielding a universal proposition, Ibn Taymiyya acknowledges this. But he holds that it yields a certain universal proposition only if it is composed of self-evident premises, which he calls the purely certain premises that must be accepted and whose contradictories are impossible. As for other [premises] like natural, medical, and grammatical propositions, these can often be refuted, and the mind does not affirm their refutation except under conditions. It is established that ordinary, i.e., experiential, propositions are often refuted. And if a universal proposition can be refuted, it is not considered by the logicians as material for demonstration; rather they consider it dialectical and rhetorical. The logicians forget that most propositions within human capability are ordinary, experiential propositions. So what value then for demonstration? That it relies on universal propositions that are hidden or more hidden than clear particular propositions or clearer ones. And Ibn Taymiyya is amazed that the logicians do not accept in the art of definition (hadd) that the clear be known by the unclear, and they consider this a logical flaw, while in demonstration it is even more flawed. The purpose of demonstration is to make the indicated object clear, define it, reveal it, and explain it. If this indicated object is 'clearer', then the work of the logicians' demonstration is to make the clear known by the unclear. This contradicts their own logical conditions. But despite this, Ibn Taymiyya admits that hidden premises may benefit some people as they benefit in debate, and he affirms that clarity and obscurity are among the matters

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's epistemology and his critique of logical demonstration (burhan). The author first characterizes Ibn Taymiyya as a nominalist and empiricist: he only accepts particulars as real, and dismisses metaphysical universals as myths. Ibn Taymiyya accuses logicians of speaking about universals without being able to provide examples, and he quotes them as admitting that they talk about things with no external reality. Ibn Taymiyya then makes a key epistemological point: inferring from universals to individuals is inferring from the hidden (kafi) to the clear (jali), because individuals of universals are more manifest and known through analogical reasoning (qiyas al-tamthil). He accepts that demonstration yields a universal proposition, but only if from self-evident premises (badihiyyat) whose contradictories are impossible. Other premises (natural, medical, grammatical) are contingent and can be refuted, so they are not material for demonstration but for dialectic or rhetoric. He criticizes logicians for forgetting that most human knowledge comes from experience, and that the universals they use in demonstration are often more obscure than the particulars they aim to prove. He further points out an inconsistency: logicians reject using the clear to define the unclear (in definition), but in demonstration they do precisely that – using hidden universals to prove clear particulars. This contradicts their own principles. Finally, he concedes that hidden premises may have some use in debate. The passage is a sustained critique of the logicians' over-reliance on universal demonstrations.

قضية كلية
قياس التمثيل

C) Because universals cannot be known at all.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique forces us to reconsider the epistemological status of universal premises and whether demonstration can truly deliver certain knowledge of particulars.

Prophetic Inference: Ayat and Qiyas al-Awla
Ibn Taymiyya distinguishes two prophetic modes of inference and critiques Aristotelian syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two prophetic methods of inference (ayat and qiyas al-awla) and explain why Ibn Taymiyya argues that Aristotelian syllogism is unsuitable for reasoning about God.

Core Idea
God cannot be placed under a universal; so the prophets used signs and priority analogy.

Ibn Taymiyya argues that the Quran contains all forms of rational inference, but the prophets specifically used two methods: reasoning by signs (ayat) and priority analogy (qiyas al-awla). They avoided Aristotelian syllogism because it requires subsuming God under a universal category, which is impossible, and representative analogy (tamthil) because God has no equal. In this lesson, we will unpack these two prophetic methods and the critique underlying them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of inference mentioned
In the Arabic, find the three types: ayat, qiyas al-awla, and the rejected qiyas shumul (Aristotelian) and qiyas tamthil. Notice that only the first two are accepted.
2
Understand the reason for rejecting Aristotelian syllogism
Ibn Taymiyya says that God cannot be gathered under a universal with other beings. A syllogism requires a universal term that covers both subject and predicate; this is impossible for God.
3
Understand the priority analogy (qiyas al-awla)
If a creature has a perfection without deficiency, God deserves it even more. If a creature lacks an imperfection, God is even more free from it. This preserves God's transcendence.
This is a form of a fortiori argument.
4
Distinguish the sign (aya) from syllogism
A sign directly entails its specific signification, without a universal middle term. The passage gives examples: sunrise entails daytime; miracles entail prophethood.

حقاً لقد شمل القرآن جميع صور الإستدلالات العقلية، ولكن كانت طريقة الأنبياء الإستدلال على الله بذكر « آياته » وإن استخدموا القياس استخدموا قياس الأولى ولم يستخدموا قياس شمول يستوي أفراده أي القياس المنطقي، لأن الله لا يجتمع هو وغيره تحت كلي يشمله وغيره من الأفراد، أي تحت كلي يستوي أفراده، كما أن الأنبياء لم يستخدموا قياس مثل محض، أي القياس التمثيلي لأن الله تعالى لا مثيل له، فلا يقاس بغيره أو يقاس غيره به. فاستخدم الأنبياء - قياس الأولى - أي ما يثبت لغيره من كمال لا نقص فيه، فثبوته لله بطريق الأولى، وما يتنزه غيره عنه من نقائص وآفات، فتنزه الله عنه بطريق الأولى. وسنشرح الآن هذين الطريقين - طبقاً لما لدينا من كتب ابن تيمية. الطريق الأول: الآيات. يرى ابن تيمية أن طريق الاستدلال بالآيات طريق قرآني، يستمد صورته ومادته من القرآن. ويفرق بينه وبين القياس بأن الآية هي العلامة، وهي الدليل الذي يستلزم عين المدلول، ولا يكون مدلوله أمراً كلياً مشتركاً بين المطلوب وغيره. بل نفس العلم به يوجب العلم بعين المدلول. فهو العلم باستدلال جزئي على جزئي ملازم له، بحيث يلزم من وجود أحدهما وجود الآخر، ومن عدمه عدمه. طلوع الشمس آية على وجود النهار ﴿ وجعلنا الليل والنهار آيتين، فمحونا آية الليل وجعلنا آية النهار مبصرة ﴾ فنفس العلم بطلوع الشمس، يوجب العلم بوجود النهار. ومعجزات محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم آية على نبوته. فنفس العلم بثبوت تلك المعجزات، يوجب العلم بثبوت نبوة محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم. والعلم بثبوت نبوة محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم لا يوجب أمراً كلياً مشتركاً بينه وبين غيره، لأن هذه المعجزات خاصة به وحده. وكذلك آيات الله، نفس العلم بها، يوجب العلم بذاته - وهذا العلم لا يوجب علماً مشتركاً بين الله وغيره. ويرى ابن تيمية أن العلم يكون هذا مستلزماً لهذا، هو جهة الدليل، ولا بد أن يكون كل دليل في الوجود مستلزماً للمدلول. والآية - وهي العلم باستلزام المعين للمعين المطلوب - أدق وأقرب إلى الفطرة من القياس المنطقي الذي ينتقل فيه العقل من حكم كلي عام إلى أحكام جزئية. ولا بد للفكر أن يستخدم لتحقيق هذه القضية الكلية تحقيق أفرادها المعينة أي الجزئية، وأن يشمل كل فرد من هذه الأفراد ما يشمل الحكم الكلي. يفعل هذا المناطقة لتحقيق صحة القضية الكلية، ثم يستخدم بعد ذلك في القياس. ويرى ابن تيمية أن هذا طريق معقد متكلف بعيد عن الفطرة، إن طريق الفكر الحقيقي عند ابن تيمية - كما هو عند المتكلمين - هو استلزام الدليل للمدلول، هو استلزام معين لمعين. فيلزم من وجود الخاص، وجود الخاص كما يلزم من وجود هذا الإنسان، وجود هذا الإنسان الآخر، ومن وجود هذا الإنسان وجود إنسان ثالث. أي يستدل بالمعين على المعين ولكن إذا كان هناك علم كلي، فانه ينشأ من العلم الجزئي، فيلزم من وجود الخاص، وجود العام كما يلزم من وجود هذا الإنسان، وجود الإنسان. ومن وجود هذا الإنسان، وجود الإنسانية والحيوانية. ولكن ينبغي أن نلاحظ أن المطلق لا يوجد بما هو مطلق إلا في الأذهان، ونفس هذا المطلق لا يوصل إلى معرفة أصلاً « فإذا علم الإنسان
Truly, the Quran has encompassed all forms of rational inferences. But the way of the prophets in inferring about God is by mentioning His 'signs' (ayat). And if they used analogical reasoning, they used the analogy of the superior (qiyas al-awla) and did not use inclusive analogy whose individuals are equal, i.e., logical syllogism, because God and other than Him cannot be gathered under a universal covering Him and other individuals, i.e., under a universal whose individuals are equal. Also, the prophets did not use an analogy of pure likeness, i.e., representative analogy, because God Almighty has no likeness, so He is not measured by others nor others measured by Him. So the prophets used the analogy of the superior – that is, whatever perfection without deficiency is established for others, its establishment for God is by priority. And whatever imperfections and defects others are free from, God is free from them by priority. And we will now explain these two ways – according to what we have from Ibn Taymiyya's books. The first way: the signs (ayat). Ibn Taymiyya holds that the method of inference by signs is a Quranic method, deriving its form and matter from the Quran. He distinguishes it from analogy in that the sign is the indicator, and it is the evidence that entails the very thing signified, and its signification is not a universal matter shared between the sought and others. Rather, knowledge of it itself necessitates knowledge of the very thing signified. So it is knowledge of a particular inference upon a particular concomitant to it, such that from the existence of one follows the existence of the other, and from its absence follows its absence. The rising of the sun is a sign of the existence of daytime: 'And We have made the night and day two signs, and We erased the sign of the night and made the sign of the day visible.' So knowledge of the rising of the sun itself necessitates knowledge of the existence of daytime. And the miracles of Muhammad, peace be upon him, are signs of his prophethood. So knowledge of the establishment of those miracles necessitates knowledge of the establishment of the prophethood of Muhammad. And knowledge of the establishment of the prophethood of Muhammad does not necessitate a universal matter shared between him and others, because these miracles are specific to him alone. Likewise, God's signs: knowledge of them themselves necessitates knowledge of His essence – and this knowledge does not necessitate knowledge shared between God and others. Ibn Taymiyya holds that knowledge that one thing entails another is the aspect of the evidence, and it is necessary that every evidence in existence entail its signification. And the sign – which is knowledge of the entailment of the specific for the specific sought – is more precise and closer to the innate disposition (fitra) than logical syllogism, in which the mind moves from a general universal judgment to particular judgments. For the mind must, in order to verify this universal proposition, verify its particular individuals, and that every one of these individuals is included in what the universal judgment includes. The logicians do this to verify the correctness of the universal proposition, then use it afterwards in the syllogism. Ibn Taymiyya holds that this is a complex, artificial method far from the fitra. Indeed, the path of true thought according to Ibn Taymiyya – as it is with the theologians – is the entailment of the evidence for the signification, i.e., the entailment of a specific for a specific. So from the existence of the particular follows the existence of the particular, just as from the existence of this human, the existence of that other human follows, and from the existence of this human, the existence of a third human follows. That is, one infers from the specific to the specific. But if there is universal knowledge, it arises from particular knowledge; so from the existence of the particular follows the existence of the universal, just as from the existence of this human, the existence of human follows, and from the existence of this human, the existence of humanity and animality follows. But it should be noted that the absolute does not exist as absolute except in minds, and this absolute itself does not lead to knowledge at all. 'For when a human knows...'
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is an exposition and defense of Ibn Taymiyya's critique of Aristotelian logic and his alternative model of inference based on Quranic signs (ayat). The author begins by affirming that the Quran encompasses all forms of rational inference, but that the prophetic method is distinctive. The prophets use two types: reasoning by signs (ayat) and the analogy of the superior (qiyas al-awla). They avoid both the Aristotelian categorical syllogism (qiyas shumul) and the analogy of pure likeness (qiyas tamthil). The reason is that God cannot be subsumed under a universal genus along with other beings, and He has no equal to be compared to. The analogy of the superior argues from a perfection found in creation to God's perfection by priority, and from defects absent in creation to God's transcendence by priority. The author then explains the first way—ayat—in detail. A sign (aya) is a specific indicator that entails a specific signification, not a universal rule. For example, sunrise entails daytime, and a miracle entails the prophethood of Muhammad. Knowledge of the sign directly yields knowledge of the signification without passing through a universal. Ibn Taymiyya distinguishes this from syllogism, which requires establishing a universal proposition by checking all individuals—a complicated and unnatural process. True reasoning, he argues, is the direct entailment of particular from particular. Universal knowledge, if it arises, does so from particular knowledge, but the absolute universal has no mind-independent existence. The passage ends with an incomplete sentence about human knowledge. The overall thrust is epistemological: Ibn Taymiyya champions a direct, intuitive, and Quranic mode of reasoning against what he sees as the artificial and indirect method of the logicians.

The Logical Structure of the Sign (Aya)
Ibn Taymiyya explains the inference by signs (ayat) as direct entailment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the concept of istilzam (entailment) and how a sign (aya) provides direct knowledge of a particular signification without needing a universal premise.

Core Idea
A sign is a specific evidence that directly entails its specific signification – no universal needed.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya elaborates on the first prophetic method: inference by signs (ayat). He defines a sign as an indicator that necessarily entails its specific signification (istilzam). For example, the rising of the sun directly indicates daytime, and a miracle directly indicates the prophethood of Muhammad. This lesson will dissect the logical structure of this entailment and contrast it with the syllogistic method that requires a universal judgment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of a sign (aya) in the passage
Find where Ibn Taymiyya says 'الآية هي العلامة، وهي الدليل الذي يستلزم عين المدلول' (the sign is the indicator, the evidence that entails the very thing signified).
2
Understand the requirement of entailment (istilzam)
The passage says: 'يلزم من وجود أحدهما وجود الآخر، ومن عدمه عدمه' (from the existence of one follows the existence of the other, and from its absence follows its absence). This is a biconditional entailment.
3
Analyze the example of the sun and daytime
Knowledge of sunrise directly gives knowledge of daytime. Why is this not a syllogism? Because there is no universal premise connecting 'sunrise' to 'daytime' in a syllogistic form; it is direct.
4
Analyze the example of miracles and prophethood
The miracle is specific to Muhammad, so knowledge of it entails his prophethood directly, without involving a universal like 'every miracle-worker is a prophet'.
5
Contrast with syllogism
In syllogism, the mind must first verify a universal proposition by checking all individuals. This is a complex, indirect method. Ibn Taymiyya calls it 'complex, artificial method far from the fitra'.

قياس الأولى - أي ما يثبت لغيره من كمال لا نقص فيه، فثبوته لله بطريق الأولى، وما يتنزه غيره عنه من نقائص وآفات، فتنزه الله عنه بطريق الأولى. وسنشرح الآن هذين الطريقين - طبقاً لما لدينا من كتب ابن تيمية. الطريق الأول: الآيات. يرى ابن تيمية أن طريق الاستدلال بالآيات طريق قرآني، يستمد صورته ومادته من القرآن. ويفرق بينه وبين القياس بأن الآية هي العلامة، وهي الدليل الذي يستلزم عين المدلول، ولا يكون مدلوله أمراً كلياً مشتركاً بين المطلوب وغيره. بل نفس العلم به يوجب العلم بعين المدلول. فهو العلم باستدلال جزئي على جزئي ملازم له، بحيث يلزم من وجود أحدهما وجود الآخر، ومن عدمه عدمه. طلوع الشمس آية على وجود النهار ﴿ وجعلنا الليل والنهار آيتين، فمحونا آية الليل وجعلنا آية النهار مبصرة ﴾ فنفس العلم بطلوع الشمس، يوجب العلم بوجود النهار. ومعجزات محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم آية على نبوته. فنفس العلم بثبوت تلك المعجزات، يوجب العلم بثبوت نبوة محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم. والعلم بثبوت نبوة محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم لا يوجب أمراً كلياً مشتركاً بينه وبين غيره، لأن هذه المعجزات خاصة به وحده. وكذلك آيات الله، نفس العلم بها، يوجب العلم بذاته - وهذا العلم لا يوجب علماً مشتركاً بين الله وغيره. ويرى ابن تيمية أن العلم يكون هذا مستلزماً لهذا، هو جهة الدليل، ولا بد أن يكون كل دليل في الوجود مستلزماً للمدلول. والآية - وهي العلم باستلزام المعين للمعين المطلوب - أدق وأقرب إلى الفطرة من القياس المنطقي الذي ينتقل فيه العقل من حكم كلي عام إلى أحكام جزئية. ولا بد للفكر أن يستخدم لتحقيق هذه القضية الكلية تحقيق أفرادها المعينة أي الجزئية، وأن يشمل كل فرد من هذه الأفراد ما يشمل الحكم الكلي. يفعل هذا المناطقة لتحقيق صحة القضية الكلية، ثم يستخدم بعد ذلك في القياس. ويرى ابن تيمية أن هذا طريق معقد متكلف بعيد عن الفطرة، إن طريق الفكر الحقيقي عند ابن تيمية - كما هو عند المتكلمين - هو استلزام الدليل للمدلول، هو استلزام معين لمعين. فيلزم من وجود الخاص، وجود الخاص كما يلزم من وجود هذا الإنسان، وجود هذا الإنسان الآخر، ومن وجود هذا الإنسان وجود إنسان ثالث. أي يستدل بالمعين على المعين ولكن إذا كان هناك علم كلي، فانه ينشأ من العلم الجزئي، فيلزم من وجود الخاص، وجود العام كما يلزم من وجود هذا الإنسان، وجود الإنسان. ومن وجود هذا الإنسان، وجود الإنسانية والحيوانية. ولكن ينبغي أن نلاحظ أن المطلق لا يوجد بما هو مطلق إلا في الأذهان، ونفس هذا المطلق لا يوصل إلى معرفة أصلاً « فإذا علم الإنسان
Truly, the Quran has encompassed all forms of rational inferences. But the way of the prophets in inferring about God is by mentioning His 'signs' (ayat). And if they used analogical reasoning, they used the analogy of the superior (qiyas al-awla) and did not use inclusive analogy whose individuals are equal, i.e., logical syllogism, because God and other than Him cannot be gathered under a universal covering Him and other individuals, i.e., under a universal whose individuals are equal. Also, the prophets did not use an analogy of pure likeness, i.e., representative analogy, because God Almighty has no likeness, so He is not measured by others nor others measured by Him. So the prophets used the analogy of the superior – that is, whatever perfection without deficiency is established for others, its establishment for God is by priority. And whatever imperfections and defects others are free from, God is free from them by priority. And we will now explain these two ways – according to what we have from Ibn Taymiyya's books. The first way: the signs (ayat). Ibn Taymiyya holds that the method of inference by signs is a Quranic method, deriving its form and matter from the Quran. He distinguishes it from analogy in that the sign is the indicator, and it is the evidence that entails the very thing signified, and its signification is not a universal matter shared between the sought and others. Rather, knowledge of it itself necessitates knowledge of the very thing signified. So it is knowledge of a particular inference upon a particular concomitant to it, such that from the existence of one follows the existence of the other, and from its absence follows its absence. The rising of the sun is a sign of the existence of daytime: 'And We have made the night and day two signs, and We erased the sign of the night and made the sign of the day visible.' So knowledge of the rising of the sun itself necessitates knowledge of the existence of daytime. And the miracles of Muhammad, peace be upon him, are signs of his prophethood. So knowledge of the establishment of those miracles necessitates knowledge of the establishment of the prophethood of Muhammad. And knowledge of the establishment of the prophethood of Muhammad does not necessitate a universal matter shared between him and others, because these miracles are specific to him alone. Likewise, God's signs: knowledge of them themselves necessitates knowledge of His essence – and this knowledge does not necessitate knowledge shared between God and others. Ibn Taymiyya holds that knowledge that one thing entails another is the aspect of the evidence, and it is necessary that every evidence in existence entail its signification. And the sign – which is knowledge of the entailment of the specific for the specific sought – is more precise and closer to the innate disposition (fitra) than logical syllogism, in which the mind moves from a general universal judgment to particular judgments. For the mind must, in order to verify this universal proposition, verify its particular individuals, and that every one of these individuals is included in what the universal judgment includes. The logicians do this to verify the correctness of the universal proposition, then use it afterwards in the syllogism. Ibn Taymiyya holds that this is a complex, artificial method far from the fitra. Indeed, the path of true thought according to Ibn Taymiyya – as it is with the theologians – is the entailment of the evidence for the signification, i.e., the entailment of a specific for a specific. So from the existence of the particular follows the existence of the particular, just as from the existence of this human, the existence of that other human follows, and from the existence of this human, the existence of a third human follows. That is, one infers from the specific to the specific. But if there is universal knowledge, it arises from particular knowledge; so from the existence of the particular follows the existence of the universal, just as from the existence of this human, the existence of human follows, and from the existence of this human, the existence of humanity and animality follows. But it should be noted that the absolute does not exist as absolute except in minds, and this absolute itself does not lead to knowledge at all. 'For when a human knows...'
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is an exposition and defense of Ibn Taymiyya's critique of Aristotelian logic and his alternative model of inference based on Quranic signs (ayat). The author begins by affirming that the Quran encompasses all forms of rational inference, but that the prophetic method is distinctive. The prophets use two types: reasoning by signs (ayat) and the analogy of the superior (qiyas al-awla). They avoid both the Aristotelian categorical syllogism (qiyas shumul) and the analogy of pure likeness (qiyas tamthil). The reason is that God cannot be subsumed under a universal genus along with other beings, and He has no equal to be compared to. The analogy of the superior argues from a perfection found in creation to God's perfection by priority, and from defects absent in creation to God's transcendence by priority. The author then explains the first way—ayat—in detail. A sign (aya) is a specific indicator that entails a specific signification, not a universal rule. For example, sunrise entails daytime, and a miracle entails the prophethood of Muhammad. Knowledge of the sign directly yields knowledge of the signification without passing through a universal. Ibn Taymiyya distinguishes this from syllogism, which requires establishing a universal proposition by checking all individuals—a complicated and unnatural process. True reasoning, he argues, is the direct entailment of particular from particular. Universal knowledge, if it arises, does so from particular knowledge, but the absolute universal has no mind-independent existence. The passage ends with an incomplete sentence about human knowledge. The overall thrust is epistemological: Ibn Taymiyya champions a direct, intuitive, and Quranic mode of reasoning against what he sees as the artificial and indirect method of the logicians.

intermediate
15 min
Ibn Qayyim's Critique of Aristotelian Logic and the Priority Syllogism
Ibn Qayyim's transmission of Ibn Taymiyya's critique and his own theological alternative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Qayyim's grounds for rejecting Aristotelian logic and explain why he considers qiyas al-awla the only valid method for knowing God.

Core Idea
Ibn Qayyim rejects Aristotelian logic as corrupting sciences and affirms qiyas al-awla as the sole valid path to God.

Ibn Qayyim, building on Ibn Taymiyya's critique, argues that Aristotelian logic is not only unnecessary but harmful to Islamic sciences. He demonstrates that the great Imams never used it. Most importantly, he distinguishes between invalid types of syllogisms (tamthil and shuhud) and the valid priority syllogism (qiyas al-awla) that leads to knowledge of God's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of critique
Find in the passage which logical system Ibn Qayyim is attacking. Note that he includes both the principles and the claim that learning it is obligatory.
2
Examine the historical support
Observe how Ibn Qayyim lists earlier critics (al-Sirafi, al-Baqillani, etc.) to show a tradition of refutation.
3
Analyze the epistemic objection
Understand why Ibn Qayyim says logic corrupts sciences: it makes false distinctions and unifications, contradicting clear reason.
4
Assess the argument against obligation
Read his statement about al-Shafi'i and Ahmad: they were sound without logic, so it cannot be obligatory.
5
Distinguish the three syllogism types
Compare qiyas tamthil, shuhud, and qiyas al-awla. Determine which are impossible regarding God and why.

فيه ، وعرف فساده ، وتداعى أصوله ، فيقرر أنه لن يؤمن بهذا إلا من عرفه ، وعرف فساده وتناقضه ومناقضه كثير منه للعقل الصريح ، ويقول إن بعضاً ممن كان قد قرأه وعني به ، أخبر أنه لم يزل متعجباً من فساد أصوله وقواعده ومعانيه لصريح المعقول ، ومن الواضح ان ابن القيم ينقل فكرة مناقضة المنطق الأرسططاليسي لصريح المعقول عن ابن تيمية ، ثم يذكر أن المنطق الارسططاليسي يتضمن دعاوى محضة غير مدلول عليها ، ومن أمثلة هذه الدعاوى « تفريقه بين متساويين ، وجمعه بين مختلفين فيحكم على الشيء بحكم ، وعلى نظيره بضد ذلك الحكم أو يحكم على الشيء بحكم ، ثم يحكم على مضاده ومناقضه به وأثر ابن تيمية ظاهر في كل هذه النصوص . ويقرر ابن القيم أنه وقف على رد متكلمي الإسلام عليه وتبيين فساده وتناقضه فوقف على مصنف لأبي سعيد السيرافي النحوي في ذلك ، وعلى رد كثير من المتكلمين وعلماء العربية عليه كالقاضي أبي بكر بن الطيب والقاضي عبد الجبار والجبائي وابنه وأبي المعالي وأبي القاسم الأنصاري وغيرهم ورأى استشكالات فضلائهم ورؤسائهم لمواضع الأشكال ومخالفاتها ، ما كان ينقدح له كثير منه . ثم يذكر كتابي ابن تيمية الكبير والصغير بالإعجاب الشديد(۱) . وينكر ابن القيم على الذين يقولون إن تعلم هذا العلم فرض كفاية أو فرض عين ، فإن الأئمة ما اشتغلوا به . فيقول « وما كان من هوى النفوس بهذه المنزلة فهو بأن يكون جهلاً أولى منه بأن يكون علماً تعلمه فرض كفاية أو فرض عين وهذا الشافعي وأحمد وسائر أئمة الإسلام وتصانيفهم لمن نظر فيها : هل راعوا فيها حدود المنطق وأوضاعه ؟ وهل صح علمهم بدونه بل كانوا هم أجل وأعظم عقولاً من أن يشغلوا أفكارهم بهذيان المنطقيين ، وما دخل المنطق على علم إلا أفسده وغير أوضاعه وشوش قواعده »(۲) . ثم يذكر أن أنواع الإستدلالات المنطقية الارسططاليسية لن توصل إلى معرفة الله . وأن الطريق الوحيد لمعرفة الله ووجوده هو قياس الأولى « الرب تعالى لا يدخل مع خلقه في قياس تمثيل ولا قياس شهود يستوي أفراده ، فهذان الفرعان من القياس يستحيل ثبوتها في حقه . وأما قياس الأولى فهو غير مستحيل في حقه . بل هو واجب له وهو مستعمل في حقه عقلاً ونقلاً(۱) »
In it, and he knew its corruption, and its foundations collapsed, so he affirms that no one will believe in this except one who knows it, and knows its corruption and contradiction, and much of it contradicts clear reason. He says that some who had read it and attended to it informed him that they never ceased to be amazed at the corruption of its principles, rules, and meanings in light of clear reason. It is evident that Ibn Qayyim transmits the idea of the contradiction of Aristotelian logic to clear reason from Ibn Taymiyya. Then he mentions that Aristotelian logic includes pure claims without evidence. Examples of these claims: 'making a distinction between two equals, and joining two different things, so that it judges a thing with a ruling and its counterpart with the opposite ruling, or judges a thing with a ruling and then judges its opposite and contradictory with it.' The influence of Ibn Taymiyya is apparent in all these texts. Ibn Qayyim affirms that he came across the refutation of it by the Muslim theologians and the demonstration of its corruption and contradiction. He came across a work by Abu Sa'id al-Sirafi the grammarian on that, and the refutations of many theologians and Arabic language scholars against it, such as al-Qadi Abu Bakr ibn al-Tayyib, al-Qadi Abd al-Jabbar, al-Jubba'i and his son, Abu al-Ma'ali, Abu al-Qasim al-Ansari, and others. He saw the objections of their most virtuous and their leaders to points of difficulty and their violations, and many of these occurred to him. Then he mentions the two books of Ibn Taymiyya, the large and the small, with great admiration. Ibn Qayyim rejects those who say that learning this science is a communal or individual obligation, for the imams did not occupy themselves with it. He says, 'What is of such a lowly nature to souls is more deserving to be ignorance than to be knowledge whose learning is a communal or individual obligation. This is ash-Shafi'i and Ahmad and all the imams of Islam—their works, for one who looks into them: Did they observe the limits and rules of logic? Were their sciences sound without it? Rather, they were too great and of too great minds to busy their thoughts with the nonsense of the logicians. Logic never entered a science except it corrupted it, changed its rules, and disturbed its principles.' Then he mentions that the types of Aristotelian logical proofs will not lead to knowledge of God. And that the only path to knowledge of God and His existence is the priority syllogism (qiyas al-awla): 'The Lord, exalted, does not enter into a syllogism of analogy (tamthil) with His creation, nor into a syllogism of observation (shuhud) whose individuals are equal. These two branches of syllogism are impossible to establish regarding Him. As for the priority syllogism, it is not impossible regarding Him. Rather, it is necessary for Him, and it is used regarding Him by reason and revelation.'
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage compiles Ibn Qayyim's transmission of Ibn Taymiyya's critique of Aristotelian logic, followed by his own denial of its obligatory status and its inadequacy for knowing God. The author begins by stating that Ibn Qayyim relates that knowing the corruption of logic prevents belief in it, because it contradicts clear reason. He gives examples: logic makes false distinctions and unifications. Then Ibn Qayyim cites earlier critics—al-Sirafi, al-Baqillani, al-Qadi Abd al-Jabbar, etc.—showing a tradition of refutation. He then admires Ibn Taymiyya's works. Next, Ibn Qayyim attacks the claim that learning logic is an obligation (fard kifaya or fard 'ayn), arguing that the great Imams like al-Shafi'i and Ahmad did not use it and their knowledge was sound without it. He says logic only corrupts sciences. Finally, he asserts that Aristotelian logical proofs cannot lead to knowledge of God. The only valid method is 'qiyas al-awla' (priority reasoning), which is necessary for God, unlike analogical (tamthil) or observational (shuhud) syllogisms, which are impossible regarding Him. The passage is a theological critique, not a technical logical exposition.

قياس تمثيل
قياس شهود

Key Takeaway
Ibn Qayyim's critique of Aristotelian logic centers on its corruption of sciences and its inability to lead to knowledge of God, reserving qiyas al-awla as the only valid method for theological reasoning.
